ii
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This thesis explores multiple take back options for the end of life of heating systems. It
covers three Viessmann products, one gas boiler and two heat pumps in the German
market. The goals of the research is to first, determine the necessary steps to develop a
take back system. Secondly, to identify and quantify the characteristics that influence
the economical viability and the environmental benefits of take back systems. Third, to
develop a co­creation and decision support tool to support the company in their
development of take back systems. After having identified the current situation, where
most heating system are recycled as mixed metal, three solutions were quantified using
Material Flow Analysis, Life Cycle Assessment, and Discrete Event Simulation methods.
The solutions are: 1. The installers recovering a few parts (Incentivised recovery), 2.
Picking up products in bulk at the installers companies and treating them in a specialised
facility (Bulk collection), 3. Picking up products at customers home and treating them in
a facility as well (Kerbside collection). Their common objective is to recover between 1­5
components and send them to their Original Equipment Manufacturers (OEMs) for
remanufacturing. The results show around 8%­11% carbon footprint reduction
compared to the baseline for all three options, and no significant energy consumption
benefits. It was identified that the main costs were logistics expenses. Additionally, the
OEMs interest in paying for the recovered components was determined as essential for
profiting from the system. However, even if it was found that most of the costs were
fixed, it was not possible to conclude whether any of the solution was viable. Though,
the bulk collection solution had the highest profitability potential. The assessment also
showed that the economical and environmental benefits were product dependent, thus
creating the need of a tool for an easy and holistic assessment of each product before
taking them back. In order to guide Viessmann in developing their take back system, a
co­creative poster was proposed, which is a unique comprehensive semi­quantitative
method for assessing the feasibility of taking back products. Finally, it is recommended
to learn disassembling product with targeted efforts as well as collaborating with OEMs
and logistics providers in the design process to arrive at a viable solution.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems

Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:11==
1
Why investigate circularity & take back
in the domestic heating industry
Residential fossil powered heating systems are being replaced in record numbers across
the EU and overall in the world. This is driven by soaring gas prices, legislation to curb
global warming and consumer interest in sustainability (German Heatpump Association,
2023; EHPA, 2023). This positive development is however generating a very large
amount of waste, predominantly of functional, but obsolete, heating systems ­ which
primary end of life treatment currently is shredding as mixed metal. There are circa 90
million oil and gas condensing boilers installed in the EU alone, will largely will be largely
will be replaced by heat pumps in the coming decades.( EHPA, 2023) .
Their replacement is mainly heat pumps, with an array of supportive solutions like: water
storage tanks, solar thermal, solar PV, and batteries ­ all collaborating to meet the
residential heating needs. Due to global warming, changes in architecture and urban
development, the demand for air conditioning (AC) is also increasing in many EU
markets (Birol, 2018; IEA, 2022a). The same tendency can be seen in many markets
globally (IEA, 2022a). As air conditioners and heat pumps use the same underlying
technology, this is driving a huge demand for heat pumping products, components and
the raw materials to manufacture them.
Raw material prices are also rising, and with the Emission Trading System (ETS)
reaching record highs in 2020s (Twidale et al., 2023), there is an increased interest in
lowering products end of life emissions and if possible, use them to reduce
manufacturing emissions. Recent supply chain disruptions also highlight the perks to be
control of a large product waste stream, as it can potentially be used as a source for raw
materials ­ even if it requires significant investment.
The EU is also increasingly interested in circular economy, from both a security and
sustainability point of view as noted by EU goals, directives and regulations like: the
circular economy action plan, Green deal, WEEE, F­Gas directives, Ecodesign
regulations and more. The EU objectives are to increase the material circulation
duration of critical raw materials (CRMs) within the EU. These goals increases the need
to handle discarded products more carefully and work to ensure their components and
materials get handled in ways that either allow for recapturing critical raw materials
and/or mitigate their dissipation (Commission, 2020; DIRECTIVE L 76/3/EC, 2018;
Directive L 197/38/EC, 2012).
Key regulation here is the critical raw materials act, extended producer responsibility
(EPR) and Waste Electrical and Electronic Equipment Directive (WEEE), obligating
product manufactures to pay for their products waste management and ideally
cooperate on ensuring the best possible waste treatment. A lot more regulation is
driving interest as better utilisation of the products materials at EoL, can have positive
effects on raw materials extraction, critical raw material dissipation and create jobs. This
complements companies requirements under CSRD, CSDD and HRDD. Current WEEE
routes fulfil these requirements, but require the products and waste to end up in WEEE
waste streams, be handled properly and for manufactures and numerous producer
responsibility organisations (PROs) to align on waste treatment and cost sharing
throughout the EU (Andersen, 2022). Many producer responsibility organisations may
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
1
==PAGE:12==
also have limited upcycling treatment capabilities due to the wide array of products they
are receiving, diminishing their ability to optimise product treatment. Product/Waste
handling through current waste streams is often sub­optimal, hindering
reuse/re­manufacturing components. There is also often a lack of information sharing
throughout the waste­chain, making smart & targeted efforts difficult ­ but some do exist
(#Miele 2023, #Elertur 2023).
Many component suppliers to climate solution manufactures are also increasingly
requesting or investigating ways of getting their components back. However what
components, in what condition, from which products can differ significantly ­ and so can
their ability to improve their components end of life.
There is therefore large interest in looking at the most circular and sustainable ways of
handling climate solutions end of life. From the regulatory level, to the manufacturers
value chain and all the way to the technicians, scrap dealers, recycling yards and end
users of the products. It is still a design space that is hard to navigate. With lack of
clarity in how to approach it, especially for large product portfolios. When are different
strategies applicable, who to collaborate with, what to strive for and how to quantify it
all? This is why we are investigating how to design take back systems for the residential
heating and climate industry. It aims to shine a light on how to select the most
sustainable ways of getting control of heating systems end of life.
1.1
Viessmanns context
Viessmann is one of the EU markets largest players in the residential heating, and has
been through multiple technology paradigm shifts, from solid fuels, to oil, to gas and now
to renewable solutions.
The Viessmanns old products represent a very significant market share of the gas
condensing boilers installed in EU & Germany, that are to be replaced. The company
also operates in other markets and regions but these are outside the scope of this
project and report ­ however many findings and methods will likely be easily adaptable to
those contexts.
The company’s current product & service portfolio is more diverse than ever before,
including traditional residential heating/climate solutions to now also spanning from
municipality solutions, to residential energy management, photovoltaic and solar thermal
solutions.
The company has a long history of being science based, and has several science based
sustainability goals. Its current climate goal is reducing its’ scope 1 & 2 emissions by
48% and its’ scope 3 emissions by 55% per million euros, all by 2030. Beyond this, its’
goal is to increase its business revenue generated from circular business processes to
30% per million euros by 2030 (#CE Pillars 2023) .
Viessmann is in talks with multiple component suppliers (henceforth OEMs) that are
interested in getting some of their components back. This requires the Viessmann to
have the capability to get a hold of the products and/or its components, in a satisfactory
condition, which currently is not the case. Beyond this, it requires aligning on the goals,
(what are components, conditions, cost sharing principles, etc..) of this new operation,
which currently is a largely uncharted territory. From a practical point of view it also
requires the component suppliers to be able to significantly improve the products end of
life, as ’procuring’ the old components can be a costly and resource demanding
operation.
2
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:13==
1.2
Our prior project on the domestic heating system
transition
The authors have previously worked with Viessmann, in a group of 6 students, in the
course 42090, Holistic Design of Engineering Systems, Spring 2022. Here the focus
was directly on finding potential in the transition to heat pumps ­ focusing on the gas/oil
boilers.
The main findings were that separate take back systems might make the most sense for
the company’s or industry’s products. Preliminary calculations showed that this could be
economically viable if developed further. The project was however missing an in depth
investigation of the different take back system solutions and quantification of the
sustainability & economics of the system. The past project also did not investigate the
role of existing waste management and regulations, nor considered material circularity.
1.3
Our initial scope
The scope and focus of the project has developed at milestones in the project and as the
findings from the analysis emerged. The focus has been split in two analysis categories
with the same overarching design goal: to design and suggest a take back system
design. The two main analysis’ have been to estimate the cost and environmental
impacts of different designs. To simplify and limit the scope, the initial focus was put on
the main environmental hotspot of the heating systems, by total emissions: steel.
The designing of the logistics of getting hold of old products was centred around the
structural and functional choices ­ not optimisation of a specific network design. This was
done as Viesmann has not selected specific logistics provider, and still needs to quantify
the potential in taking back the old heating systems ­ and the main factors influencing it.
Whilst the project does touch upon circular product design, the main focus has been on
the quantification of sustainability of different take back system designs, and findings
ways to manage them.
In the project brief the goals have been split in 3 categories, between the authors and
the unifying goals:
Aksel’s initial goals
1. Mapping different boilers distributions in German regions.
2. Quantifying TEA/TBL of different logistics setups for the take back system.
3. Quantifying the TBL for the current end of lives for boilers.
4. Quantifying TEA/TBL for different boiler treatment options.
5. Develop a decision support tool based on those analyses.
Antoine’s initial goals
1. Mapping current heat pump recycling route system and stakeholders
2. Quantifying the impact of recycling, remanufacturing and reusing
materials/components in new products
3. Quantifying the impact of system design for circularity decisions scenarios.
4. Comparing the benefits of circular product design with the net­zero target
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
3
==PAGE:14==
5. Propose a system design concept to improve the benefits of using recycled
material.
Synergising goals
1. Synergise the take­back system and heat pump system design results.
2. Develop a flexible system design accompanied of decision making
recommendations for different scenarios.
1.4
Overview of the task
The focus ended up on exploring a few main take back system configurations,
quantifying their cost & environmental differences. This was then used to create a tool to
support managing a large portfolio of products. Due to the high value of other materials
than steel, especially copper and aluminium, it would drastically limit the economical
feasibility and representativeness of the study, if the scope was limited to steel. There
would also be unnecessary constraints on economical and environmental benefits
options. Thus, the scope for materials and components was expanded to including all
hotspot materials (excluding refrigerant) and several key components in the heating
systems. This was done as a byproduct of the data modelling, and the realisation that
the other hotspot materials would be essential to accurately model the value of the
heating systems at end of life.
Research questions
To guide this project a set of research questions were
developed, which will be attempted to answered in the project.
1. What are the necessary steps when designing take back systems for different
heating systems?
2. How does heating systems characteristics influence the design, planning and
operation of take back systems?
3. What decision making tools can be used to plan efforts of taking back large
product portfolio?
1.5
Our design approach and methodology
As millions of heating systems are to be replaced, mostly due to fuel economy and
environmental concerns ­ before they are obsolete, it is a good case to salvage some
products and components. However the waste stream is atypical, there are many
players and an opaque waste handling as of today. With interest from both Viessmann,
its’ suppliers and competitors in enhancing sustainability & circularity, there is a demand
to find solutions enabling systemic change ­ so society does not have the same issue
when the heat pumps are replaced.
The problem and solution space is inherently multidisciplinary, spanning from the end
customer experience, to installer companies work practices, waste treatment and
quantifying the costs and gains from changing the system.
There exists multiple types of design problems. It is worth evaluating what type of
problem it is, to gain insight into the types of solutions and long term perspectives for
proposed solutions. As argued in Frame Innovation, modern problems, often called
wicked are open, complex, dynamic and networked (Dorst, 2015). All of which is true for
this thesis problem. It is open, as there are multiple brands with different heating
systems, installers are free to select what waste disposal they see fit. It is complex as
4
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:15==
there are many different waste management actors, and practices for handling the
waste. The problem is dynamic, as the products and waste management practices,
legislation and capabilities are changing and evolving. It is networked as different
organisations (competitors, OEMs, EPR, PROs, legislative bodies) all have a vested
interest in handling this problem.
Another more stringent test of the problems wickedness is proposed by Pyykkö et al.,
2021, whose article focuses on sustainability transitions in supply chains from a wicked
problems angle.
Figure 1.1: From Pyykkö et al., 2021, which adapted 3 papers definitions of wicked prob­
lems to evaluate whether ones problem is wicked.
Going through the list briefly:
1. It is hard to definitively define the problem in our case, especially due to the
obsolescence of gas heating systems. But it could be broadly said to be exploring
the possibility of giving organisations circular capabilities for end of life residential
heating systems. But this is not a definitive formulation nor the only way to look at
the problem.
2. There is not necessarily a final solution, due to the evolving waste management
landscape. If established waste management actors, add capabilities to allow for
component re­circulation, separate take back systems might need to change. The
change in types of products being discarded also drives a need to adapt the take
back system. Therefore, there is no final solution.
3. The project is about improving the EoL management of the products. It is
inherently a trade off between cost, work required and realised benefits ­ and how
else these resources could be used. There is no perfect/true­or­false solution.
Some ’true­or­false’ solutions that are often preferred within sustainability, such as
reuse, may have rebound effects, making other solutions preferable.
4. The discarded heating systems and context is constantly evolving, changing the
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
5
==PAGE:16==
baseline, changing the assessment.
5. A take back system could significantly impact both installers, scrap dealers and
OEMs operations. This could reduce their willingness to comply with
experimentation.
6. As covered in the literature review and gaps. This type of value chain and type of
product is somewhat understudied. Also the lack of voluntary take back systems of
similar product, makes it hard to find exhaustively described solutions. However
most solution typologies have been described in research, even if their details are
more centred around smaller products discarded by end users themselves.
7. This is true for take back systems, at least within product categories and most
regions. Each product has unique characteristics and potential at EoU and at EoL.
8. Arguably, as will be described in more depth in the situation analysis, this problem
would be significantly easier to solve, if current waste legislation was followed and
the established waste management routes that are evolving were utilised. Also
regulatory limits, significantly constrains the solution space. The problem is
therefore partly a result and symptom of other problems. The main one being
waste managements focus on the most economical disposal, not the most
sustainable one ­ and the general systemic discrepancy between those two in the
economy.
9. As demonstrated in 8. one can frame this problem in many ways, giving clear
solution spaces which clearly omit entirely different types of solutions.
10. As take back systems are voluntary, it is up to actors in the waste chain to
participate. But there are clear consequences for how many can participate
depending on the solution chosen. Naively selecting a narrow solution, will have
clear, quantifiable environmental and economical consequences.
In summation, as the problem satisfies all 10 requirements, it can be considered a
wicked problem.
1.5.1
Participatory research
Due to our external position with Viessmann and time constraints, it is clear that we don’t
have access to all the resources that would be needed to have a complete analysis of
the situation and potential solutions. The approach we took to compensate that is to
create the conditions for a participatory research with Viessmann. The goal is to give all
the insights we have from the analysis and create the tools to guide them to improve and
reuse our learnings in an effective way. It is also directed towards co­creation of the
research and decision making tools. In this sense we propose a way to involve partners
in the discussion to create the best possible take back solution.
As students and Viessmann externals, we are of course biased in some ways. Given
our will to do what is right for the environment, it is possible that we have involuntarily
neglected some solutions that would have shown more profitability potential but less
environmental benefits. Our interest in using certain already known quantitative tools
might have made us consider better option against more suited assessment tools.
Moreover, the thesis requirements influences the research aspects of the thesis to a
point were we aim to have a novel approach and participate to the research discussion
on the subject. We also tend to arrive at a succeeding result where our research
questions are positively answered, which might bring biased judgement, in aspects that
would otherwise invalidate our hypothesis. This plays a role in trying to fulfil Viessmann
6
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:17==
expectations about the project. Therefore, our frame of thinking is pulled by multiple
aspects that can influence the final results of the thesis.
1.5.2
System oriented problem solving
Due to the nature of problem, being wicked and therefore complex. We elect to adopt a
system oriented problem solving approach. The overarching framework we chose has
been the N­model (Oehmen, 2022). This was chosen for several reasons: 1. Prior
experience and familiarity with the framework. 2. Simplicity and clear direction, affording
good ergonomics for us as designers. 3. Easy to explain findings to those who are
unfamiliar with it.
Figure 1.2: The framework is as of now, unpublished, but was introduced by Oehmen,
2022 and has been tested by dozens of groups projects over many runs of the course
Holistic Design.
Model based systems engineering
The problem solving approach has also been characterised by constructing qualitative
and quantitative models to explore the impact and viability of solutions. Giving the
project ties to model­based systems engineering (MBSE). MBSE is an approach that
uses models to make the wicked problem more tangible and create shared lenses
through which to view the problem and solution domain. MBSE is extensively used
when large an complex systems needs to be designed and implemented, and has
gained increasing popularity with the lower barrier to construct digital models of complex
systems. Shevchenko, 2020
The choice of MBSE also fits with Viessmanns interest in getting numbers on the triple
bottom line of different take back systems performance. Besides using quantitative
models, there has also been a focus on creating useful qualitative models and boundary
objects. As these boundary objects to provide overviews and support knowledge
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
7
==PAGE:18==
sharing about the intricacies of implementing take back systems for their products. The
problem and solution space is inherently multidisciplinary and therefore benefits of
models and tools to facilitate a common understanding, design, and management of it.
Our analysis approach
Figure 1.3 describes the analysis approach that was taken. It is composed of an iterative
data collection, situation analysis and ideation phase. These phases lead to the decision
of quantitatively assessing the three most promising concepts (Kerbside collection, Bulk
collection, and incentivised recovery). The baseline and the three solutions are then
modelled in Anylogic (DES software) in order to use the results to compare them with
environmental and economical aspects. They are then used to make recommendation
and suggest implementation steps, with the creation of a co­creative poster to support
the discussions.
The modelling approach is inspired by Lieder et al., 2017 however in this project the
focus here is on the take back system (’reverse transport’ in their Figure 2.3). By
simulating agents (heating systems) with components and materials, it is possible for
them to undergo transport, and treatments, impacting what components and masses
end up at different endpoints. This can be based on criteria such as heating system
type, components or derived features from weight, etc.. In the models used, this was
kept fairly simple, but the approach is very scalable ­ albeit somewhat hard to manage.
Other methods, qualitative and quantitative are discussed in more depth in the literature
review.
The agents (heating systems or components) can then end up at different end points.
The accumulated materials &/ components can then be modelled in LCA for the given
EoL/remanufacturing scenario that endpoint represents. In theory the endpoints can
have features too ­ which could be used to control the LCA model.
Figure 1.3: the overarching analysis approach, combining the design of take back systems
with the sustainability evaluation.

available and our own work is outlined below. Some of the work is also documented in
the appendix. The usage of each data source is referenced in the text.

Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:21==
2
How to design a take back system for
the circular economy
This chapter will cover the literature review, the gaps in the literature related to the
design of take back systems in a circular economy.
2.1
What does the literature say?
This thesis is at the intersection of several disciplines and topic areas. A small literature
review was conducted for each area and its finding synthesised to create a cohesive
understanding of the problem and solution space as a whole.
A literature review were conducted for
1. What is circularity & sustainability and how does it relate to large steel products
2. What are take back systems & end of life management options for large products
3. What design methods & tools exists to navigate the design of take back systems
4. What quantitative methods exists to evaluate the performance of take back system
designs
5. What regulation applies and influences take back of heating systems in the EU?
2.1.1
Literature review methodology
The main literature search tools used was findit.dtu.dk which indexes a wide array of
STEM databases. After selecting relevant articles, they were explored in Web of
Science. This approach was chosen as findit.dtu.dk indexes a broad range of
specifically STEM related databases, similar to Scopus, Web of Science and others.
The tool also supports stemming and other important search operators (Library, 2023).
Search strings were generated systematically for each topic. Here the following
procedure was used: 1. finding relevant keywords, 2. finding synonyms, 3. establishing
the right level of stemming. 4. scoping of combination of results. Especially the 4th step
was important as it can easily become a very broad literature search. For each topic
area a spreadsheet was created to keep track of the amount of search results for each
search string. By having the broadest possible keyword combinations, this helped guide
us, when we were approaching the limits of current research.
There was a focus on recent research, circa 10 years. However this was naturally
expanded when needed, especially through the use of Web of Science, to gather
seminal works and review articles. Due to the broad amount of categories to cover,
there was a focus on other literature reviews and meta analyses. However as specific
questions and gaps emerged we found specific articles discussing those topics.
2.1.2
An overview on sustainability and circularity
Circular economy (CE) is a concept that has been used and defined by multiple sectors
such as politics, economy, industry and more. Korhonen et al., 2018 argue that finding a
realistic and scientific definition is not an easy task because of the multitude of sectors
that have co­developed the CE concept. They attempt to define it as:
“Circular economy is an economy constructed from societal production­consumption
systems that maximises the service produced from the linear nature­society­nature
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
11
==PAGE:22==
material and energy throughput flow. This is done by using cyclical materials flows,
renewable energy sources and cascading­type energy flows. Successful circular
economy contributes to all the three dimensions of sustainable development. Circular
economy limits the throughput flow to a level that nature tolerates and utilises
ecosystem cycles in economic cycles by respecting their natural reproduction rates.”
(Korhonen et al., 2018, p.39)
The goal of circular economy is to frame the economic and human activities so that it
respects the nature and earth ecosystems limits. Steffen et al., 2015a define these limits
as planetary boundaries. Each planetary boundary has a safe operating space where
earth systems are within their limits. An uncertainty zone where an increasing risk of
crossing the limits of ecosystem stability. Finally a zone of high risk where the limits are
likely to be crossed. On the same note, Borrello et al., 2020 states that traditionally CE
is linked to eco­efficiency which aims to reduce the relative impact for a given business
activity, which only reduces the rate of impact. It is argued that CE should strive for
eco­effectiveness where the goal is to reduce the total impact to an acceptable level for
the ecosystems. Thus, viewing CE as a systemic transition and not just as a business
model innovation strategy.
Korhonen et al., 2018 stress that CE aims to improve the triple bottom line (TBL) of
sustainability by improving environmental, economical and social metrics. In the view of
material use improvement, they mention take­back systems and reverse logistics
concepts as part of business model innovation potentials.
Even thought the TBL is a fundamental aspect of CE, Kravchenko et al., 2020 finds that
most papers analysing CE metrics fail to include measurements on all three metrics:
environmental, economical and social impact. Most papers are actually only measuring
“natural resources” related indicators. Only a few of them include environmental metrics
and link material production with waste production. Most of the papers also focus their
efforts on recycling as a CE strategy, leaving behind durability, performance, and other
reuse activities.
Albæk et al., 2020 say that the design decision made in the early development of
products have a huge impact on the circulation of the materials and their environmental
impact. That is why they argue that it is worth looking at design choices with a circular
approach. The tool they developed is for product design but the general idea can apply
to systems design as well. The approach used consists of a few steps that are inspired
by life cycle thinking and existing Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) methods. (1) define the
systems hotspots, (2) compare the concepts, (3) define the scope and circular
strategies, (4) Prioritise the circular strategies, (5) customise evaluation, (6) be
informative, (7) Remain simple and guided.
Blomsma et al., 2019 define the different circularity strategies of parts and products
through an extensive literature review and industry workshops. They define the different
reuse options and are presented in a hierarchy:
(1) Reuse: “Extend to new use cycle by reusing a part/product (discarded/not in use)
that is still in good condition and can fulfil its original function in a different use context
(new customer/user).” It implies a restricted amount of processing like cleaning and
packaging (Blomsma et al., 2019).
(2) Refurbish: “Extend to new use cycles by returning a part/product (discarded/not in
use) to a satisfactory working condition that may be inferior to the original specification.
(This may involve: cleaning, repairing, resurfacing, repainting, re­sleeving. Partial
12
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:23==
disassembly envisioned” (Blomsma et al., 2019).
(3) Remanufacture: “Extend to new use cycles by returning a product (discarded/not in
use) to at least Original Equipment Manufacturer (OEM) performance specification and
quality. (Usually more rigorous and costly than refurbishment and involves total
disassembly and reassembly.” (Blomsma et al., 2019)
Sustainability in CE are also highly related to recycling. More than that, CE is often
described as a closed­loop system where resources get recycled almost infinitely.
However, closed­loop systems can only be achieved when the material is not
downcycled. Helbig et al., 2022 describes the difference between recycling, upcycling
and downcycling. In contrast with the European Union’s Waste Framework Directive
recycling definition, Helbig et al., 2022 define downcycling based on literature review
and workshops:
(4) Recycling: “Any recovery operation by which waste materials are reprocessed into
products, materials or substances whether for the original or other purposes. It includes
the reprocessing of organic materials but does not include energy recovery and the
reprocessing into materials that are to be used as fuels or for backfilling operations.”
(Directive, 2008).
(5) Downcycling: “Downcycling is the phenomenon of quality reduction of materials
reprocessed from waste relative to their original quality, where waste means any
substance or object which the holder discards or intends or is required to discard.
Downcycled materials count as recycled materials. One can distinguish between
thermodynamic, functional, and economic downcycling.” (Helbig et al., 2022).
It is said that downcycling is an effect of recycling and that it depends on the value that is
expected from the recycled material. A material that requires a higher thermodynamic
effort to be recycled is thermodynamically downcycled. A material that cannot fulfil the
same function as it’s previous life cycle, is functionally downcycled. A material that
losses economic value after recycling is economically downcycled. The three
perspectives are closely associated to each other. The main reasons for downcycling
are evaluated by Helbig et al., 2022 to be dilution, contamination, low demand and
design related.
(6) Upcycling: In the contrary, upcycling would describe materials that are recycled to a
higher value than their previous life cycle (Borrello et al., 2020). This is a somewhat
vague term, that can be extremely case, business model, and temporally dependent ­
e.g., influenced by the current valuation of materials/products. In the report, for brevity
and clarity, the term upcycling is used as an umbrella term for reuse, repurposing,
remanufacturing, refurbishing and improving the recycling significantly (functional
recycling). Some literature uses the word reuse as this umbrella word, however as that
this is a specific strategy, and brings connotations that the component can simply be
reused, we prefer using upcycling, as it is strategy independent. Viessmann uses
repurposing in their materials and internally.
So when the term upcycling is used in this report, it refers to strategies that improve the
circularity and reduces environmental impact. Not necessarily strategies that increase
the economic value ­ however this will most often also be the case.
Korhonen et al., 2018 mention the limits of CE such as the thermodynamic nature of the
resources that limits their ability of being recycled and reused in a close loop theorised
by Georgescu­Roegen, 1971. Moreover, reuse, remanufacturing and refurbishing should
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
13
==PAGE:24==
be prioritised compared to recycling, given that the latter has a lower thermodynamic
efficiency. An other point made is that the perception of concept of waste has an impact
on the way they are handled and represented in society. This makes it harder to support
the upcycling of materials when they are represented as waste in the first place.
How steel fits in the circular economy
The analysed products are made of around 80% steel which is one of the most recycled
materials. Su et al., 2022 give a global overview of the two main routes through which
steel is produced. Blast furnace­basic oxygen furnace (BF­BOF) accounting for 73% of
the production and mainly for primary steel. Besides that, the Electric arc furnace (EAF)
route accounts for 26% of the steel production and mainly produces secondary steel
which is scrap based. The steel scrap accounts for 32% of the total steel production
when taking in account the share used in primary steel production for cooling purposes
(Su et al., 2022; IEA, 2022b). Even though steel is supposedly recycled infinitely, one
reason given for the lower amount of secondary steel production is that scrap supply is
limited by past production (Pardo and Moya, 2013). Indeed, steel is often used in long
lived products and the supply is constrained by the lower production from 10­20 years
ago in the case of heating systems.
It is highly recognised that EAF steel production is less energy and carbon intensive
than the BF­BOF route (Su et al., 2022; Li et al., 2018; Harvey, 2021). However, Harvey,
2021 says that the secondary steel production mostly supplies the construction industry
because of the low steel quality that is inherent to the wide range of post­customer steel
scrap properties. This implies that steel is mostly downcycled because of the lack of
knowledge on the composition of scrap and the integration of tramp elements. Li et al.,
2018 found that EAF have a higher potential of reducing the overall carbon emissions of
the steel industry as they are more energy efficient and prone to the use of renewable
energies.
On the economical side, IEA, 2020 reports that the production prices between BF­BOF
and EAF today are relatively similar. However, MPP, 2020 looks at the potential future of
the production technology prices and find that EAF will probably have a significantly
lower price in 2030 and 2050 compared to BF­BOF. The downside with EAF is the
current electricity availability and the constraint of scrap supply. They also suggest that
the growing social tendency to recycle materials should help improving the scrap supply
in the future, but it needs policy incentives to have significant impact on steel production.
However, even with the best efforts and arriving at a steady state stock, Harvey, 2021
founds that the need of iron ore mining would remain at about 10% of the current mining
rate. The reason is that the accumulation of tramp elements in the recycling and the
losses during the life cycle of the steel reduce the number of cycles the material can
forego and thus require raw material input. In this sense the BF­BOF route still has its
place in the transition to a circular and sustainable steel industry, and even in the case
where the steel consumption reaches a plateau.
The next step would be to have a perfect knowledge about the scrap supply in order to
limit the integration of tramp elements in the steel production. As Korhonen et al., 2018
says, only utilising the infinite energy of the sun and perfectly retracing each elements
during the steel processing would allow a theoretical perfect recycling, but how to move
in that direction?
The quality of scrap has a large influence on the energy and electricity expended during
recycling in electric arc furnaces (EAF). Haupt et al., 2017 found that low quality scrap
results in 38% higher energy demand, compared to average scrap mixes, at the same
14
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:25==
EAF. This increased the carbon footprint of steel production in EAF by 16% (Haupt et al.,
2017). Therefore ensuring high quality scrap and using pretreatment, might be an
avenue for reducing emissions of recycling steel. The study is limited by not looking at
the resulting steel quality. Generally the resulting steel quality is rarely considered from
a quantitative point of view, and it is difficult to do so (Haupt et al., 2017). It can however
be deduced that being more certain in ones scraps chemical composition, one can
achieve a tighter range of steel qualities. The study stresses that EAF is still preferable
to primary production, and that low quality sources of steel still needs to be recycled,
which is less carbon intensive than the loss of material (Diener and Tillman, 2015).
Scrap quality standards and how scrap is traded between scrap sellers and steel mills is
the defining feature here. Both actors facing different incentives. Scrap sellers being
motivated by lowest cost per scrap delivery and steel mills in securing feedstock that
enables them to achieve the desired steel quality at the lowest cost. (Compañero et al.,
2021)
Steel quality is hard to define and in industry it is generally defined by: dimensions
content, physical conditions, shapes and homogeneity. (Compañero et al., 2021). Some
standards do exist (EFR, n.d.) and they differ slightly internationally, e.g., Sweden has
their own standards. These scrap quality criteria exists to have something to negotiate
prices and conduct transactions. However a lot of information is missing, and it affords a
certain type of recycling that is likely to result in both dissipation of critical elements and
accumulation of tramp elements. There are also difficulties in what denotes high quality
scrap, e.g., copper is generally considered a tramp element but can in some steel alloys
be an alloying element. However the more that is known about the scrap composition,
the less virgin materials is needed to achieve the desired alloy (Compañero et al., 2021).
Yellishetty et al., 2011a supports that alloying elements can have positive or negative
impact on the steel properties. Depending on the desired functional results of the
recycled steel, alloying/tramp elements can be utilised or contaminate the material.
In a follow up study, (Compañero et al., 2023) found that prefect information of scrap
composition could save steel mills between 8­10% of their production cost. This
highlights the value of redefining scrap standards and negotiating unique prices with
steel mills when one has significant quantities of extremely high quality scrap. With
perfect information, the study found that 100% scrap utilisation was possible, whilst with
partial information, a maximum of 81% was possible. Nakamura et al., 2017 supports
these findings by saying that the sorting of scrap steel has a huge impact on the ability to
reuse alloying elements as such. They find that with a poor sorting only 30% of the
chromium and Nickel elements can be reused compared to 70% with a high level of
sorting based on their alloying type called functional recycling.
Upcycling at the heart of circular economy
Peng et al., 2022 finds that remanufacturing can contribute up to 50% of the global
warming potential (GWP) reduction of the products when excluding use and end­of­life
phase. Herrmann et al., 2021 finds that LCAs on remanufacturing often only look at the
materials and exclude the use phase, the fact that environmental benefits would hold
when adding the use phase is a strong assumption and not true in all cases. They look
at energy using products and find that environmental benefits hold only in the case of a
technologically mature products. Ziout et al., 2022 provides a Life Cycle inventory of the
remanufacturing process of an air conditioner (AC). The purpose is to deliver an
authentic documentation of remanufacturing process that can be used in other studies to
help assessing the environmental impact of such processes. They assume the use of
100 watt tools such as angle grinder and screw drivers for the dismantling. The
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
15
==PAGE:26==
refrigerant (here R22) is safely collected and disposed of. They describe the cleaning
phase using compressed air, steam, and the energy needed for these processes. Liu
et al., 2016 assess the remanufacturing of a cylinder head which is subject to higher
mechanical constraints than AC or heating systems parts. Using high tech laser
cladding to remanufacture the product, they still find a reduction of environmental impact
of 63.8% on average on the observed indicators for the entire life cycle. They also find
the limit to which the remanufacturing is not the environmentally preferred process,
which is when the number of cracks on the cylinder exceeds 16.
Mallick et al., 2023 gives a comprehensive set of indicators related to reverse logistic
and thus closely linked to this project. They span the three main aspects of sustainability
(financial, environmental and social) and even touch operational and management
indicators. The most relevant sub­aspect for this project are
1. Financial indicators
(a) Cost: Specifically logistic costs and treatment costs. Costs are also an
important indicator for other stakeholders such as installers and Original
Equipment Manufacturers (OEMs).
(b) Profitability: The profit by recovery efficiency is the preferred indicator for the
case.
(c) Revenues: The total revenues from a given number of products.
2. Environmental indicators
(a) Emissions & Pollution: The prioritised indicator is CO2 equivalent emissions
as the company’s target is to arrive at a net­zero emissions by 2050.
However all other emissions and pollution are part of the evaluation to avoid
burden shifting.
(b) Energy: The total required energy is a strong indicator as it is directly
correlated to CO2 emissions and costs.
(c) Resource conservation: It is the mainly used indicators for CE and recovery
rate is divided into two main indicators, recycling rate and remanufacturing
rate. In addition, products diverted from lower value end­of­life (energy
recovery and landfill) is an interesting indicator for the company.
(d) Waste: Waste disposal costs, and waste diverted away from lower value
end­of­life.
3. Social indicators
(a) Other stakeholders: Here other stakeholders encompass installers and OEMs
in priority. As the business model is B2B2C, the final customers has very low
impact on the end­of­life decisions for the product. For installers, the main
social impact is the excessive working hours, which is linked to an acceptable
wage indicator. For OEMs it is the fulfilment of the expected companies
decision making. for both stakeholders, costs are also an important indicator,
and is covered in the cost section.
4. Operational indicators
(a) Collection: The maximum accessible distance to a collection/drop­off centre
is the main indicator in this section.

Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:27==
2.1.3
Take back systems and end of life management of products
Take back systems tends to be defined as ”... the series of activities required to retrieve
a product from a customer in order to either dispose it or recover value” (Mallick et al.,
2023; Prahinski and Kocabasoglu, 2006). Take back systems abstractly consists of: 1.
product acquisition, 2. reverse logistics and 3. value recovery. (Guide Jr. and
Van Wassenhove, 2006; Larsen et al., 2022). Depending on the goal, this scope might
be expanded to re­marketing and internal reuse as done by (Larsen et al., 2022),
however this also falls under value recovery.Take back systems have many names, and
in literature it is referred to as: reverse logistics, circular supply chain, dedicated
recycling, product recovery and closed/open loop supply chain. There are some
differences in the meaning, but the use in literature is not always consistent. Generally,
reverse logistics, refers to the logistics of transporting an acquired product to a value
recovery facility. Product recovery is also used, however not much, but generally refers
to resource recovery activities ­ i.e., focusing on recovering materials such as copper
etc.. (Parajuly et al., 2016). the term product recovery conflicts with the chemical
industry activities and research into chemical product recovery. Dedicate recycling is
similar in focusing on either correctly handling hazardous waste, or recovering valuable
materials. Open/closed loop supply chains refers to whether the waste flows are
organized in the same sector or different sectors (Mallick et al., 2023; Vegter et al.,
2020).
Reverse logistics in research, often focuses on optimising returning products to
manufactures, due to warranty, product recalls and similar ­ not due to EPR or EoL.
Most research also centres around B2C value chains or B2B ­ whilst our value chain is
business to business to consumer. End of life centred research, often focuses on
smaller consumer WEEE product. Residential heating and climate systems do fall under
WEEE legislation ­ any product using electromagnetism to operate does (Directive L
197/38/EC, 2012), they are significantly larger, heavier, longer lifespan and contains
very small amounts of electronic components by mass compared to e.g., speakers,
lamps, vacuum cleaners, monitors etc.. They are also more seldomly replaced, due to
their long life time.
A close analogy is white goods, such as tumble dryers, refrigerators, washing machines
etc.. which has been studied too. However this research generally focuses on
organisations which refurbish white goods to sell them in a reverse market or just
recycling. (Lechner and Reimann, 2015).
Reverse markets encompasses markets where typically customers, but also
businesses, offer products or components, at the end of use or end of life to recovery
companies. (Stindt et al., 2017). This definition also extends to businesses reselling
EoU/EoL products to other businesses. Stindt et al., 2017 Evaluated multiple reverse
markets, including white goods using their proposed 5 reverse market forces, inspired
by Porters five forces (Porter, 1989). For brevity, their most relevant and analogous
findings have been summarised here:
1. Access to recoverable products
(a) High market potential that is geographically dispersed.
(b) Significant proportion of bad condition products, that are hard to move.
2. Threat of market entry
(a) Technically hard, but low strategic costs and ineffective certifications & laws
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
17
==PAGE:28==
3. Rivalry for recoverable products
(a) Multiple very heterogeneous rivals: brokers, re­manufacturers and recyclers.
With few alternatives and a lot of intermediaries.
4. Adverse effects on core business
(a) A product category with a high pace of innovation, with products designs
unsuitable for re­manufacturing with high quality differences between
recovered products.
5. Remarketing opportunities
(a) Known secondary markets of unknown size, with customers willing to pay for
reputable brands old products ­ but with limited growth potential due to the
continuous innovation in the technology and limited product stream.
Reverse markets can therefore be seen as being technically hard, opaque and therefore
economically hard to compete in. Especially due to the low mobility and geographical
dispersal of both established specialised rivals and the value generation possibilities
with offered products.
Legislation and regulation is not homogenised across European markets, with different
requirements for handling discarded products. Also taxation of waste is often based on
product weight rather than resources and material compositions. Which is a hindrance
from a circular and sustainability perspective. (Uhrenholt et al., 2022)
Reverse logistics current literature focuses on the trade­offs of partnering with 3rd
parties that can collect the product whilst remaining in control and being targeted (also
known as: intelligent product return), (Uhrenholt et al., 2022). This is especially true to
B2C markets as was the focus of that research. There is some academic disagreement
on the effects of remanufactured goods. Some find that it can cannibalise existing
markets, however others argue they address another market segment. Some fear it can
dilute the brand and/or that re­manufactured goods can be perceived as inferior as they
are made of waste. Of course most of this depends on disclosing and marketing the
products as re­manufactured.
Most literature finds synergies between product servitization and maintaining ownership
as in many value chains, there is a need to incentive the customer/last hand on the
product to exert the effort to return the product. This also inevitably results in affecting
the brand, to drive participation. (Uhrenholt et al., 2022)
Recovering value from acquired products is one of the main cost drivers. It is hard to
generalise findings, but for the most studied product categories, there tends to be a
preference for manual disassembly, as it avoid contaminating materials to increase the
value generation. However all of this is very case and product dependent. Porta et al.,
2005 found that destructive disassembly is a very advantageous technique for
household appliances. They evaluated different cutting technologies to optimise
disassembly times, as large parts of the household appliances needed to be broken
down. They found that plasma cutters are surprisingly safe, fast, ergonomic and
versatile for cutting sheet metal products. Preferable to nibbler and angle grinders, for
cutting sheets and chassis elements.
Quite a lot of research covers disassembly planning and optimisation, i.e., Xia et al.,
2020; Anil Kumar et al., 2021; Guo et al., 2021; Ren et al., 2021, to mention a few. The
focus of the research largely is modelling a product, e.g., as a graph, and using
18
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:29==
annealing and similar search algorithms to find the optimal order of operations, with
multiple constraints and goals. A lot of this research is highly relevant for large, valuable
products (large industrial equipment, buildings, air planes etc.) or if you have a large
stream of homogeneous products, like during a product recall. The research also
focuses on replacing parts, and generally is non destructive. The research is highly
relevant, and if the product stream affords these types of optimisations, the earnings and
environmental savings would of course increase. However in the context of a
heterogeneous, less controlled product stream, it might be less applicable and overly
complex. It can be speculated, that similar optimisations and leanings may be achieved
through utilisation of LEAN and Kaizen. Embedding continuous learning and
improvement in a disassembly line, of homogeneous products, could potentially achieve
the same savings for many products, and be more flexible.
Critiques aside, these types of studies can be relevant in the product design phase, if
the product is to be taken back or serviced. They can potentially identify issues in new
product designs.
Uhrenholt et al., 2022 finds that there is a need to adopt a systems perspective to be
successful in taking back products’. As it is a highly multi disciplinary process with
constraints involving many different disciplines.
2.1.4
Design methods and tools to design take back systems
To guide the design of take back systems a few different frameworks and design
methods have been developed and suggested in literature. The literature review
methodology will miss a lot of grey literature, which tends to be be rich in easy to use
and practical design methods.
One of the most prevailing frameworks is De Brito & Dekkers, ”Why, how and what”
framework described in their seminal paper (De Brito and Dekker, 2004).
1. Why are things returned?
2. How are returns processed?
3. What is being returned?
The framework also identifies three general forces driving reverse logistics 1.
economics, 2. legislation and 3. corporate citizenship. The main reverse logistics
activities: 1. collection, 2, inspection, 3. re­processing and 4. redistribution. the main
product characteristics: 1. the product composition, 2. the deterioration, 3. the use
pattern. Finally also the actors involved in a reverse logistics system: 1. forward supply
chain actors, 2. specialised reverse chain actors, 3. opportunistic players (e.g., charities
and independent scarpers). (De Brito and Dekker, 2004).
The framework is easy to use and captures the main areas to investigate when
designing take back systems or reverse logistics network. The framework does however
not specify or detail the influence of each of the dimensions. A key strength is the
general applicability of the framework, and has been widely used in literature. However
its generality leaves a lot up to the practitioners.
The already discussed (Uhrenholt et al., 2022) adapts the De Brito & Dekker paper into
a nested ”context, supply chain company” model, to investigate the dimensions
impacting the financial performance of product take­back systems.
The more narrow framing here, affords some ergonomics in focusing on the financial
performance. Their figure 9, neatly summarises the key considerations when focusing
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
19
==PAGE:30==
on achieving a profitable value chain. Furthermore the paper identified 12 factors
impacting the financial performance. Their more detailed factors are reported in a set of
5 figures/tables. Split across the context, supply chain, company, product and
operations. Illustrated below:
Figure 2.1: Uhrenholt et al., 2022s figure 9. illustrating the relations across the three main
dimensions affecting the financial performance of product take back systems.
Compared to De Brito & Dekkers framework, their paper and framework offers concrete
factors, their effects and thereby provides and overview of what to consider in the design
process of take back systems ­ as economic feasibility is paramount for most cases.
In the paper (Mallick et al., 2023) the authors do a systematic review of take back
system literature, with the goal of proposing a conceptual framework from planning
reverse logistics. Uniquely this study focuses on products at end of life, not end of use.
Not only does the paper provide an abstract framework, it gathers relevant: KPIs,
logistic setups, cost sharing, incentives, relevant technologies and regulation ­ to
support the design and planning of take back systems.
The proposed framework, illustrated below, expands upon De Brito & Dekkers
framework. Especially detailing the ”How” section and adding an implementation and
evaluation step. With the supportive information overviews, this offers a good way of
exploring the design space for taking products back. In talking to the lead author Mallick
(#Mallick 2023c) and reviewing take back literature, this is the most comprehensive
more qualitative framework. The framework can easily be used as a boundary object for
workshop and as a description of a take back system flow. The authors acknowledge
that the framework is as of yet untested ­ but for this thesis intent, it offers the most
tangible approach to explore taking back heating systems (Mallick et al., 2023). The
framework can also be expanded, adding quantitative methods to many of the steps.

Figure 2.2: Mallick et al., 2023, framework expanding upon De Britos & Dekkers frame­
work.
Another source literature that was extremely valuable was the Circit Workbooks. Circit
Norden was a 5 year research project (2017­2021), across most of Scandinavia,
focusing on creating tools to support Nordic companies enter and explore the circular
economy. They published 6 workbooks, offering design methods and strategies to enter
the circular economy (Blomsma et al., 2020). Especially the last book collects all of the
threads and provides the resources to run circular economy design focused workshops.
Containing all needed to run the workshop: guides for facilitators, design methods,
posters, time plans etc.. Critically all the books and methods have been tested in
industry.
• Book 1, covers circular economy sustainability screening
• Book 2, covers circular economy business modelling
• Book 3, covers circular product design and development
• Book 4, cover smart circular economy (digitisation, IoT, big data etc.. )
• Book 5, covers how to close the loop (re­circulation and recovery strategies)
• Book 6, covers how to collaborate and network for a circular economy
The books provided actionable insights on when each circular approach is relevant, and
is an comprehensive resource on circular economy in practice. For this thesis books 4, 5
and especially 6 very applicable.
2.1.5
Quantitative methods for modelling take back systems performance
Quantitative assessment and investigation of the take back system configurations is a
central part of this project. A multitude of different analysis approaches can be
applicable ­ but what has been used in literature?
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
21
==PAGE:32==
Simulating take back systems
Discrete event simulation is time and tested in manufacturing and logistics simulation.
Modern software solutions such as Arena and Anylogic, also implement agent based
and system dynamics into their simulation programs, allowing to do a hybrid approach to
simulate complex systems. Simulation in the early design phases gives a risk free option
to investigate vastly different solutions. (Rossetti, 2016)
In literature multiple different simulation techniques have been used to guide the design
of controlled product end of life’s and reverse logistics. (Lieder et al., 2017) used a
multi­method simulation system, using agents for products in a discrete event simulation
for the logistics, and LCA for the environmental impacts. This was then coupled with
different business model configurations and product design considerations. They are
then able to track the amount of product design efforts, in different business models, the
global warming reductions, depending on how much a component is recirculated and
more. This study is quite comprehensive and their figure, shown below offers an
overview of how to approach mixed method simulation from the lens of quantitative
sustainability.
Figure 2.3: (Lieder et al., 2017), how they coupled agents (washing machines) with a
circular supply chain simulation to look at different design & business models impact on
sustainability.
The literature around reverse logistics and take back systems has its’ roots in logistics
research. This has resulted in a lot of papers researching ways of optimising and
designing reverse logistic flows. The prevailing simulation model of choice here is
various forms of mixed integer linear programming models (MILP). In recent years, multi
criteria models have been becoming more common. These allow optimising the reverse
logistics supply chain according to both economical and environmental factors
(Asgharizadeh et al., 2019). These models are incredibly useful in both exploring the
use case, but given the efforts in creating and validating them, they seem to be mostly
used for optimising a single take­back/reverse logistics scheme.
The general simulation framework that will be used is Rosettis simulation methodology,
illustrated in the figure below: 2.4 Rosettis methodology. The final phase of
implementation is omitted, as this project does not involve the actual implementation,
only recommendations and interpretation of the results.
