Figure 2.4: Rosettis simulation methodology: (Rossetti, 2016)
Using Mass Flow Analysis for visualising global steel flows
Brunner and Rechberger, 2004 defines MFA as a good starting point for environmental
analysis. It tries to describe the flow of material and the mass balance of a system. It
also aims to represent a simplified reality to help understanding the flows of materials,
and at the same time still has the required definition to have an accurate vision. The
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
23
==PAGE:34==
basic principles for the use of MFA related to the thesis are:
1. Controlling pathways for materials use and industrial processes.
2. Creating closed loop industry practices.
MFA results are meant to show the most important processes during the life cycle of the
material and thus outlining the hotspots. It also helps to visualise the stocks and losses
of the system and tracks the behaviour of internal loops.
Ohno et al., 2017 and Nakajima et al., 2013 use MFA to optimise the separation of car
scrap fractions in order to recover a maximum of alloying elements so that they keep
their functionality when recycled. This approach can help companies to manage their
design and waste management processes in order to functionally recycle materials and
have a minimal downcycling effect. This approach was considered for the thesis but only
the visualisation part has been included in the scope of the analysis.
Using Life Cycle Assessment to measure the environmental impact of taking
heating systems back
As stated in Hauschild et al., 2018, LCA serves as a valuable tool for product and
systems designers, guiding their assessment of the sustainability implications of their
design choices. Approximately 80% of products ans systems environmental impact is
determined at the design phase. Therefore, adopting a systematic approach that
incorporates environmental and life cycle thinking in product and system design confers
competitive advantages to companies. In order to implement ecodesign in products and
systems effectively, it is necessary to identify their critical areas, develop diverse design
concepts and scenarios, compare their environmental impacts, and ultimately select the
most favourable option based on their triple bottom line (TBL) performance. It is said
that, when aiming to create environmentally friendly products and systems, it is highly
recommended to utilise LCA as a supporting tool. Taking advantage of its holistic
perspective allows quantitative comparisons of design alternatives and prevents shifting
environmental burdens. The main limitation of using LCA in the early design phase lies
in the uncertainty surrounding the future processes that products or systems will
undergo. One approach to overcome this challenge is to utilise LCA studies conducted
on similar products or rely on existing LCA data for guidance. Additionally, it is not
advisable to rely solely on LCA when making ecodesign decisions; instead, it should be
combined with a wide range of established design and synthesis methods. Performing a
comprehensive LCA requires considerable time and high­quality data, while ecodesign
is an iterative process that often operates with limited data in the initial stages. To
address this scaling challenge, a Simplified­LCA can be employed to identify hotspots,
allowing designers to highlight key design opportunities and challenges. A suggested
eco­design process proposes to use the following steps:
1. Understanding the context
2. Conducting an overview
3. Developing an eco­profile
4. Analysing the stakeholder network
5. Quantifying relevant factors
6. Generating conceptual designs
7. Formulating an eco­strategy
24
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:35==
The recycling allocation approach utilised in the metal industry and described by
Johnson et al., 2013, is known as EoLR (End­of­Life Recycling). It involves accounting
for the impact of primary production, subtracting recycling credits, and considering
recycling and decommissioning consumption. Here is an image to help illustrate the
concept:
Figure 2.5: (Johnson et al., 2013), about the EoLR (End of Life recycling) crediting ap­
proach
In this approach, the emphasis is on understanding the environmental implications of
both primary production and the recycling process. By accounting for recycling credits
and evaluating supplementary consumption from recovery process, a comprehensive
assessment of the overall sustainability performance can be achieved.
2.1.6
The role of regulations in waste management and circular economy
An entire project could be written about the relevant regulation around waste
management of large white goods, its developments and how it applies to circularity.
This chapter briefly describes the most relevant legislation for taking back heating
systems.
WEEE ­ Waste from Electrical and Electronic Equipment
WEEE is an EU directive, with different implementations in all EU countries, so specific
implementation details varies from country to country.
The registration and management of the WEEE implementation is delegated to an
organisation which manages the registration, guidance and detailed implementation of
the WEEE directive. In Germany it is managed through Stiftung Ear. Stiftung EAR,
authorises 3rd parties to handle OEMs/importers extended producer responsibility.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
25
==PAGE:36==
Viessmann, as most others, have partnered with an authorised representative.
(Directive L 197/38/EC, 2012)
Centrally to this project, WEEE obligates manufacturers or importers of electronic
products, to help ensure and pay for the collection, recycling and disposal of electronic
products. This responsibility can either be managed by the importer or manufacturer
itself or outsourced to a third party, called a Producer Responsibility Organisation (PRO)
­ the latter is by far the norm.
All products which uses electromagnetism to operate, with a few exceptions are subject
to WEEE. All products from Viessmann considered in this project are subject to this
regulation and would classify as category 1 or 2 items in 2015, and category 1 items
after 2018. That is ”Temperature exchange equipment” (Andersen, 2022; Directive L
197/38/EC, 2012). Many products are given as examples for the product classifications
from both the EU directive, from Stiftung Ear and its’ Danish equivalent, DPA ­ but none
explicitly mention: gas boilers, oil boilers nor water tanks/heaters. However air
conditioners and heat pumps are mentioned in the 2018 lists. Given the broad nature of
the regulation, broadly speaking includes all products using electromagnetism ­ it is safe
to assume they are all subject to WEEE.
Its the PROs that partners with local recyclers, 3PLS etc.. to ensure responsible
collection and recycling. The cost & environmental benefits sharing principles differs
from PRO to PRO (Andersen, 2022; Stiftung Ear, 2023). The WEEE directives comes
into effect in Germany with the ElectroG Regulation (ErsetztG2129­43, 2015).
WEEE has multiple targets (goals) fore both recovery of products (amount of products
correctly entering the WEEE stream and after 2018, also goals for prepared for re­use or
recycling. For category 1, targets are set for relevant equipment’s to 85% recovery and
80% reuse and recycling.
Whilst the latter, are goals that should help move towards a circular economy, WEEE
has been criticised for being focused on recycling, and that it only accounts for the
products that end up in the WEEE streams. It can therefore be a bad metric for if a
product is reused, i.e., WEEE targets and PROs would not account for consumers
reselling their phone, ensuring its reuse. These types of circular behaviour are therefore
not rewarded in WEEE (Andersen, 2022). Products may also be disposed off through
other routes, and thus not be accounted for.
RoHS ­ Restriction of Hazardous Substances in Electrical and Electronic
Equipment
The RoHS directive aims to reduce and phase out hazardous substances included in
products and materials. For example it sets the maximum content of Lead, Mercury and
Hexavalent chromium to 0.1% per weight of material. Other substances present in
polymers are also restricted like flame retardant (PBDE) and plasticizers (DEHP) (
DirectiveL 174/88/EC, 2011)
In the circular economy, the effect of phasing out chemicals can be unpredictable.
Highly recycled materials can keep a trace of substances in consecutive life cycles even
after a total ban of the said substance (Fantke, 2022).
The Ecodesign directive
The Ecodesign directives for water heaters and water tanks are written with an
eco­efficiency angle. This regulation obliges to have energy labelling and sets
requirements on the energy efficiency, nitrogen oxide emissions and heat losses
(Directive2009/125/EC, 2017).
26
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:37==
There are proposals to adopt new and more ambitious Ecodesign directives by 2030. It
should put requirements on durability, reusability, recycled content, remanufacturing,
carbon footprint and even Digital product passport use
(EuropeanCommsissionPressRelease, 2022).
The F­gas
The current F­gas directives limits the use of fluorinated greenhouse gases. It prohibits
the intentional release of harmful substances and obliges to keep track and limit
leakages. Installation, leak checks and disposal require certification. It also prohibits the
entry in the market of certain substances (Directive L 197/38/EC, 2012).
Emissions trading system
The European Emissions trading system has the target of helping EU industry to
achieve carbon neutrality by 2050 and 55% net reduction (compared to 2005 level) by
2030. Allowances are given to industrial sectors based on their emission assessments.
Depending on the sector, allowances are phased out more or less quickly. However all
free allowance are predicted to be phased out by 2034 (DIRECTIVE L 76/3/EC, 2018).
Thanks to the carbon border tax, non­EU industries that don’t comply with EU standards
will see a tax applied to the imported products. Relevant materials subject to the carbon
border adjustment mechanism are steel and aluminium. These materials might see their
price rising due to the tax by 2026 when the mechanism will start to phase in (BCG,
2021).
2.2
What are the literature gaps?
In the literature several gaps were identified which will be covered here.
2.2.1
Lack of examples of voluntary take back systems
As a lot of recent literature reviews note on take back systems, there are few existing
take back systems. Even with a large amount of research, few companies have scaled
up their take back systems. This limits successful case studies, to draw learning’s from
and results in a large amount of research being theoretical. A large portion of
implemented take back systems are from the personal care, clothing and small
consumer electronics industries (Mallick et al., 2023; RTS, 2021). These products are
logistically easy, with many partnering stored ­ and even if that is not and option, postal
services are an option. Product treatment options and requirements also differ a lot from
this projects case.
2.2.2
Lack of research on B2B2C
The majority of take back system literature focuses on B2C or B2B value chains. The
thesis case focuses on B2B2C case, which poses unique challenges. The waste
generation occurs at end customers, but the ownership of the discarded heating system
transfers to the installer company, a business entity. Issues such as product storage at
companies, how to drive participation and trade offs with existing commercial waste
management strategies is understudied.
2.2.3
Lack of transparent costs & values for take back systems
The current literature has few resources for standard process times and logistic costs in
reverse logistics and take back systems. This can hinder evaluating the economical
viability creating a reliance on company cases, and getting cost estimates with possible
3rd party logistic providers. More price transparency, could help foster a more even
baseline to compare studies of different approaches to take back systems.
2.2.4
When to take something back ­ and when to collaborate
Implementing a take back system can come with downsides, not only for the company in
managing & operating it, but also complicates an increasingly complex waste
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
27
==PAGE:38==
management ecosystem when disposing of ’waste’. Literature is not very clear on how
to evaluate when to collaborate with existing waste management actors, how to do it and
the benefits that can be gained from it.
2.2.5
Realised benefits of sorting scrap
Several studies identified theoretical benefits from increasing the purity and certainty of
scraps purity. However it is unclear whether all of these benefits will be realised if the
scraps purity is increased. This largely depends on the smelter/recycler, and their
utilisation of the scrap quality information.
Current quantitative sustainability assessment studies tends to focus on CO2eq, energy
usage or electricity demand when evaluating recycling. A lot of studies also assume that
the material qualities are preserved and focuses on the main element, e.g., iron in steel ­
and lacks to quantify the loss/dissipation of alloying elements, which are often critical
raw materials. It is entirely possible to quantify these, but it complicates the assessment
and adds uncertainty to the end results.
Scrap quality, whilst multiple widely used national and international standards exists for
scrap classifications, these may be insufficient for a truly circular economy. Efforts into
expanding the classification systems and how to deal with more certain, but still mixed
scrap categories would greatly benefit research into partial dismantling of products,
before riddance. Scrap quality is also intrinsic to the goal of using the scrap, which rarely
is reflected. E.g., if copper is a tramp or alloying element depends on the desired steel
alloy being produced.
Several studies also acknowledge informal secondary markets, and informal actors
removing components from discarded waste. Lack of research into the size, economical
and environmental impact of these markets makes it more difficult to establish a good
baseline. Studies quantifying the prevalence and practices of these informal markets,
will help establish a representative baseline. For if no informal treatment is assumed,
such as informal removal of copper components, then the baselines negative
environmental impacts will be overestimated.
2.2.6
Lack of semi­destructive disassembly planning
A lot of disassembly research focuses on replacing parts, and careful disassembly.
Current research lacks easy to approach methods for planning semi­destructive
disassembly, and guidance on what technologies to use. Circularity centred research is
often keen on careful disassembly, as destructive disassembly is seen as destroying
value or polluting the materials. However the time savings, can in some cases enable
further dismantling, thus creating both economical and environmental value. Research
into how to optimise these trade­offs from a circular material point of view would be
advantageous. Combining this with the current mono­fraction sorting & separation
technologies and new scrap quality techniques could yield concrete methods for
reducing material dissipation.
2.2.7
Guidance on technology obsolescence of large goods
The majority of research assumes product obsolescence results in full product
obsolescence. Little research focuses on the reuse of components from obsolete
product and thus partial reuse or partial remanufacturing. This can often avoid several of
the consumer centric concerns many companies have, i.e., consumer perceptions of
remanufatured products being inferior.
28
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:39==
2.2.8
Lack of research on remanufacturing
It was found the literature on remanufacturing is scarce. Thus, there is a lack of
quantification method that lowers the ability of confidently assessing the benefits of the
process.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
29
==PAGE:40==

Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:41==
3
Situation analysis ­ how are heating
systems disposed and what is the
potential?
3.1
Current and future products
Viessmanns product portfolio is large and spans most heating and climate solutions.
The current product categories offered to residential housing are: 1. Heat pumps 2. Gas
condensing boilers 3. Solar thermal systems 4. Photovoltaics 5. Energy management
solutions 6. Smart connectivity & control solutions 7. Oil condensing boilers 8. Water
heaters 9. Restive electric heaters. The company also offers solutions for commercial
facilities, municipalities and the like. These are however entirely out of scope of this
thesis.
Currently customers are predominately replacing gas heating systems, mainly gas
condensing boilers. However some oil condensing boilers and heat pumps are also
being replaced. Their main substitution are heat pumps, depending on the clients needs
and climate, they may opt to install water tanks, water heaters and solar thermals
systems to meet their heating demands (#Tariq 2023).
Viessmann offers gas condensing boilers in three main types: heating only, combi
(heating and hot water) and system boilers. System boilers are for use with a water
heating storage tank. Customers choice of gas condensing boiler system, generally
depends on their heating needs and the infrastructure available at their residence.
Larger homes, with more users, opt for system solutions. Areas with district heating may
opt for heating only, whilst others need combi gas heaters.
Gas has long been preferable to oil for several reasons. Mainly the fuel economy, as
gas burning can be controlled more granularly. With digital control this allows a
significantly better fuel economy. Gas infrastructure has also been prevalent in most of
the German market, removing the inconvenience of refilling oil tanks. Environmental
legislation across EU has also pushed the market into the more energy efficient gas
condensing boilers.
The main drivers for selecting heating systems have historically been fuel economy, cost
and reliability. However it varies across different customer types. The main ones worked
with internally at Viessmann as personas are: avoiders, pragmatics, sceptics and
enthusiasts. (#Personas 2023). With only pragmatics and enthusiasts adopting new
technology on their own volition. Generally the choice of heating system, for many, relies
on the recommendations from their installers, as end customers generally have long
relations with their installers. This is driven by their annual/bi­annual servicing of heating
systems.
There has been significant efforts from heating systems manufacturers to help guide
and drive uptake of non­fossil heating systems. Some efforts here include guidance in
applying for government rebates for improving energy efficiency or reducing emissions
for residential heating. These efforts have been both targeted at end customer and
towards installer companies. For with a regular in person visit from an installer they can
efficiently & effectively go over the energy efficiency calculations, grants and guide
customers in their possibilities (#Tariq 2023, #CircHeat 2022).
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
31
==PAGE:42==
The overarching current trend is out­phasing of gas condensing boilers. This trend was
already growing, but due to gas price rising dramatically and uncertainty in supply
stability over the past few years, it drove interest in heat pumps to unseen levels.
German Heatpump Association, 2023 Whilst prices and supply security has normalised
somewhat, there is still an incredibly high demand for heat pumps, and customers are
weary of investing in new gas infrastructure (#Installers 2023/03/07)
Whilst the main replacement is heat pumps, they alone may not be sufficient for a
customers heating needs. Here addition of water storage tanks with resistive heating
elements is a common solution. There are other solutions bringing customers into the
territory of energy management solutions.
For with current developments in variable electricity prices, there is interest in optimising
when heating & cooling is running and moving the demand around. Both from the end
customer perspective, but also from electricity grid managers, worrying about load
balancing and expanding the electricity’s grids capacity. They are often called residential
energy management, and several solutions exists depending on the residence.
Overheating and cooling in cheap periods, if one has the storage (either water tanks,
floor heating or the living­space itself) can result in significant savings without noticeable
quality degradation. These solutions can also be combined with battery solutions and
domestic electricity power generation, this can enhance the electricity savings for
heating even more. On top of this, there is also an increasing need and interest for air
condition (esp. cooling) in Europe (IEA, 2022a, #Tariq 2023).
All of these solutions are driving complexity and variety in domestic energy, heating and
cooling management. It is worth considering when a company operating in most of
these markets, considers implementing take back systems, for just heating.
Nonetheless, there is a possibility that the take back system will be expanded to cover
other product categories in the future.
3.2
Current and future ecosystem
The products are not just sold for installation, they are part of a service system, due to
the long life span 10­20 years for gas condensing boilers and heat pumps. This is both
to ensure the durability, but also to ensure efficient operation. To support this a wide
array of service systems are employed to streamline this process.
To investigate the activities and supporting services around heating systems, the life
cycle of a heating system has been mapped for Viessmann using user activity cycles
(UAC) Vandermen, 2000. Three different user activity cycles were used to understand
the product service landscape: 1. the activities around a heating system, 2. the actors
involved in making those steps happen, 3. the digital and value adding services for each
step.
Together these figures illustrates the array of actors involved in decommissioning and
installing new heating systems. The key triggers for servicing and needs for heating
systems. Some of the key findings from these activity maps were that there are
established actors for training installers, the academy. This gives Viessmann the ability
to influence installers perception of how to dispose of the products.
It also highlights the many roles and hats installers wears. They are both acting as a
sales person, technician and installer. In many installer companies these efforts might
be split up. But it notes the importance of installers not only being technically competent
but their role in building relationships with their customers. The digital landscape is quite

simple, from the installers point of view there are 2 central apps in servicing and
installing heating systems. But for the consultation & sales they have 2, one for getting
leads for new customers and one supporting them in securing subsidies for the
customers. (#Installers 2023/03/07, #CircHeat 2022, #Tariq 2023)

3.2.3
Installers
Installers are today generally organised in small businesses, generally consisting
anywhere between of 2­25 employees, the majority working on installing & servicing
various forms of residential heating systems. Significantly larger HVAC companies do of
course exist. In the project the main installer companies we have interviewed have been
based in Denmark or Switzerland, mainly due to difficulty in getting in contact with
German installer companies. The German installer companies have been involved in
surveys, however with a very low response rate. (#Survey 2023 a, #Survey 2023 b,
#Installers 2023/03/07)
The majority of installers are as of 2023 still unusually busy with installing heat pumps
due to the large interest and demand from customers. All installers involved in phone
surveys have been limited in their time due to their packed calendars.
Given the projects focus on taking back heating systems, only the process for replacing
gas condensing heating system will be described. Generally it is as follows: As shown
broadly in the UAC the general steps are as follows: 1. The old heating system is found
36
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:47==
to be uneconomic, either at regular servicing or at system failure (the latter being rare).
2. The heating needs are estimated and various configurations are evaluated between
the installer and customer. This is supported by various digital offerings, guiding the
installer if the customer is eligible for subsidies. 3. The systems are ordered. 4. The
installation is planned. Generally installing heat pumps requires specialists in closing off
old chimneys, and drilling holes for piping to the outdoor unit. It is fairly straightforward
but generally beyond just the competences in the installer company. 5. For higher end
brands, the heating system is delivered just­in­time by the OEMs logistics service. A key
service quality parameter here is to minimise the amount of time the end customer is
without heating. 6. The old heating system is removed, and the new system is installed.
Generally this takes half­hole workday, however this is very dependent on the difficulty
of installing the system in the residence and the chosen solution. 7. Waste and other
stuff is generally stored outside, unprotected from the elements. Given the installation
location, size and weight of some heating systems and especially water­tanks removal is
nontrivial. 8. After installation of the new heating system, remaining waste is gathered
and transported away by the installer. 9. After removal the gas is formally turned off by
the local gas infrastructure manager.
The final step does differ for some installer companies, however in the majority of cases
it is the installer removing the waste, as they are the contact point for heating expertise
for the end customer. It is their reputation on the line and they will be most impacted by
leaving a dirty site, dysfunctional heating system etc.. as they are to be reliant on their
servicing contracts.
Current installer disposal routes and pre­treatment
There is some variance in how installers dispose of heating systems. The disposal
routes have been identified through the surveys and questionnaires ­ the findings have
been confirmed using triangulation, i.e., calling recycling centres and PROs (#Survey
2023 a, #Survey 2023 b, #Installers 2023/03/07, #Elertur 2023, #Recyclers 2023). The
following waste routes in order of prevalence: 1. Installers transports the heating system
waste back to company facility/head quarters and throws it in a mixed metal container
provided and facilitated by a local scrap dealer. 2. Installers transports the heating
system waste home, storing it, and regularly drives the accumulated waste to a nearby
recycling yard/center. 3. Installers transports the waste directly to recycling yard after
replacing the old heating system. 4. Installers having a deal with a local scrap dealer to
pick up the waste from the clients home. 5. If the installer company is replacing many
heating systems in e.g., an apartment complex, they may organise a mixed metal
container with a scrap dealer.
It is still unclear how normal removing components from the heating systems is. From
our data gathering the majority of installers remove no components. If some are
removed, there is a focus on the high value components, mainly the circulation pump
and easily accessible copper parts. Few surveyed did this, some companies also
participate in Grundfos circulation pump take back system. Many HVAC companies do a
lot more than only install residential heating systems, and have a lot of different types of
mixed metal waste: from radiators, to industrial cooling system components. This is one
of the reasons why the mixed metal container agreements is so popular, it is mainly the
convenience for the installer companies. Both minimising their time spent sorting and
transporting waste to recycling yards or directly to scrap yards. Generally scrap dealers
pick up for free if the volume is large and regular enough, paying the regional mixed
metal value. Few installers wanted to share figures, but around 200 euros per container
load. For reference the kg, price for unsorted mixed metal being in range of 0.07 € per
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
37
==PAGE:48==
kg, corresponding to roughly 2900 kg of scrap (ScrapMonster, 2023). Whilst the scrap
dealer also forgo transportation fees, manage and servicing of the containers. It is worth
stressing that the sorted and especially dismantled value of the scrap metal container is
much higher and they would generally have to pay to dispose it at a recycling yard
(#Installers 2023/03/07, #Installers 2023/05/16).
Installer companies deal with more waste than just the old heating system when
replacing. They also deal with the packaging from the new heating system,
miscellaneous piping etc.. How this is stored on site differs but a common solution is a
’big bag’ for construction waste. Packaging waste can have its’ own fraction(s) or
disposed of as grey waste (#Installers 2023/03/07, #Installers 2023/05/16).
Refrigerants from heat pumps needs to be recaptured and disposed off properly, to
avoid GWP and ozone depletion impacts. Refrigerants are recaptured and sent off as
hazardous waste. This is required under the F­gas regulation (Directive 2003/87/EC,
2023). Currently there are several recycling systems for refrigerant, however the goal is
to transition to propane which may make these recycling systems largely redundant, as
these can be safely vented.
It is clear that there are some secondary markets for old heating systems and
components sold as spare parts (Figure 3.5). Prices vary a lot, and any postings on
sites like Ebay, DBA etc.. appear to rather old and stale. But people are trying to sell:
complete used heating systems (either for reuse or for spare parts) and mainly
components like: circulation pumps, manifolds, heat exchangers and expansion vessels
(ebay, 2023; DBA, 2023; dba, 2023a, 2023b). No market studies on the size or value of
the secondary market for heating systems was identified. But it is clear that some
installers and private individuals are at least trying to reuse and capture value from old
heating systems, and that currently a lot of gas heating systems are being replaced,
whilst fully functional. Also note that reuse is not directly rewarded in WEEE, and the
heating systems/components will still be disposed off. b
38
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:49==
Figure 3.5: Components sold on the secondary market by private people
What influences installer waste management practices
HVAC companies choice of waste management solution and product treatment will vary
quite widely from region to region. The higher the cost of labour and the more busy that
installers are in the period they are decommissioning the heating systems, the less
treatment the systems will undergo. In regions with lower labour costs, there is likely
also a higher interest in used spare parts. Which can make it more lucrative to attempt
to resell good condition salvaged components. When/if the scrap price of copper and
similar materials rise, there may be an increased interest from HVAC companies to
retrieve these. Regional regulation may limit installers ability to store old heating
systems. One Danish HVAC company reported that their company was not allowed to
have more than 3 containers for waste before being subject to soil contamination tests
and similar scrap yard regulation. This claim was however not verified (#Installers
2023/05/16).
3.2.4
Waste management today
There are several waste management actors handling both gas heating systems and
heat pumps. In Figure 3.6 below the overarching current waste disposal routes are
presented:
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
39
==PAGE:50==
Figure 3.6: Figure illustrating the design teams qualitative understanding of the factors
impact profitability per heating system
The disposal routes were found by phone surveying & surveys with installers in
Denmark, Switzerland and Germany, as outlined in (#Installers 2023/03/07, #Installers
2023/05/16). Most installers were questioned if that was the normal/standard way of
doing things, and all replied positively.
Multiple installers were puzzled and doubtful of getting scrap dealers to pickup heating
systems from clients houses ­ ”you don’t get a scrap dealer to drive for less than 500kg”
and similar statements from installers in Denmark. Some Danish installers did have
experience with using scrap dealers if they were changing heating systems for an entire
building complex, here they would get a container setup (#Installers 2023/03/07).
Some installers in surveys would drive directly to the recycling yard, however none in
phone surveys used this disposal route. From phone surveying municipal recycling
yards, they did not receive many gas heating systems, and they were sorted as mixed
metal. When explicitly asked about whether that was correct sorting in terms of WEEE
(as ’modern’ gas boilers are subject to WEEE), they responded ”technically yes, but you
know, it is mostly iron and steel” (#Recyclers 2023). Heat pumps that arrive at municipal
recycling yards however were sorted correctly as white goods ­ it is however worth
mentioning that these also appear to be ending up as mixed metal waste in the majority
of cases.
White goods and electronic waste are picked up by local Producer Responsibility
Organisations (PROs), due to WEEE directive on extended producer responsibility. The
main PRO in Denmark was interviewed, they expressed that they did not receive gas
boilers, but did receive a limited amount of heat pumps. This confirms the findings with
surveying installers, that recycling yards is the exception and not the norm ­ and
therefore gas boilers and heat pumps are not being handled by PROs (#Elertur 2023).
2 survey respondents did respond that they have heating systems picked up by scrap
dealers. This confirms the findings from the previous project that it does exists, but it is
somewhat rare. In the prior project, the installer then foregoes all scrap value, and
ensures that the decommissioning waste is ’organised’ and easy to pick up for the scrap
dealer. It is unclear if these scrap dealers process the heating systems differently.
That concludes the brief overview of the waste disposal routes. Now onto a description
of the different waste actors.
40
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:51==
The process of removing an old heating system
For gas boilers and watertanks the process is fairly straight forward. The gas is turned
off at the the valve, so is electricity and water. The gas boiler is then safe to be removed.
Depending on the size of boiler and gas and installation location transporting it out may
be a bit cumbersome, requiring a trolley and similar to transport it out in a safe manner.
Plastic foil or similar covers, may be put down to avoid damaging the floor or carpet in
the residence. If the gas condensing boiler is replaced with another heating technology,
various piping, gas line and similar may have to be plugged up, shut off and similar.
Heat pumps are more difficult to decommission, and dependent on the product and its’
installation. Refrigerants needs to be reclaimed from the systems, the main techniques
is using a refrigerant recovery machine, and continuously weighing the amount of
refrigerant recovered. By closing part of the refrigerant cycle, one can get the
compressor in the heat pump system to force out the refrigerant into the recovery tank.
However for some refrigerants and systems, there is a need to draw a vacuum to fully
extract the refrigerant. The procedures are well known and practised by HVAC
technicians as it is a legal requirement for some refrigerants, see section on F­gas. The
procedure is different for future propane heat pumps as R290 (propane) is legal to vent,
due to it’s low global warming and ozone depletion impacts. Propane however poses a
other challenges for fires. Generally a vacuum is drawn in the container tank, to suck out
the R290. After evacuating the system is flushed with nitrogen. For some heat pump
installation sizes it may also be legal to vent R32 (#Survey 2023 a, #CircHeat 2022,
#Tariq 2023).
Recycling centres/yards
Recycling yards are generally run as non profit ventures jointly by municipalities in a
region, however exceptions do exists. Recycling yards handle both residential and
commercial waste. For commercial actors they are generally pay­to­use, either weight
based or based on an entry fee, which is higher for dangerous and toxic materials. Due
to being the point of contact for private individuals, they handle most fractions. However,
the amount of waste fractions can vary quite widely from recycling yard to recycling yard.
This can result in unfavourable situations and a hard to navigate recycling system for
companies. (#Installers 2023/03/07, #Recyclers 2023).
Recycling yards have fractions to handle the different WEEE categories. These are then
routinely picked up by the local producer responsibility organisations, for further
treatment.
In our surveys, some installer companies do use recycling yards, it is however not a
common disposal route for the heating systems themselves. However many installer
companies are HVAC companies, and therefore deal with a lot of different waste, much
more than just mixed metal. They may also handle various forms of plastic waste,
significant amount of packaging waste (cardboard, paper etc.). As decommissioning
heat pumps become more common installers increasingly have to evacuate the
refrigerant and treat that container as dangerous waste. Here recycling yards are a
common solution to get rid of it. However dangerous waste, such as refrigerants, is
subject to extra fees at recycling yards.
As recycling yards are pay to use, they are generally the least favoured solution by
installers, as scrap dealers generally are willing to pay for the waste ­ even if it is from a
large perspective insignificant amounts. Some HVAC companies may even seek out
handlers of specific fractions, even if it is not a source of income: ”barely if even covering
the fuel costs ­ but we also do it I guess, to do the right thing” (#Installers 2023/05/16)
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
41
==PAGE:52==
Producer responsibility organisations
Producer responsibility organisations (PRO) differ quiet a lot, and there are both
commercial and non­profit PRO organisations. PROs’ service offerings differ quite a bit:
some specialise in EPR for packaging, others in WEEE and total solutions also exists.
PROs service offerings publicly available differ quite significantly, and in the limited
contact wit PROs in the project, it appears that PROs main route for getting WEEE
products is from regular pick ups from regional recycling yards (#Elertur 2023,
#Recyclers 2023).
However as the PROs handle the OEMs EPR, there might be more obligations. For
larger equipment, the end customer can demand that the electronic product is picked up
from their home, free of charge. This is also the case for Viessmanns products, however
this is used very little, ”a handful of times a year and only by very environmentally
regulation aware consumers”. This current system is an expenditure for Viessmann, and
a compliance thing, currently no special treatment is known to happen to these products
(paraphrasing from #Tariq 2023).
This pickup service is for example provided by the Landbell group for Amazon.de, and it
states that the pickup within 10­15 business days depending on demand, and that for
”select larger goods, larger than 120x60x60cm” it can be scheduled to be picked up with
delivery of other items (amazon, 2023).
As it is a competitive market, collaborating with PROs is an avenue of both enhancing
the price­per­tonne of product and the environmental benefits. In a comparative study
done by Andersen, 2022 that interviewed several PROs across northern EU, they found
that PROs do not necessarily view product identification as a necessary step, as they do
not do such targeted efforts. There are exceptions, e.g., one PRO needing to date
products from some client as that determined whether a toxic chemical was used in that
product. Several technologies have been investigated to improve these abilities, as
there are some theoretical benefits too it: 1. better allocation and tracking of product
recovery and therefore cost sharing. 2. more targeted efforts, possibly allowing for
recapturing specific components or materials. However given the complexity in scanning
and identifying products in such a heterogeneous product stream that PROs handle, it is
difficult without some standard for product identification, that gives useful information for
PROs and recyclers of the product composition. Efforts are being made in this area,
namely, the digital product passport (DPP).
Some PROs are actively working with circularity, especially reuse & refurbishment, and
some with component recovery/harvesting. The UK Environcom and Danish Elretur both
work with reuse, especially repairing and refurbishinging old white goods, and reselling
them. Both organisations offer warranties for the products. Several other PROs and
similar actors do the same. This is generally limited to ’classic’ white goods:
dishwashers, dyers, freezers, fridges, tumbledryers and televisions. However as the
PROs source their products from recycling yards, they are incentivised to expand into
any easily repairable &/ refurbishable small electronics, and profitable than recycling. It
is important to stress that the same organisations may receive the product after its
second use phase, and process it for recycling. Under Polluter pays Principles, the OEM
is still responsible for paying for this end of life treatment. (Environcom, 2021;
Freiesleben, 2021, #Elertur 2023)
Similar schemes have not been identified for residential heating appliances, some
appear to exists for air conditioners. Heating appliances are a tougher sell, given the
importance of fuel/heating economy. Heating appliances also require certifications to
42
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:53==
install, and installation fees are a significant part of the total cost. The reduced life span
and worse fuel economy, also makes second hand heating appliances a tough sell for
consumers. The use phase is also the most polluting phase of heating systems, which
also reduces the interest from sustainability minded consumers.
Some PROs are working towards component re­circulation. Elretur expressed interest in
achieving this in their new treatment facilities under construction (Freiesleben, 2021,
#Elertur 2023). Besides recirculating components to OEMs, they can also build up
regional networks of recyclers for specific components or develop component treatment
technologies. For example, UK Environcom has develop an automated compressor
recycling machine, drastically increasing the efficiency and effectiveness of compressor
recycling.
Scrap yards handling of heating systems
Scrap yards and scrap dealers are the most prevalent route of disposal for heating
systems, both heat pumps, gas boiler with and without water­tanks in our work. There
are two routes for disposal at scrap yards: either through the scrap dealer offering mixed
metal containers that are emptied at regular intervals/on demand or through direct drop
offs, either in bulk or just after installation.
Scrap yards are the preferred solution by many installer for multiple reasons: 1. Get
paid for the scrap material/not paying to get rid of it. 2. More convenient disposal. 3.
Ability to increase the value of the old eating systems if time allows for it.
All surveyed scrap yards, state that it is not worth their time to remove components or
materials, from heating systems and water tanks (#Scrap yards 2023). Current end of
life here appears to be straight to the shredder or exported as mixed metal waste for
further treatment with the goal of steel and iron recycling (#Scrap yards 2023
#Scrappers 2023, #CircHeat 2022).
The scrap dealers and yards ability to retrieve valuable components and either resell or
otherwise upcycle them, is inherently very difficult, as they cannot know if the products
have been stored or handled properly, and if any components are functional. As for
recycling yards, the large product diversity and the heterogeneity of their product stream
makes value recovery beyond material recycling very difficult too. However as shown
with some PROs in subsubsection 3.2.4, some refurbishment of some product
categories does occur. Prices with scrap yards and recyclers are negotiable and large
quantities are strongly favoured by them. It is also opaque what fractions are accepted
and exists, for whilst there are international standards, then as covered in the literature
review, it is can be hard to give scrap and specifically scrap metal a quality.
Some of Viessmanns dealing with scrap dealers and recyclers, consists of scraps from
manufacturing. They are all different steel grades, all of which are collected in separate
bins, stored in containers and sold in bulk, per alloy to recyclers and scrap yards. This
purity has inherent value, due to the certainty of the scraps composition ((#Ahlborn
2023/01/25, #Steel price 2023 ). Scrap yards and recyclers can be highly specialised
and for most mechanical or electronic components it is possible to find a company
specialising in recovering value from them, (including transformers, compressors,
capacitors etc.. ). All components poses their own challenges at end of life and in
recycling, and therefore benefits highly from specialisation (#Scrap yards 2023,
#Scrappers 2023, ScrapMonster, 2023).
Scrap dealers
There can be drawn a distinction between the scrap yards, buying large amounts of
materials and selling to recyclers ­ and the small/independent scrap dealers generally
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
43
==PAGE:54==
selling to various scrap yards. The independent or small scrap dealers practices vary a
lot more. Their service offerings and their general working conditions are are less clear.
To investigate what independent scrappers do, a small video study was done of publicly
available videos of scrappers explaining how they scrap gas condensing boilers, air
conditioners, water tanks and heat pumps. Generally gas condensing boilers take
anywhere between 30­90 minutes to scrap into almost entirely pure scrap fractions
(even breaking down pumps, motors and other components into their material fractions)
(#Scrappers 2023). The large variation depends on the techniques, tools and level of
disassembly. For some scrapper have buyers for transformers, electric motors and
compressors, making it more attractive for them not to break down these components.
Most report that they earn around minimum wage from scrapping these products.
Remember that this is without the economies of scale and specialised tools for them.
Combining some of the techniques observed in the videos, this process should be able
to be sped up. The scrappers wage for dismantling into scrap fractions also largely
depends on the product composition of the heating systems. For example, some boiler
OEMs having replaced many copper components with aluminium or steel components,
making it unviable for the scrappers ­ due to the significantly lower scrap value. Some
scrappers report being able to source these condensing boilers from: side of the road
(possibly a form of theft?), scrap yards who cannot dedicate the time to dismantle them,
end consumers at home renovation and finally from HVAC companies (#Scrappers
2023).
The scrappers observed here were all English speaking and from the US, Canada and
the UK. As the main constraining factor for their ability to dismantle and scrap these
products is dependent on their countries cost of living and wages in general. Of course
regulation plays a role too, as the scrappers technically need to be certified to handle it
(#Miele 2023).
Finally on the topic of independent and private scrappers. It should not be used as an
end of life management strategy. The working conditions are less regulated, and there is
no reporting of the size of efforts etc.. However it is also practically impossible to avoid
products being dismantled like this. There is a well documented problem with theft of
components and materials from discarded products. Some installers also experiences
theft in their mixed metal containers and storage areas (#Installers 2023/05/16)
3.2.5
Steel recycling and material recoverability
As mentioned in literature (Ohno et al., 2017 and Nakajima et al., 2013) it is indicated
that it is possible to improve the amount of functional elements in the recycled steel.
This is done by optimising the fractions to which a product is separated at its end of life.
If it were to be done in the heating industry, the strategic elements that should be
focused on to improve quality are the following (figure 3.7). Taking the Vitocal 111s
indoor unit as an example, one of the main interesting elements is copper (Cu). It is
found in large amount in the product at a component level, has a high scrap value, and
is hardly extractable from steel when the recycling process has started. This is why it is
a good reason to separate it before hand. Studies (Diener and Tillman, 2015 and
Yellishetty et al., 2011b) suggests that some alloying elements like Tin (Sn), Nickel (Ni),
Chromium (Cr) and Zinc (Zi) are hard to recover from steel alloys. Tin (Sn) and Nickel
(Ni) are both not metallurgically extractible from steel, as they are part of the steel
alloying elements and thus are already included in the steel matrix. Moreover, including
Chromium (Cr), they are the ones that have the highest value per mass fraction, which
makes it interesting to recover their functionality in the steel alloy to reduce production
44
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:55==
costs. The Zinc (Zn) is however, of lower value, but found in larger amount than the
three alloying elements aforementioned. To functionally recover these elements, it would
require to separate the materials that contain most of them in fractions that can be
recycled in a closed loop with the same alloy. The studies also indicate that functional
loss is more carbon intensive than mass loss when recycling.
Figure 3.7: Figure from situation analysis presentation on the recoverability of different
alloying elements in steel.
Today the two main routes for steel recycling are the Blast furnace­basic oxygen furnace
(BF­BOF) for primary steel and Electric arc furnace (EAF) for secondary steel (figure
3.8). Literature (see section 2.1.2) mentions that the primary route is more energy and
carbon intensive than the recycling one. However, it is said that the costs of producing
recycled steel is slightly higher. The other drawback is that recycled steel tend to be
used in construction industry for long products such as rebars, which suggests that the
quality of the steel is lower than the primary route.
Figure 3.8: Figure from situation analysis presentation on the products created by recy­
cling steel and its’ routes.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
45
==PAGE:56==
3.2.6
Take back systems today
Whilst most take back systems focus on smaller B2C products. From fashion items like
Lewis jeans, to small personal electronics like iPhones. Given the value chain, and
product characteristics these were not investigated, as the use cases were deemed too
different. There are some take back systems worth exploring, however, their scales are
rather unknown. In research the majority of take back systems are described as being
small or pilot stage (see subsection 2.1.3). But some do exists and they are worth
drawing inspiration from.
Mieles take back system for electronics components
Miele currently has a take back system in place, not for its’ entire products, but for
electronic components (besides normal extended producer responsibility requirements).
Especially for printed circuit boards (PCBs), micro controllers, displays and similar
products. This is largely driven by their promise to customers of offering spare parts for
minimum 10, but up to 15 years, from the end of production of that product series (Miele,
2023, #Miele 2023).
Predicting future demand for spare components for such long lived products is difficult,
and spinning up production of old electronics is very costly ­ and so is retrofitting new
components. This makes the discarded, but functional/refurbishable components quite
valuable for Miele. Some of these components, e.g., the PCB also experiences little
wear.
Taking back products also fits in line with Mieles dedication to sustainability. Mieles
focus on long lasting high quality products, also makes the components well suited for
recirculation. The components are well suited for refurbishment, as the white goods,
often also being replaced due to fashion/style changes, energy efficiency upgrades or
only a single part has failed.
Their take back system for components is so far limited to EU. Their main source of
components come from warranty claims, when their own technicians service products.
They currently do not get components from independent service companies. The issue
with getting them from independent service companies is it requires a special
certifications to remove components from products that are legally waste ­ such as a
broken washing machine (#Miele 2023).
They are working to establish a pathway of components from products EoL, from
companies, like PROs and scrap dealers, who handle the discarded white goods.
Technicians will write the serial number with a marker on the white goods. Participating
waste actors then have a positive list of products with interesting components. The large
visible model numbers makes targeted recovery of the components easy. The waste
actors are incentivised with around 10­20 EUR per component. Electronic components
are then refurbished at a central facility ­ and they do offer warranty on the replaced
parts. The high incentive, stresses the high value these components represent for Miele
(#Miele 2023).
Grundfos take back system for circulation pumps
Grundfos has a take back system for circulation pumps. HVAC technicians, gather them
in bins and deliver them in bulk at a wholesaler/supply­store. From interviews it also
appeared that Grundfos picked up pumps sometimes. Quite a lot of these pumps come
from heating systems, like gas condensing boilers. Not all HVAC companies participate
at all and no publicly disclosed capture rates or participation rates were found. In the
phone surveying of Danish installers, Grundfos country of origin, it appeared to be a
minority of installers participating. However not all were asked explicitly.
46
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:57==
The current goal of the take back system is to ensure the best possible material
recycling and creating socially inclusive jobs, for workers with social/health reasons
reducing their working capacity. The optimal recycling of the components in the pump
requires manual disassembly. This also reduces labour costs due to various
government programmes (grundfos, 2023, #CircHeat 2022).
Currently it does not appear that components are reused or refurbished, this is due to
the difficulty of achieving competitive performance with old electronics. Circulation
pumps are generally not easy to repair, and often replaced instead, so replacement
parts for them is not a large concern. The almost always on state nature of circulation
pumps also makes improving energy efficiency one of the most important aspects in
improving the total product life cycle emissions.
Wilo is also in the works to establish a similar take back system for their pumps.
However details on it are sparse. They mention recycling in their materials and note that
it takes proper handling to ensure optimal recycling. This take back system therefore
also seems to be dominated with a focus on effective recycling (Wilo, 2023).
Take back of heat pumps
Mitsubishi is collaborating with handling the old boilers and heating systems replaced by
select heat pump products in some regions. Here they are collaborating with regional
PROs, and only picking up heating systems in bulks of 10 or more. Mitsubishi, 2023. It
appears to be a recycling scheme, however details on the programme are sparse.
Other heating appliance manufacturers do appear to have begun implementing take
back systems. However it is unclear what type of take back systems they are ­ and if it is
’just’ collection and effective recycling through PROs. It is also unclear if they are
capturing the majority of the heating systems that are not ending up in WEEE streams
(#Recyclers 2023).
3.2.7
What should happen today
The current end of life of both heat pumps and gas condensing boilers, appears to a
large extent to be improper. The disposal should strictly speaking be through white
goods at recycling yards. However, as the products mainly consists of steel and iron and
limited electronics, it is generally disposed off as mixed metal. The scrap yards handling
these mixed metal containers from HVAC companies, say they do no further treatment
of the gas condensing boilers and the same appears to hold for heat pumps. All
surveyed scrap yards, did no further treatment, unless it was immediately available, e.g.,
removing insulation if it was very easily accessible (#CircHeat 2022, #Recyclers 2023).
The things may be sorted in similar groups of products at the scrap yard. Depending on
the scrap yard, these fractions are then shredded, and then sold on the scrap market.
Some scrap yards will export the mixed metal fraction, for steel recycling. If any further
component recovery or treatment of the products happens at these metal fraction buyers
is unclear. However mono­fractions of shredded metal can be sorted further with several
technologies, and multiple metals can be recovered from the melt as described in
subsection 3.2.5.
Alternatively the scrap yards could have fractions for gas boilers and heat pump and
ensure they were registered as white goods through PRO and EPR organisations.
However given that the products have low potential for upcycling through existing
channels this current waste route is economical for the stakeholders involved. Little time
is spent, and some value is recovered for all across the ’waste value chain’. Most
importantly, the products are treated as metal, and the bulk of these materials can be
recovered ­ albeit imperfectly. Installers avoid paying to dispose, the scrap yards profit
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
47
==PAGE:58==
off the metal contents and steel smelters have a source of steel, even if it is a dirty
fraction.
However what ends up in the mixed metal containers at installers does vary a bit. Some
installers do actively remove copper and some components. This is not extensive by
any means, in the regions we studied. Not all electronic components nor recoverable
components are recovered. There is a focus on the easy parts that can account for the
time spent. Installers only disclosed their experiences with gas condensing boilers as
this is vastly more normal for them to handle than heat pumps. The ones who did spend
time removing components focused on: circulation pump, copper, cables and
electronics. This process is stated to take 20­30 minutes for the installers (this has not
been verified through measurement nor observation) (#Survey 2023 b, #Installers
2023/05/16). The lower the cost of labour in the country the more feasible this becomes
­ and likely the more the components are broken down and the more is recovered.
Increasing scrap prices may also make this more prevalent. However, it would not be
ethically appropriate to send old products for scrapping in other countries for that
reason, at least not developing countries.
3.2.8
Interest from component suppliers
One of the drivers for beginning this project is that multiple component suppliers, OEMs,
have expressed interest in getting their components back. This was also previously
identified as a way of potentially making the take back system economically viable.
At the time of writing 4 OEMs have expressed interest in the potential of getting their
products back. Currently 3 circulation pump suppliers and the main supplier of heat
exchangers for their products. These address some of the environmental component
hotspots in the product too (#Tariq 2023).
What happens to the products that the OEMs get backs varies. However from the
publicly available information it appears to focus on best possible and effective
recycling. Whilst reuse and upcycling of components is possible, it is less applicable for
long lived products, like EoL heating systems. Many OEMs they may not have explored
the option, as their component generally is a small sub component of a larger product,
and the difficulty of extracting them is infeasible ­ but the more participating OEMs the
more the price can be split amongst the OEMs.
There is a lot of different small components. From temperature sensors, to heating coils,
to pumps etc.. Some of the OEMs that have expressed interest in their components, are
generally manufacturers of components that are generally replaced or upgraded to
improve a heating/climate systems performance. Both Alfa Laval and Grundfos
advertise with these benefits in B2C contexts. This makes it easier for them to source
these components, as some technicians will have removed them from the HS anyways,
reducing the overall work needed from their side. Manufacturers of invisible
components, like heating coils, sensors etc.. may have deemed it infeasible to source a
large enough homogeneous supply of old components to make it viable to eve
investigate take back systems and upcycling.
There could be a potential in investigating smaller, ’invisible’ components condition at
EoL and discussing their potential with OEMs. Many components may not be fit for use
in heating systems due to the focus on fuel efficiency in the use phase. But may be
usable in other product categories.
48
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:59==
3.3
System demarcation ­ scoping what will be affected by a
take back system?
A system demarcation is broadly speaking the scope of what the project investigates,
what the solution can utilise and what it will directly and indirectly impact. One of the
early thinking principles in designing a take back system for heating systems, was
avoiding making it more difficult to install or replace a heating system from Viessmann ­
compared to other brands. This has naturally constrained the solutions space to
focusing on the decommissioning phase, and not impacting the other product life cycle
phases. The solution space is also naturally divided in two: there is the large bulk of gas
condensing boilers being replaced now and the future steady state of replacing heat
pumps with heat pumps.
Heating systems are also generally out of mind for most customer types ­ they work in
the background, and consumers mostly don’t notice them working. For installers they
are also interested in making the replacement process seamless, and minimising active
involvement from the end customer. Due to the B2B2C value chain, it is important to
also cater to the installers preferences and making their operations easier, more
convenient and giving them opportunities to excel in their b2C relations.
3.3.1
The product life cycle perspective
This project only directly intervenes with the decommissioning and disposal phase but
has the potential to indirectly impact the entire life cycle of the heating systems. This can
be been seen as a grave to cradle scope. The grave being obsolete systems and the
cradle being use in new systems.
The reuse of components would directly impact the design and performance of the
heating systems. As heating and climate systems use­phase performance is by far the
most important phase from both a sales and environmental point of view, the disposal
phase cannot constrain this design phase needlessly. The long lifetime of heating
system products also inherently makes it very difficult if not impossible, to predict and
make these long term gambits. All in all this makes the decommissioning and disposal
phases the only life cycle steps to directly intervene in for gas condensing boilers. But
leanings from gas systems disposal can be transferred into product design of heat
pumps to make the take back more performant. It also drives changes in the
sustainability initiatives portfolio as take back system is part of the sustainability strategy
to improve circular economy. The system demarcation is shown in the figure below
Figure 3.9.
Figure 3.9: The system demarcation illustrated as a product life cycle diagram with flows.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
49
==PAGE:60==
3.3.2
Impact of the system demarcation and scoping
The main impact of the scoping is that we cannot alter the products design. But we are
able to design solutions which implement services that alter the practices around
existing heating systems. But the organisation(s) can learn from taking back old
products to change design practices of future products ­ the lead time for these changes
being around 15 years. This make the quantification of these design changes very
uncertain. The materials or components may be reused or remanufactured, but may not
be used in heating systems specifically.
The environment is however the entire product life cycle, as a lot of relevant regulation
and practice can ’accumulate’ in the product at end of life. Regulation on disposal of
hazardous substances, critical materials etc. can change during the products life time
and may be retroactive/applicable for products being disposed.
3.4
Quantitative assessment of baseline
In this section the current handling of heating systems will be quantified, with the goal of
being able to compare it to new take back systems.
3.4.1
The interfaces between the different simulation methods
One issue with using different simulation methodologies, is defining a consistent
interface between them, so the cumulative analysis makes sense ­ and all data is
transferred between the models The interface between the DES and LCA model was
chosen to be the ’final’ disposal ­ henceforth endpoint. In the baseline simulation this
would either be the scrap yard, recycling yard or PRO after treatment.
The main outputs of the DES model for an endpoint is the total masses, time spent (by
each actor), ton kilometres for each end point, amount of components and heating
systems. More is tracked, such as the number of shipments, number of heating system
types etc..
The input to the DES model is then the wrangled/transformed data of heating systems.
This allows us to remove components and send them to another disposal or fraction,
and have all the correct masses reflecting in every endpoint. This is also illustrated in
Figure 1.3 detailing our analysis approach.
3.4.2
Creating data models of heating systems
The heating systems used
All of the analysis takes basis in 3 different heating
systems, and 4 different products. One very successful, and still in production gas
condensing boiler: the Vitodens 100w. Ideally one or more old, 10­20 years old product
would have been used to model the gas condensing boilers. But due to the difficulty of
finding a representative BOM or similar data for the old products, the Vitodens 100w was
chosen. According to our external thesis supervisor, there has not been large changes
in the technology and material compositions ­ so from an EoL focused assessment, it is
a good approximation (#Tariq 2023).
Two successful heat pump systems were also analysed. The heat pump systems
consists of both an indoor and an outdoor unit. The heat pumps analysed are: Vitocal
100s and the Vitocal 111s with a water tank. The outdoor unit, Vitocal 1XXs is
manufactured and designed by a 3rd party. Both system can use the same outdoor unit.
These products are also still in production and were chosen for the same reason as the
boiler (#Tariq 2023).
A heat pump system therefore yields two products when replaced, whilst a gas
condensing boiler system yields one. Gas condensing boiler systems, can also be used
50
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:61==
in combination with an external watertank/waterheater, but this was not considered in
this assessment. The Vitocal 111s however has an integrated watertank. One key issue
with modelling the heating systems using multiple different modelling techniques, was
finding the right balance between the amount of data outputs between the models, and
being able to easily compare the different heating systems and setups. The BOMs are
extremely detailed, and consists of components, which often consists of multiple
materials. Much is also included in BOMs which is not present at the products EoL.
Namely: packaging, shipping materials, spare parts, optional installation parts and the
like (#BOM 2023).
Whilst it would be technically feasible to input the entire BOM into the DES model, it
would be hard for us as modellers to wrangle the amount of data and make sense of the
output. A lot of the inputs would also be largely meaningless or uninteresting for the
analysis. For example, all nuts and bolts, are included, but functionally can be largely
seen as a part of the chassis ­ or just be viewed as steel. The BOMs do try to represent
how things are assembled, which could be interesting for modelling disassembly.
However the assembly levels are hierarchical, and do not represent all the physical
connections. E.g., the burning chamber is mounted on the back­plate, and can therefore
be seen as an sub­assembly of the chassis ­ but is also connected with pipes to other
components. Simply put, the BOMs assembly levels are used to model the
manufacturing process, and are ill suited to model the potential for disassembly. Here a
better representation would be a graph, illustrating all of the physical connections
between components ­ however this would require CAD models and would be
technically even more difficult to handle in DES.
Bom extraction
The BOMs were also created using different methodologies and
material nomenclature: some using internal company lingo, some German and some
using international standard material IDs/names. E.g., some components are specified
as being made of ’stainless steel’ and others ”1.4301”. This difference in specificity limits
the accuracy and complicates creating a highly accurate MFA and LCA analysis.
Important components such as pumps etc. are supplied by OEMs, and therefore had no
details on their material composition in the BOMs. These are general difficulties in
modelling products quantitatively. The large array of materials, coatings, treatments etc.
are hard to completely specify and standard formats for it are lacking. This results in a
lot of data wrangling, to make it fit ones use case (#Tariq 2023). Which is also the case
here.
Therefore it was elected to ’wrangle’ the BOMs extensively into more meaningful and
simple material and component categories, to fit for our analysis. In LCA contexts this
could be viewed as a simplified LCI (life cycle inventory).
The components in the BOMs were split into both materials and main components. The
standardisation of materials allowed to split the aggregated components into material
distributions too. This allowed for better modelling the material impacts and changes of
removing components.
Both the pure material and component views, account for entire mass as far as possible.
Components
The component categories are as follows: 1. Blowers (either the gas
blower or the fan), 2. Pumps, 3. Expansion vessel, 4. Piping, 5. Cables, 6. Chassis,
7. Electronics, 8. Insulation, 9. Heat exchanger, 10. (Other) Components.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
51
==PAGE:62==
The final category acting as a miscellaneous bucket, for things not fitting in the other
categories. More component categories were used in the early phases, but the added
complexity and lack of usage in subsequent analysis made it redundant, and they were
lumped into the components category.
Materials
The material categories are as follows 1. Steel, stainless; 2. Steel, alloy;
3. Steel, non alloy; 4. Steel, galvanised; 5. Aluminium, 6. Copper, 7. Polymers,
8. Rubber, 9. Glass fiber, 10. Electronics, 11. Paper, 12. Miscellaneous.
Steel was broken into different categories as it makes up the bulk of the mass and a
large part of the environmental impacts of the products.
A few notes on the BOM extraction process
The BOM dataset were quite
messy/dirty, with multiple data issues. It required quite extensive sanitation. Multiple
issues like: missing values, inconsistent letter casing, mixed data types, redundant
components, components with no quantity or with missing weight, trailing spaces, and
more. The redundant components were generally components with multiple different
suppliers, where one was the current. Some components were missing material
designations, generally these represented a very small amount of the mass. For the few
exceptions it was usually clear by its’ position/name what material it should be
designated as ­ so this was manually corrected. Some components also had no
quantity, these were removed. After sanitation the products could be grouped according
to their assemblies at different levels (#BOM 2023).
To split the data into different materials/components, the products were grouped
according to material name/component name, this was done using string
searching/matching. By adding component names to each category, and exhaustively
going through until all components had a category. This got faster for each BOM, as a
list of components names was built up for each category, and the search functions got
refined. This is more difficult in practice as there are overlapping names and sub­strings
one can search for which needs to be filtered out. After the two groupings were
achieved, the components groups could be reclassified as materials. E.g., as all
components in a component group now also had a corresponding material designation.
The same was done for materials, but was sped up by the fewer unique values. A series
of plots are available in the appendix, section A.4, illustrating the masses of
components, materials and the position number (also called assembly numbers, which
illustrate how deeply embedded materials are). The python code is available in an
external appendix as python files section A.4.
The BOM extraction process is outlined with a few examples from the Vitodens 100 in
Figure 3.10:
52
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:63==
Figure 3.10: BOM extraction process.
Afterwards, some components with very simplistic material designations, e.g., the pump,
were expanded upon using data from ecoinvent ­ scaled using the total mass of the
pumps as specified in the BOM. This was done for the circulation pumps, as they were
logged as being only made of glass­fiber­reinforced polyamide in the BOM.
Below select plots are shown from some products, Figure 3.11, show the material
distribution of different components & materials. It clearly shows that few components
make up the bulk of the mass for most categories. The assembly plots show that a lot of
the materials are not that ’embedded’ in the product. However some exceptions are
present, like the electronics.
