3.4.3
DES of baseline
This section will briefly and concisely follow the Rosetti framework as outlined in the
literature review. The following section assumes some familiarity with DES & agent
based modelling nomenclature.
In the Discrete event simulation appendix a lot more intricate details on how different
54
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:65==
agents and functions have been implemented. The model is implemented in Anylogic,
and as with any simulation framework, it is best explored in depth in the software.
Phase 1 ­ problem formulation
As outlined in the introduction of this sub­chapter, the goal is to accurately simulate the
ton kilometres, time spent, materials, components and heating systems ending up at the
different waste disposal endpoints. In the baseline this is: at the scrap yard and a PRO.
There are several routes as show in the figure below.
As the goal is not to optimise current waste management in the baseline system, a lot of
complexity usually included in discrete event simulations, pretty much all seizing of
agents can be omitted. This is fine as we do not care about storage times and similar.
To abstract and simplify the analysis even more, it was chosen to use a single flow to
represent installer companies and vary the transport distances to reflect the regional
differences between installer companies. The alternative of trying to model different
company setups would lead to a lot of complexity that would require a lot more data and
ultimately result in a lot of uncertainty ­ with questionable gains. The model is shown as
a simple flowchart/activity flow diagram in Figure 3.12.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
55
==PAGE:66==
Figure 3.12: simplified activity flow diagram of the baseline simulation.
Parameters
A full overview of the parameters, is available in section A.3. A lot of
parameters are shared between the Baseline and TOBE models.
Data gathering
The data for the baseline behaviour comes from phone surveys in Denmark &
Switzerland, and 2 surveys for Germany. However the low amount of respondents of the
Germany surveys made it necessary to gather transport distances from google maps too
(#Survey 2023 a). The procedure here was to select HVAC companies identified in
56
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:67==
sending surveys out and finding local scrap yards/recycling yards in the area and noting
down the distance. This was not done extensively, as the transport distances are largely
unavoidable and from an environmental impact point of view insignificant. But the
transport data estimates are of very low quality.
Phase 2 ­ Simulation model building
In this section a brief overview of the main model building decisions is offered. A full
overview of all the data and distributions and data used in the models is available in the
DES appendix, section A.3.
Bulking mixed metal
All of the containers in the baseline system are mixed metal
containers and from phone surveys it was identified that anywhere between 3­10 heating
systems may be replaced before the container is emptied. There is generally room for
many more heating systems, especially if the mixed metal container is stuffed properly.
However in reality, the mixed metal container is also filled with all other metal waste the
HVAC company gets rid off. Like radiators, piping, air ducts etc.. This is entirely
dependent on the size of the HVAC company and the mix of their business operations.
As the main consequence of this is the amount of tonne kilometres that different heating
systems are transported, this has been simplified to using the average amount of heating
systems that can fit in the 10 ft container, as also used in the TOBE logistics modelling.
It is worth noting that the heating systems have very different sizes, a better approach
would be to model the volume and subtract it directly or apply a stuffing algorithm.
Transport functions
The transport distances are drawn from triangular distributions,
as it was hard to get enough data to make more representative distributions. The source
of the minimum, average and maximum values are described in the data gathering
section. The average transport speed is assumed to be 60 km/h, for this simulation. The
actor conducting the transport is also noted, including the time spent transporting the
systems.
The transport distances are stored on the agent passing through the system, as they
need to allocated for the amount of mass that is being transported. When splitting up the
heating system the previous transport is transferred to the new agent representing the
components. When the agent enters an endpoint, its’ cumulative transport distance is
multiplied by it’s weight and converted to ton. km.
Using a single flow to simulate the installer companies
A single flow was used to
simulate all the installer companies. This was done as it is technically much simpler than
simulating a population of different installer companies. By varying the transport
distances, heating systems and practices, the same average picture was achieved.
Phase 3 ­ Experimental design and analysis
Strictly speaking, no experiments were conducted for the baseline. However many
sanity checks were created to verify the baseline models integrity. This was done by
using parameters to limit the flow to a single route through the model flow. afterwards the
data was manually inspected and it was internally discussed if the results were realistic.
For the baseline, based on the gathered data, a best estimate setup for the parameters
was created. This best estimate setup was run in 3 different configurations: 1. 50/50 mix
of heat pumps and boilers. 2. 100% boilers and 3. 100% heat pumps. To allow for direct
comparison, each flow through the waste management system was also evaluated, i.e.,
only direct home pickup by scrap dealer, only direct to recycling yard etc..
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
57
==PAGE:68==
Results
The main findings from the DES baseline are: 1. Heat pumps take slightly
longer for any stakeholder to handle. 2. Any kind of bulking reduces the time spent per
heating system. 3. Bulk transport of heating systems to recycling centre takes longer
and overall takes more time for involved stakeholders. 4. Kerbside collection of heating
systems by scrap dealers is a significant cost. 5. Direct to recycling yard can surprisingly
fast for installers.
(a) Comparison of waste routes ton km &
value of actors time.
(b) Comparison of waste routes, time spent
by actors.
(c) Baseline best estimate of time and actors
times value.
(d) Baseline best estimate of time and ton
km.
Figure 3.13: Main graphs of baseline system.
Phase 4 ­ Evaluation and iteration
The primary method for verifying that the output data was correct and realistic, was to
look at the average amount of material/transport/time per heating system in an endpoint.
Due to using a student version of Anylogic, it was limited to 50000 agents, in practice
this limited the models to evaluate roughly 3000­5000 heating systems depending on
how disassembly and bulking happened in the flow, as both actions require the model to
create new dynamic agents at these point, counting towards this limit (Anylogic, 2023).
However results were very stable and consistent.
3.4.4
Discussion of the baseline DES model
The baseline model is very simple, as the current handling largely is very simple. Late in
the project it was identified that some small portion of installers might remove some
components (subsection 3.2.4, #Installers 2023/05/16). As this is kind of analogous to
the incentivised recovery, it was elected not to add this to the model. However the focus
of these installers is to remove components which are valuable as scrap (especially
copper), or for other take back systems (currently pumps). More work should be put into
mapping the prevalence of this and how this limits the viability of accepting heating
systems from some waste streams. There is also some reselling on a secondary
market, but this is not considered as this project focuses on the EoL not EoU cycles.
Ideally a population of installation companies had been modelled, with different
attributes and preferences, to better compare the different installers. However this has
58
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:69==
been approximated by changing the ’same’ installer companies behaviour according to
the data gathered.
The data used is of quite low quality, especially the transport estimates which are very
regionally dependent and in the baseline make up the bulk of the time. However a lot of
that will incur no matter what, i.e., installers going too and from clients.
3.4.5
MFA ­ where do the atoms end up?
Before they at end of life and have the chance to be recycled, the materials forego
extraction and production, which is an intricate system with multiple actors and routes.
The goal of the MFA is to determine how the main constituent of heat pumps, steel,
flows in its supply chain. It helps understanding the importance of the two main
production routes. The BF­BOF for the primary steel, and the EAF for the secondary.
Given the fact that both routes tend to use both pig iron and scrap in their process, the
system is not a simple flow.
There are evidences that products with long lifespan have a large impact on the ability of
providing scrap for recycling stream, and the MFA should show the extent of that effect.
The modelling method was following the suggested structure of Brunner and
Rechberger, 2004. The literature review of section 2.1.2 on steel production route was
used in the modelling of the flows and retrieving data. Therefore, it captures the global
market condition and not the actual flows that would be specific to the Viessmann’s case
in Germany. The processes are quantified based on transfer coefficient found in
literature and the scale of the flows are sized to Viessmann’s material flows. It is not
possible to quantify every parameters with good data sources. When data is not
available, sources from relevant other material flows and systems are used, and in the
last resort qualified estimates are made. The software used is STAN 2.7.101 (Cencic
and Rechberger, 2008) and it supports data reconciliation. It allows to integrate
uncertainties and missing data and still being able to propagate results along the flows
with sufficient confidence (Cencic, 2016).
The steel cycle
BF­BOF and EAF are the two most important flows according to literature (section 2.1.2,
Su et al., 2022) respectively for the primary production and for the secondary
production. Figure 3.14 shows the steps that steel crosses in Viessmann products
during its life cycle.
1. Extraction: Harvey, 2021 defines the steel flow by starting with with the extraction
of Pig Iron 92% of which is distributed to BF­BOF and the 7% rest goes to direct
iron reduction for EAF route. It is assumed to have a 1% loss in the process.
2. BF­BOF or DRI­EAF: Harvey, 2021 shows that 39% of the steel is lost in the blast
furnace process and the rest either goes to the basic oxygen furnace (61%). They
also give the metallisation rate for the Direct reduction of iron as 96% (going to
EAF) leaving the remaining 4% as losses. In addition, the BOF is said to have 5%
loss, and 9% being recycled as slag. The remaining 86% of the steel is the BOF
production efficiency that goes to treatment.
3. Treatment: The steel treatment is the first steps of forming steel into plates, bars
and going through galvanisation. The BOMs (#BOM 2023) provide the suppliers
losses during the manufacturing and the values are used both for the treatment
and the manufacturing of the parts. It is said to have around 14% losses in the
process in average. Leaving 12% of the steel being sent to the next step to the
OEMs and 74% to Viessmann after treatment.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
59
==PAGE:70==
4. OEMs & Viessmann manufacturing: The manufacturing process also has 14%
losses and the 86% remaining go to the next step. Namely, from OEMs to
Viessmann or from Viessmann to the use phase. It was said (#Ahlborn
2023/01/25) that most of the losses from the Viessmann’s manufacturing process
are being recycled as high quality scrap. These pre­consumer scraps are already
separated on an alloy basis when created, as they come from production lines,
where machines produce large amount of the same pieces. Therefore, they are
sold as pure alloy fractions, and the scrap ends up as high quality scrap when
being recycled.
5. Use: Once in the use phase, the products will remain used for about 10 to 20
years, 20 years being selected in the simulation for a high estimate. This means
that the amount of post consumer scrap produced for one heating system is
relative to the amount of products installed 20 years ago.
(German Heatpump Association, 2023) reports that for the year 2002, there were
less than 13 thousand heat pumps installed in Germany. This is assumed to be the
scrap supply in 2022 and is about 4.6% of the 2022 installations. This means that
the equivalent of 4.6% of the 94.1 kg of steel present in the heat pump would enter
the waste management route for every heat pump installed in 2022. It was also
assumed that around 10% of the heating systems would not end up in the waste
management stream by that time.
6. Waste management: Gauffin et al., 2016 finds that waste collection losses for
steel products would be around 7%, which means a 93% collection rate.
7. Separation: Su et al., 2022 shows that 80% of the scrap collected end up in low
quality route through the BF­BOF to cool the primary steel production. the
remaining 20% are high quality scrap that goes through EAF route.
8. Recycling: Rem et al., 2012 finds that post­consumer scrap treatment has an
efficiency of around 70%.
The MFA was built with all the above transfer coefficients and by inserting the 94.1 kg of
steel of the considered heating system. In this case it is the weight of the steel in the
Vitocal 100s with its outdoor unit. The results and missing data gets automatically
calculated and reconciled if needed.
Figure 3.14 indicates that the secondary steel production from heat pumps can only
supply around 10% of the total material needed for the current production level (see
appendix A.1, table A.2). The main reason is the fact that post consumer waste equals
to the amount of heat pumps installed 20 years ago when the demand was much lower.
This means that improving the sustainability aspects by increasing the steel recycling is
limited to the accessible waste. In this case, if more environmental benefits are sought,
one should turn to other upcycling options presented in section 2.1.2.
This is true when we assess the system as a closed loop. However, to achieve higher
recycled content in the products, secondary steel could be supplied by other scrap
sources. This would have consequences in the secondary steel market that are not
assessed here. Experts would say that if the secondary steel market is constrained
(which is likely the case as the supply is limited) using more recycled steel would result
in other companies to turn to primary steel as less secondary steel is available. Thus,
there would be no net environmental benefits of using more recycled steel. But the
reality is hard to determine and more research should be made in this specific subject to
conclude anything.
60
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:71==
Figure 3.14: Results of the MFA of steel for the baseline
In this analysis, only the heat pump production was considered. However, as a large
amount of boilers is currently decommissioned it could be taken in account that some
alloys could be used as recycled steel in new heat pumps. However, the extent to which
it would be true is nor assessed here.
The evolution of the system by 2030 and 2050
IEA, 2022c predicts that the amount of installation of heat pumps would rise up to more
than 6.7 million products in 2030 for the European Union. Allocating that number to
Germany using the population share between EU and Germany (PopulationPyramid,
2023 and calculations can be seen in appendix A.1, table A.2) gives that more than 750
thousands heat pumps would be installed. This number is in line with
German Heatpump Association, 2023 market studies. In this study, it is also mentioned
that the total amount of heat pumps in use would reach 16.5 million by 2045. They
suggest that the growth rate would peak in 2030 and stay at a steady state afterwards.
An approximation of that state of the market evolution was calculated in appendix (A.1).
Figures 3.15 and 3.16 show that the effect of the long lifespan of the products is still
visible until 2030 when the growth rate is still high. The secondary steel supply from
heat pumps scrap for 2030 only increases to 12%, compared to the number of sales.
The later is multiplied by almost 13 between 2010 and 2030. However, when the growth
rate lowers and the sales approaches a plateau by 2050, the scrap supply can provide
up to 53% of the total needed steel. This comes with the fact that the sales is almost
identical to the 2030 sales (appendix A.1, table A.1). It indicates that as long as the
heating market has not reached a peak, there will be a limited amount of steel supplied
by the old products which can be repurposed to cut down primary steel production.
However, it also suggests that, as said in the literature (Harvey, 2021), even with a high
recovery rate and a steady state, the production of primary material is still needed to
make up for the material lost in the process.

3.4.6
LCA of baseline
How to use LCA in the context
As said in section 2.1.5, Life Cycle Assessment is a good tool to use in early design
phases. Simplified­LCA is an approach that can help making design decisions without
making a full fledged and standardised assessment. Therefore, this LCA follows the ISO
14040 as much as possible using the Life Cycle Assessment Theory and Practices
Handbook (Hauschild et al., 2018) but is not entirely ISO compliant. Not all steps have
been followed closely and this means that this is not an official LCA for Viessmann. The
method was adapted to better match the needs and constraints of the thesis and the
results should not be taken outside of this context.
Defining the goal of the LCA
The goal of using LCA in the context of take back systems is to identify the main
materials and components contribution to environmental impacts. These hotspots are
defined in the production and disposal phases for gas boilers and heat pumps. The
second intention is to evaluate the improvement potentials of the baseline scenario and
compare it to the scenarios proposed as solutions. The use phase is excluded from the
analysis which consists of one of the main limitations. It is said by Famiglietti et al., 2021
that the use phase is the main environmental contributor with around 97% of the CO2
emissions for gas boilers, with potential of reduction of around 20% for heat pumps.
However, in this case the goal is to have a circular economy angle and understand the
role of different end of life options on the material contribution to environmental impact.
Another limitation would be the access to data, which means that the life cycle inventory
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
63
==PAGE:74==
(LCI) has been derived from available BOMs that were not necessarily complete nor
intended for deriving component compositions. Moreover, the materials have also been
simplified in larger groups rather than considering every alloy nuances.
The study is meant to help inform about the consequences of decisions based on
structural changes such as the implementation of a take back system. Therefore, it is
targeted towards the sustainability team at Viessmann. In addition, it is used to shape a
decision support tool that would help guiding non­practitioners to make decisions with a
sustainability aspect.
Defining the scope of the LCA
The results of the LCA presented in the thesis are the LCI and life cycle impact analysis
(LCIA) along with the interpretation of the results for the different take back scenarios.
The system assessed is the end of life baseline and different take back scenarios of the
Vitodens 100w, Vitocal 100s and Vitocal 111s with their outdoor units. The function of
the system is to safely dispose of the materials of the heating systems, and to produce
new components from the waste to be used in similar functions. This can be achieved
by recycling or upcycling the materials and components.
Define the functional unit to unify comparison
The goal is to describe the system in a way that makes the baseline comparable with the
solution options. For every scenario, the functional unit is: “Produce material from the
end of life of one heating system with enough quality for a similar use case in Germany”.
The input of the model is the quantity of material for one heating system and the output
is the same quantity of recycled and/or upcycled material. Given the simplified nature of
the assessment, there are no secondary functions taken in account in the modelling of
the system and a mix of attributional and consequential modelling were used.
The system boundaries are represented in figure 3.17 and include the material
extraction, the material and product manufacturing, the decommissioning process of the
product with the transport and the treatment of the materials, and the final disposal with
relevant recycling and upcycling processes.
Figure 3.17: The system boundaries showing the processes taken in account in the LCA
The data for the amount of materials used come from the available BOMs provided by
Viessmann. However, the detail of their emissions and processes are from the ecoinvent
3.7 database made available by DTU. This can impact how the result compare with the
official Viessmann LCAs. The material origin is straight forward and they were all
modelled as global market average materials. Some of the processes such as
decommissioning and recycling were defined as European. As the products are
expected to have been manufactured 20 years before their decommissioning, their
64
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:75==
possibly is a slight differences in the data compared to the actual values. The same
applies to the technological representativeness as processes are mainly the average
global, it is expected that the technology used have slight differences compared to the
reality.
The impact assessment methods
The main indicator for the impact assessment is the global warming at midpoint, which is
showing CO2 equivalent emissions. However, all impacts are calculated and checked
whether there are indicators that have an increased impact when global warming
decreases to avoid burden shifting. The method used is ReCiPe Midpoint H 2016 as the
main indicator and then compared with other methods to assess the variability and
uncertainty.
The method to model the system was iterative. The first versions were modelled to get a
sense of scales and to perform sensitivity analysis in order to help prioritising the data
collection efforts.
Recycling approach
The recycling process was approached using the EoLR described in section 2.1.5. The
avoided material is the raw material production needed for the treatment and melting of
the metals. Pig Iron, aluminium oxide and copper concentrate production is avoided
when recycling respectively steel, aluminium and copper materials. The recycling
process was derived from the ecoinvent database by taking the material production
processes from the melting phase. The upcycling approach was performed by avoiding
the entire material process as there is no need of remelting and treating again the
components at the material level. As found in section 2.1.2, Ziout et al., 2022 defines the
remanufacturing process of air conditioning equipment as follows. It starts with
disassembly using electric tools of 100 Watt power. It continues with cleaning the
disassembled parts pressurised air and steam where they calculate 0.07345 MJ of
energy for it. They assume the energy for reassembly to be around 0.001 MJ with
experts judgements. These values were used in the remanufacturing process for the
LCA as the best estimate that could be made. Even though the remanufactured product
in the study is not a heating system, it is relatively functionally similar. It is also said in
the remanufacturing community (Borg Automotive Reman, 2023 and
Golisano Institute for Sustainability, 2020) that around 90% of the material is saved when
remanufacturing. Given that there were no studies found on the subject, it could be the
only best estimate and it might vary a lot depending on the component that get upcycled.
In this sense, the remanufactured is avoiding 90% of what a new component would use
as materials, with the 10% remaining being new material added to compensate the loss.
One unit of heating system is defined as one gas boiler or one heat pump indoor and
outdoor devices. Following this principle, three mixes of heating systems from the DES
were used to describe the potential combination of the products at end of life.
Firstly, mix (1) shows a potential best estimate of half step between today and a future
based on a majority of heat pumps. The shares are 50% Vitodens, 25% Vitocal 100s,
25% Vitocal 111s, and 50% Vitocal 1xxs.
The second one, mix (2), represents the overwhelming presence of boilers that are
decommissioned today with 100% of Vitodens 100w.
The third mix (3) taken in account is a heat pump only composition, that would happen
when all the fossil fuel boilers have been decommissioned. The proportions are 50%
Vitocal 100s, 50% Vitocal 111s, and 100% Vitocal 1xxs.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
65
==PAGE:76==
Figure 3.18 describes the process flow of the baseline that was prefered for the
comparisons, namely the mix number 1 of the list above. The detailed LCI is to be found
in Appendix A.2, table A.6. The materials are a simplified version of what was available
on the BOMs to match the available materials in ecoinvent database (see appendix A.2,
table A.3 for details). Doing so integrates more uncertainty but the order of magnitude of
the results would remain the same and allow the comparisons.
The amount of materials in each component comes from the BOMs data extraction (see
section 3.4.2). The pumps and some of the blowers had no material fractions available
and thus, the circulation pumps from ecoinvent database was used as a replacement.
The manufacturing processes are a combination of the ecoinvent material production
processes and the metal working ecoinvent processes for steel, aluminium and copper.
The polymers are divided in two groups, in the one hand, the one already formed in
shape or including forming processes in the database. In the other hand, the polymers
that were only defined as raw materials and to which was added a average injection
moulding process. As little information on precise manufacturing process was available,
the material working processes were adjusted to assume that processing 1kg of material
corresponded to a standard working process for 1kg. More details on the processes can
be found in appendix A.2.
The removal process consists of a building hall divided by 1 ∗109 to account for all the
other processes happening in a building. It also has a electric tool for unscrewing and
other activities for 3 hours. However these numbers are the same in all other scenarios
so the precision of the values are of low importance and are pure assumptions. The
power for the electric tools used for the heating system removal and separation come
from the suggested power consumption in Ziout et al., 2022 section 2.1.2. However, a
unit mistake was introduced in the LCA model and the power consumption were scaled
to kWh instead of Wh. This has little influence on the results, but the given removal
process should actually be 10 time lower.
The transport process is defined by an average ”unspecified truck size and consumption
efficiency”. This is because the first iterations showed that transport has insignificant
impact on the overall results and thus there is no need of more precision. The ton.km
values used for the process are taken from the DES results.
More separation and shredding equipment energy consumption were found through
literature and equipment references found in appendix (A.2, table A.6). The activities
taken in account here are shredding the entire heating system and separating the metal
fractions using electromagnetic technologies. This allows to separate ferrous and
non­ferrous metals. Here the copper is also considered to be separated as a best case
scenario, but is not necessarily the case in reality (Harvey, 2021). All polymers are
considered as polymer waste using the average ecoinvent process for it. The remains
are going to an average municipal solid waste management which uses a mix of
landfilling and incineration processes.
For all the aforementioned decommissioning and disposal processes, the times, ton.km
and amount of recycled materials were defined using the DES results in the LCI in
appendix A.2.
66
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:77==
Figure 3.18: Baseline system flow chart for LCA for the heating system mix 1
Figure 3.19 shows that depending on the mix of boilers and heat pumps, the baseline
impact varies. Given the fact that the heat pumps have more impactful materials it is
clear that by increasing the amount of heat pumps in the mix, the footprint follows. It is
also due to the fact that the average heat pump taken in account has more mass but it is
a lesser important factor as shown in figure 3.20. Indeed, the CO2 emission are not
correlated to the mass but are growing faster. This is given by the trend line slopes
(617.65 for CO2 and 70.983 for mass). This confirms the fact that heat pumps have
more carbon intensive materials.

contributors in mass as well. The highest impact score on per kg basis is the electronics.
It also indicates that steel recycling has the highest impact reduction in the baseline.

==PAGE:79==
Figure 3.21: Materials contribution to global warming of Vitodens 100w in the baseline
scenario
Interestingly, when plotting the materials by component mass, it becomes clear that
there are a few components that have large masses and thus, if targeted, could have a
large impact reduction potential. Vitocal products scatter plot show the same and are
displayed in appendix A.4.1.
As there are potential in targeting certain components it is necessary to understand
which components or family of components contribute the most to the carbon footprint of
the products. The chassis, heat exchangers and expansion vessels are the three
highest contributors to the Vitodens 100w emissions (see figure 3.22).
For the Vitocal 100s it is still the chassis and the heat exchangers that have the highest
impact, in addition to the electronics. The same holds for the Vitocal 111s with the water
tank in complement (see figures ?? for Vitocal 100s and 3.24 for Vitocal 111s)

Figure 3.24: Components contribution to global warming of Vitocal 111s in the baseline

LCA baseline findings
It is shown that there are a few component families that make up for the largest part of
the global warming impact. However these families, especially chassis and electronics,
are likely to have a large variety of suppliers. Moreover, as mentioned by #Tariq
2023/04/11, the products are ill suited for reusing pcbs. The OEMs of these components
are assumed to be hardly interested to take them back. This is why the scope should be
expanded to pumps, blowers, expansion vessels, heat exchangers and hydroblocks.
Even though they contribute less to global warming than chassis and electronics, they
have the potential of driving OEMs interest to upcycle them. The piping, mainly made
out of copper also has an important contribution. At the same time, copper is the
material that has the best scrap value which remains approximately the compared to
primary material.
Here we can also see that the recycling has a limited impact reduction of around 10% of
the total positive impact. One of the reason is the fact that heating systems are
discarded mainly as mixed metal. Even though the effort to shred and separate the
material fractions are insignificant compared to the material impact at the production, it
lowers the quality of the recycling. The recycled aluminium, steel and copper would in
fact only avoid the lower grades of the same materials as their composition is hard to
know. Taking the case of steel as an example, most of it (80%, see section 3.4.5) would
end in low quality scrap route that goes through the primary productions to cool the
melted steel. Even when the scrap follows the recycling route, most of the steel ends up
in lower quality functions such as construction rebars (Harvey, 2021). A better end of life
would occur if better information was given to scrap dealers concerning the metal alloy
and composition as said in section 2.1.2. It would also yield improvements if
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
71
==PAGE:82==
components were upcycled.
The last option would be to use less carbon intensive materials and focus in avoiding
high emissions steel, aluminium and copper, or reducing the amount of electronics.
However, changing materials should not affect the use phase efficiency as it is the actual
main emission source, and this goes beyond the thesis scope.
3.5
Cost analysis
In this section the main costs of actors affected by the implementation of a take back
system will be briefly estimated.
Operations costs
In the discrete event simulation, for all actions (unloading, loading and transporting), it
was logged what actor did it and how long it took. At the endpoints it was then
aggregated along with the other data. This allowed us to estimate the time­value per
heating system for each flow. Here the installers wage, time value was assumed to be
24.63 €/hour (“SalaryExpert”, 2023). The scrap dealers wage was assumed to be the
same as recycling workers: 17.63 €/hour (“SalaryExpert”, 2023).
These costs are shown in the baseline result figures: Figure 3.13
3.5.1
The value of scrap metal
As discussed in the situation analysis of the current waste management and installer
preferences, the main driver for discarding of waste through mixed metal container is not
the earnings, but the lack of paying to dispose and convenience. For some installers
there might be interest in increasing the scrap value but this is limited.
The value of heating systems at EoL
Products have vastly different values as scrap depending on the quality of the scrap
volume and the buyer. Using publicly available scrap prices for the standard scrap
fractions (see appendix A.2, table A.4), we can estimate the heating systems scrap
value, without doing anything, dismantling them completely, and finally complete
dismantling without the COI. All perspectives are interesting as the COIs may be unfit for
OEMs to take back, leaving the scrap value as the only option. The values below do not
take into account selling some components as components for scrap. Some dealers will
buy transformers, compressors and other similar common components, regardless of
state ­ which could be a better trade­off for the time to dismantle it (#Scrappers 2023).
The values also do not take into account negotiating a higher price for volume,
regularity, high purity or alloy specific prices. All of which is likely to increase the scrap
value of the products. The values are estimated using the material groupings form the
BOM extraction, scrap values from ScrapMonster, 2023 and raw material prices from
Ansys, 2021. The results are shown in Figure 3.25.
72
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:83==
Figure 3.25: Different scrap values of heating systems
Steel circulation benefits
There is an opportunity in improving indirect benefits from recycling materials. The steel
recycling route and prices were investigated to understand the extent to which
secondary material prices can differ from primary ones. For the same process and
quality of steel, the IEA, 2020 sets the current BF­BOF and EAF prices to a similar level.
It indicates that producing the heating systems with secondary materials would not have
an effect in the material costs currently. However, as mentioned in section 2.1.6, there
are changing regulations as the ETS that could affect the carbon pricing and thus the
production route prices for steel. As given by MPP, 2020, it is likely that all steel route
costs would rise due to carbon and energy prices increasing in 2030­2050. It is said that
the EAF route would have relatively lower cost increase than the BF­BOF route. Primary
steel production could even have up to a factor 40 more increase compared to
secondary material for a global average steel production.
The consequences by 2030 for the production of a Vitocal 100s with its outdoor unit
would be around 6% to 47% savings depending on the amount of recycled content in the
product (see figure 3.26). The former would be the result of supplying secondary
material from the end of life of decommissioned heat pumps (Own recycling). The latter
would be if the recycled content hypothetically reaches 100% of the steel supplied (Full
recycling). However, due to the uncertain nature of such predictions, these potential
benefits were not integrated in the final cost analysis of the solution.

73
==PAGE:84==
Figure 3.26: The current and potential future steel route production costs
3.6
How heating systems are disposed
The situation analysis showed that the current end of life of heating systems and similar
products is relatively opaque. Even though regulations like the WEEE should direct
heating systems into some treatment to recover and correctly dispose of electronic
elements, the findings trace an other reality. The assessed products are viewed as
”mostly steel” and treated like so by installers and scrap dealers (#Recyclers 2023,
#Scrap yards 2023, #Installers 2023/03/07, #Installers 2023/05/16). So there tends to
be a systematic downcycling of the metals and a loss of all other materials represented
in the waste stream.
Literature (section 2.1.5, Ohno et al., 2017 Nakajima et al., 2013) shows that other
industries like the car industry have tried and succeeded to improve the recycling of their
products. Applying ecodesign principles and modelling the waste stream to optimise the
separated fraction to functionally recycle valuable elements is feasible today.
Existing waste management routes also have their limitations, depending on the take
back systems goals. Current waste routes have issues with informal waste actors,
salvaging or removing some components. The scale of this is unknown but significant
according to PROs. This limits the upcycling potential for PROs and recycling
companies in general, for they may be handling the worst condition products, and
sometimes with the most valuable components removed (#Elertur 2023). This is of
course not always the case, but a consistent and large issue ­ and likely varies from
region to region. As refurbishment is not of interest for whole heating systems, this is not
the largest concern. However it limits the ability to upcycle components, as they may be
sought out for scrap. But the removal of copper piping and auxiliary components, before
shredding, is environmentally positive. Especially as it is may be out of the scope for
PROs. The current waste stream is therefore somewhat limited in its ability to give the
stakeholders with the most product expertise full control of their products EoL.
However the current established waste management routes are not available for the
74
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:85==
majority discarded heating systems. This poses a multitude of challenges: 1. it makes it
difficult to track efforts for improving disposal practices, 2. it makes it very hard to benefit
from the economies of scale of existing waste management systems.

4
Root cause analysis & Goal definition ­
what could and should happen to
heating systems?
4.1
Root cause analysis ­ Why is it hard to take back heating
systems?
The goal of this section is to identify the limitations and causes that shape the current
situation. It aims to summaries the important characteristics of the system and their
relations, which are usually symptoms of more profound systems behaviour. Analysing
the root causes brings to light the intricacies of the problems to solve and help shape the
solution space. This was done using the fishbone method, which plots different
influencing areas of a main factor. A series of why questions are then asked to determine
the origin of the factor influence on the system, using the areas to guide the process.
The basis for this analysis is the situation analysis and our developed expertise on the
area. The factors treated here are: 1. The low value of steel, 2. The current tendency to
inefficiently recycle products, 3. The limited amount of scrap supply, 4. The limited
installer participation potential, 5. The diversity of the products portfolio. After evaluating
the factors impact on the system and their root causes, each analysis was combined in a
large fishbone diagram that summarises and tries to account for the factors interactions.
Moreover, the factors were turned into solution spaces that would be investigated later in
ideas generation phase. The final diagram is shown in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: The combined fishbone diagram representing the root causes and the derived
opportunities that make up the solution area.
Competing against scrap dealers
The current established waste management is based on the most convenient and
economical way of disposing and recycling materials for the actors involved (see
subsection 3.2.4). Introducing a new take back system, even if the recovering quality
increases, has to be competitive with the existing one. The scrap dealers are entities
that are specialised in recovering value from products such as the heating systems. The
scale of the material flow they receive and its diversity does not allow them to have
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
77
==PAGE:88==
much time to treat the products in detail. However, they have developed the processes
and relations to get the best out of the large amount and variety of waste they handle.
Thus, making economies of scale enables them to create profit. The fact that they
handle different waste streams makes them more resilient to market fluctuations than a
single product focused waste management system, which makes the competition even
harder. As they are a long established industry, they also have all the needed
certifications and stakeholder trust built up, in contrary to a new take back system.
A sparse geographical coverage
The geographical coverage of decommissioned heating system is highly uncertain and
distances between each site is probably high. This leads to the challenge of offering an
efficient pick­up service with reduced costs. It also challenges to efficiently cover the
distances between the sparsely decommissioned products as a last miles logistic
system would do in the reversed case of a delivery. It would also potentially hinder the
sustainability if the transport were to outweigh the environmental benefits of the
improved end of life.
A low material value
One of the key challenge is the low material value of heating systems. Most of the parts
have materials that hardly go above 5€/kg (see Table A.4). At this price it is difficult to
imagine recovering scrap, selling it and ending up with a profit margin. In addition to
that, most of currently decommissioned products are oil and gas boilers, which are being
phased out due to energy price and sustainability concerns. This means that the
potential of remanufacturing and reusing parts in the same use case is largely
diminished. Thus, selling scrap would be the main end of life activity for boilers.
However, boilers are being replaced mostly­ by heat pumps which currently breaks all
records of installation growth rate in Germany and the EU. This also means that the
leanings from boiler recovery, even if not profitable directly, might help improving the
potential profits of heat pumps end of life.
Beyond established steel recycling
As the materials used in heating systems have low values, it is not a priority to recycle
them efficiently. However, it still lowers the scrap value and thus, the profit potential from
selling it. It also lets the tramp elements accumulating faster as separating the alloys
before shredding is not a priority because the cost of doing so would overcome the
material value (subsection 3.2.4, #Scrap yards 2023, subsubsection 2.1.2, #Scrappers
2023).
Brand participation
Including other brands in a take back system is a challenge as information sharing would
be needed and would be seen as a risk for other brands. The sensitive information
about production might be needed to get the most out of the material and components
recovery. If information sharing is limited, it would impact the economical and
environmental benefits of the take back system. However, if workarounds are found and
proven to make other brands participate, it would open opportunities for more profitable
remanufacturing and reuse of components.
Different installers workflows
The different installers workflows might complicate the take back system integration.
Depending on the delivery system used by the installers and their current way of
transporting old products to their end of life with a variety of deals with EoL actors, it
might be hard to have just one fixed take back set­up. The more the differences, the
harder it would be to accommodate a given take back system, if not thought of in
advance for flexibility. At the same time, the drop­off or pick­up distances would also be
fluctuating from installer to installer and thus the costs more uncertain.
78
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:89==
Making an effort in participating
It also needs to convince stakeholders to participate and build trust that it won’t impact
negatively their business. Installers are also extremely busy workers as there is a lack of
manpower in this profession (#Tariq 2023, #CircHeat 2022,
German Heatpump Association, 2023). This would not facilitate their acceptance in
increasing efforts in discarding wastes. More than that, the need of changing the way
they have always worked, presents risks that the value of a new take back system must
compensate. Installers are also the key actors that have the last eyes on the used
products. By deciding on their participation, they drive the collection rate for a take back
system, and thus it has to accommodate to their needs closely. There are also storage
space availability to consider, and at installer’s companies, they are currently used for
their core business.
Economic treatment
Due to the heterogeneous nature of the waste stream, the main treatment process is
assumed to be manual labour. This is expected to be a expensive part of the take back
system as every product has to be inspected and treated separately. There are ways to
reduce the variations in the product stream, but even the same product has multiple
versions over the year and might show large differences that doesn’t allow for
automated processes. As for brand agnostic streams, the need of sharing sensitive
information might be challenging if multiple actors are involved in treating the products
and components.
Steel downcycling
The current inefficient way of recycling steel (or downcycling), still has its place in the
industry. Unless there is a perfect information sharing and pure waste separation,
downcycling will occur. This has to do with the fact that remanufacturing, reuse, and
recycling cannot have a closed loop because of thermodynamics laws and product
demand evolution (see subsubsection 2.1.2). Given the long lifetime of the heating
systems, the material and components might not find a similar function to fulfil when they
reach end of life. This is especially true in the current context, with heat pumps replacing
oil and gas boilers. It is also linked to the low value of the materials and thus, the low
interest in recycling every alloy separately as it would yield insignificant value
improvement. In the same time, current steel demand is rising and thus there will always
be a need for primary steel, where low quality scrap is used anyway for cooling the liquid
steel (see subsubsection 2.1.2). It is also possible to fully provide secondary material for
the heating system only if the steel scrap source comes from outside of the heating
industry. The opportunity to get control of the end of life of the materials creates good
conditions for reducing any supply chain risks. It allows to have a strategic supervision
of the previously used material in the industry.
New products & opportunities
When learning to take back old heating systems, the insights have a high potential of
being usable in new product design ­ making future products easier to take back. In
addition to that, when knowing that today’s heat pumps will be replaced by heat pumps,
the components are likely to be more easily reusable. Heat pumping is also built on
more general components, with more use cases. Moreover, having a profitable take
back system for end of life products could allow for new service offerings for competitors
or other industries. This is also where being early in such a development gives the
opportunity to create value out of the leanings.
The opportunities for solutions comes from challenges
Challenges are parameters that needs to be overcome, but they also have the power of
highlighting opportunities shown in Figure 4.1. Having a flexible inbound logistic and
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
79
==PAGE:90==
accepting brand agnostic products might be opportunities that would reduce the
challenges of installer participation linked to the established waste management system
and the geographical sparseness of decommissioning sites. It would also be a strategic
solution to adapt the take back system to any waste streams, regions, and market
conditions. This goes in hand with designing a system that accepts other brands and
products which it would make it easier to scale up and create more potential for profit.
From a sustainable point of view, it would also drive the installer participation if there is
one solution for any product brand and type. This would drive a high collection rate and
increasing the amount of recovered products, and thus, the overall environmental
benefits. The low material value and potentially expensive treatment labour would be
asking for a targeted and planned disassembly. Targeting the right components and
materials would allow the most efficient cost/benefits ratio. The interesting parts might
be given by their economical value, environmental impact, or the relevance for suppliers.
The value of materials could be improved by targeting known mix of alloys which would
give more potential recycling value and use cases. The dynamic nature of the market
and building trust with other brands would require to come with long term roadmaps,
which would include the four previous opportunities. This would need to be discussed
with the main stakeholders to reach a point where most interests converge. In this sense
it also opens opportunities for collaborative solutions that would improve the co­creation
of take back systems.
It is crucial for the system design to set the goals that would enable to catch most of the
stated opportunities. Including flexibility in the logistic set­up and treatment planning
from the early phase of the system design would, lower the risks of the entire venture. It
would also allow to include other brands and products as the system develops. This will
enable more value creation the leanings. Targeting the right parts of the system would
allow to make most economical and environmental benefits, while still having the
potential of improving the recycling of less interesting materials. Gaining control of its
own waste is a strategic position to reduce risks. In the end, collaborating with the
stakeholders in the design process gives the opportunity to create the take back system
that suits all stakeholders the best.
4.2
Reflection ­ what should be the goals?
This section defines the goals for the solution design. It builds on the situation analysis
and literature reviews that assemble all the needed knowledge about the current
system. The findings are then summed up to highlight opportunities and challenges that
shape the solution space. This chapter starts by outlining the opportunities and their
particularities. It then sets a list of measurable objectives for the design of solutions,
where the ideas are to be evaluated against. The needs that were defined by interviews
with installers, scrap dealers and Viessmann’s contacts are the basis with which the
objectives are developed.
4.2.1
Types of successes
At this point it is important to consider the different types of successes in designing take
back systems for heating systems. For the goals established at this stage, they will set
the focus with which will be conducted the finding of solutions and combining them into a
full take back system. The successes are not mutually exclusive but different in nature.
The main ones considered are:
1. Improving material circularity
2. Reducing environmental impact at end of life
80
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:91==
3. Increasing supply chain security
4. Profiting on heating systems end of life
5. Enabling upcycling, such as reuse, remanufacturing and reconditioning of
components.
These successes were defined after extensive discussions with Viessmann and in line
with their sustainability strategy and road map (#CE Pillars 2023, #Tariq 2023, #Wade
2023). Simultaneously, they are derived from the findings in literature review and other
field interviews.
1. Improving material circularity
It is clear from the analysis of the product
composition, current waste management, and especially steel recycling processes, that
a clear metric of success for the take back system is the amount of avoided dissipation
of critical materials. This has several positive knock off effects, the purer separation
results in longer cycling of materials in the EU market, which is an EU goal. It also
makes it less risky to use critical materials, like copper. Enabling the use of materials
without worrying about their dissipation to the same extent, could enable design that are
more efficient in the use phase ­ which is by far the most important life cycle phase for
heating systems. For an overview of materials that are recoverable, see: Figure 3.7
There are prospects that more materials and alloys can be recovered in the future
(#Panatere 2023). However, since high tech steel alloys are seldomly used in heating
systems, it is not particularly applicable. It could become more relevant as regulations
tighten and costs fall, but it would need to be very significant.
Reuse and remanufacturing of components shows high potential of improving the
circularity of materials as most materials would be reused as is. In this case, the
inevitable thermodynamic downcycling would be reduced to a minimum for all the
reused material.
2. Reducing environmental impact at end of life
In line with increasing material
circularity, clean fractions also result in less polluting recycling processes, and offsets
some primary production. This has been shown for steel recycling (see
subsubsection 2.1.2), and it is assumed the same case holds for other materials.
Reuse and remanufacturing have high potential in reducing environmental impacts as
they allow material elements to keep their functionality. They also demand less energy
to process as reused material is not melted and only need refurbishment and cleaning. If
the shape is kept, some embodied energy is also retained. There are some concerns
that the efficiency/performance of reuse and remanufactured products could be limited
due to less design flexibility.
3 Increasing supply chain security
Supply chains have been disrupted in the past
years, and the demand for especially semiconductors has posed challenges for
equipment manufacturers. Many companies have reported salvaging electronic
components from discarded products, and begun reverse logistics efforts to continue
production ­ as it was finally economically &/ strategically viable
(German Heatpump Association, 2023; MADE, 2023).
Establishing a take back system, will support OEMs in getting the capability of salvaging
components and partly mitigate supply chain issues. However the case for electronic
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
81
==PAGE:92==
components may be somewhat limited or very product dependent for heating systems
due to the long lifespan. On the other hand the longevity might be an opportunity as
manufacturers are obliged to supply spare parts only for 7­10 years for a product that
can function for 20 years (EuropeanCommission, 2023). This means that it could be
mostly beneficial for heat pumps (which are thought to be the future of heating system) to
be able to recover reusable spare parts in the decommissioned products after 10 years.
4. Profiting on heating systems at end of life
In order to have a functioning take
back system, environmental benefits are not enough. It must at least break even. If not,
there will be no take back system, and thus, no environmental benefits (#Tariq 2023).
The other way to oblige a take back system is by law. However, even the latter solution
would make costing the primary drive, potentially minimising environmental benefits.
Therefore, the best way to maximise the environmental benefits is to have an
economically viable take back system
At scale, it can be possible to profit slightly off the heating systems end of life. This is of
course always interesting. It can also be a very positive branding story, going the extra
mile for sustainability. However, the main factor determining what heating system is
chosen by consumers, is fuel economy and reliability (#Tariq 2023).
Depending on the chosen take back system design, it can also be a market differentiator
for the installers. If it saves the installer companies effort when replacing heating
systems, then they may in busy periods be more inclined to recommend Viessmann’s
systems.
There are several trends making it easier to profit off of the end of life, namely the rising
ETS prices and upcoming enforcement, the rise of raw material prices, value scrap
prices, interest in circularity and consumer interest in sustainability. Moreover, reusable
components might show higher value than their scrap value (Twidale et al., 2023, #Steel
price 2023 ).
Why are they successes for Viessmann?
From the point of view of Viessmann, the above mentioned points hold true. The main
factor is the ability to have an economically viable business model. It is clear that they
have committed to reduce their environmental impact with ambitious objectives. But for
them to be achieved, there needs to be short term benefits as they cannot afford to
loose market shares. They have to be competitive in today’s world if they want to be
environmentally sound in tomorrow’s. On the other hand they have to comply to
regulations industry practices which pushes them to assess their environmental footprint
and report on it. Thus, having circular economy projects is needed as a branding
opportunity. With a highly competitive market and supply chain risks, a take back system
as any other Viessmann’s system, has to be efficiently conducted. Most importantly as
complexity does not scale up linearly, it is necessary for them to have eyes on the
quantitative nature of their systems, so that they can develop and maintain efficient
processes. Therefore, being able to lower the costs and increase the profitability of their
business model.
4.2.2
Is it necessary to develop a separate take back system for heating
systems?
There are clear gains, across multiple domains, this confirms our prior findings and
recommendations from the previous project ­ now with a lot more nuance and backing
by data (#CircHeat 2022). It is clear that the main opportunities lies in improving the
material circularity and passing on the upcycling capabilities to OEMs. There is some
82
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:93==
potential in positive branding, market differentiation and profiting off of the EoL phase,
however it appears to be limited.
However given that current EoL goes through less managed disposal routes, there is
very limited potential for the existing waste management system adapting to handle
these products in a way that allows component reuse and remanufacturing. It would
require either significant changes in waste handling, legislation and/or enforcement. The
limited amount of heating systems that do end up at recycling centres, here only heat
pumps appear to be sorted as white goods and are received by PROs ­ so there is more
potential for heat pumps to be handled through this route. However the vast majority of
heating systems end up in mixed metal. To use PROs, it will requires installers to
change waste management practices (see subsection 3.2.3 & subsection 3.2.4).
Current PROs focus on product reuse, and currently do not have component
recirculation capabilities ­ but are working on it. But their current waste streams have
several issues: namely a large proportion of valuable products or products’ components
are stolen before they receive them (#Elertur 2023). The storage and handling of the
products can also damage or limit the circular potential.
Therefore there is clear and long term potential in getting control of heating systems end
of life closer to the source:
1. Avoid all/most theft of valuable components
2. Control and document EoL handling.
3. Reduce payment to EPR, as less ends up in WEEE streams.
4. Utilise product expertise to target efforts and further enhance product end of life.
5. Provide upcycling capabilities, through the EoL control.
6. Ability to ensure further material separation
Why ’recreate the scrap yard’
For most current waste actors, it is infeasible to dismantle the product to increase its
scrap value. Whilst some do do it, it is not commercialised efforts, but done by private or
independent scrappers (#Scrappers 2023, #Scrap yards 2023). This is largely due to
the inability to streamline and optimise the process to justify the logistics and labour
costs associated with it. The existing waste management system is also reliant on
purely the scrap value, and practices of recirculating the components to OEMs are not in
place. Given their raison d’être, it is also hard for them to implement ­ they are dealing
with almost limitless product variety and complexity. Keeping track and identifying what
different products are and what components are of interest for OEMs to recirculate them,
is simply out of scope currently, as component circulation/reuse currently is so limited.
If waste disposal practices changed and heating systems did end up at PROs this would
be a feasible inbound logistics route. It is however unclear if it would be within their
capabilities to streamline the dismantling process enough to retrieve the necessary
components. The condition and presence of the components can also be questionable.
A separate treatment facility may still be necessary.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
83
==PAGE:94==
4.3
Requirements and criteria
The main stakeholders that affect and are affected by the potential solutions, are listed
below with their solution performance criteria. This is based on the situation analysis
and our developed expertise on the area.
1. Installers
(a) Low cost: Solutions should not increase the cost of handling waste.
(b) Low workload: Their workload is already high as there is a lack of heating
technicians in Germany (#Tariq 2023, #Installers 2023/03/07, #Installers
2023/05/16). In this sense it is a limiting factor if they have to spend more
time than before to be part of the solution, even if they are fairly
compensated. At the same time there are seasonal variations in the amount
of work for installers. Thus, even a small additional workload might become a
challenge for installers when installations are peaking.
(c) More attractive than existing: Installer participation in important for the take
back system to work. It should not be much more difficult for installers to
dispose of product. This means that it needs, either to better compensate the
installers for the work done than their current practices. Or lift the burden of
handling waste off their shoulders.
2. Treatment partners (scrap dealers, recycling centres and other treatment
facilities)
(a) Low cost: The cost of handling the waste should be low as they are the ones
that would have the higher costs in the chain. Because their business is
handling waste they would be the one paying for transport and treatment. The
lower the cost of theses operations, the higher the chance of making profits.
(b) Fair benefits: As their business is about treating and selling wastes, they are
bound to make profit during the process. Thus, the benefits should fairly
compensate the handling costs.
3. OEMs
(a) Low cost: As they have fixed prices and quality standards for their products
based on ”standard” supply chains, they are limited in what they can afford to
spend for reused components.
(b) Good components condition: The reusability of components is given by their
functional condition when they are recovered, and thus, OEMs would not pay
for unusable parts. This means that having the best condition for the
components would result in higher benefits, otherwise they would be sent to
recycling which holds less value to the said components.
(c) Compliant end of life: For the components unfit for reuse, they need to be
disposed of at minimum in a compliant way. Increasingly there may be an
interest in paying for better disposal if the environmental benefits can be
documented for their sustainability reporting.
84
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:95==
Another set of criteria apply directly to Viessmann’s take back system.
1. Logistics
(a) Low cost: The transport and storage costs need to be as low as possible.
The lower they are, the more flexibility it gives to the system to get the best
out of the material recovery while maintaining a profit margin.
Low transport distance: This requirement is directly related to the logistics
costs. However, it also gives the environmental perspective, as there could
be cheaper options where the transport emissions would outweigh the
environmental benefits of material recovery.
(b)
2. Take back as a whole
(a) Low product leakage: The take back systems ability to keep the entering
products in the system and not dissipate them while they are handled. Lost
products (or unwanted ones) would incur extra handling costs.
(b) Direct control of end of life: A long lasting solution would require to have the
possibility to directly influence the handling in the waste stream. The more
control Viessmann has on the handling steps, the easier it is to guide and
change practices according to the evolution of the context.
(c) Suitable for all residential climate solutions: The opportunities of economies
of scale also come from the possibility of adapting the take back system to
any kind of Viessmann’s residential products. This could be even better when
accepting other brands to enter the system.
(d) Keep products/components condition: The economical and environmental
potential of upcycling components is connected to the system’s ability to keep
the components integrity. Having a cheap system that recovers only
damaged components, will reduce the systems economic and sustainability
potential and thus its overall feasibility.
(e) Information sharing: Sharing information both from installers on the product
integrity and internally/across OEMs/brands will enable more targeted and
specialised treatment, reducing costs and increasing earnings.
(f) High level of disassembly: Disassembly creates the opportunity to improve
the end of life treatment of both the components and materials.
(g) Recycling focus: There are multiple recycling focuses possible at the same
time. 1. Focusing on recovering the highest priced scrap would enable to
have less labour for better benefits. 2. Focusing on the lowest downcycling
option would increase the value of the part recovered. This includes
upcycling and functional recycling as better options than current downcycling
(h) Collaborations: When collaborators can also benefit from the system, it yields
a competitive advantage for all of them, as they are all striving for a common
cause. However, cost consciousness will create a need for negotiating fair
costs and benefits sharing between stakeholders to succeed.
(i) Costs and benefits analysis & co­creativity: On the same note as the point
above, being able to model, calculate and predict the costs and benefits, will
improve the ability of collaborators to project themselves in taking part in the
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
85
==PAGE:96==
take back solution. This transparency will support the co creativity, than can
help the best performing take back system for all stakeholders.
Evaluating the proposed solutions with these criteria will enable us to find the best
possible trade­offs between the possible options. The criteria are suitable for a take back
system that collects any heating system category that have a B2B2C business model.
Most of the criteria are interconnected with many others. Performing good in one might
have positive effects for other objectives, and the contrary is true as well. A requirement
relation matrix was done using our designers experience and the knowledge of the
context to fill it.
The main insights are:
1. Increasing the quality of material recovery (climbing the circularity ladder) results in
more costly logistics and disassembly.
2. Keeping components condition when taking back is harder but beneficial for later
stage.
3. Keeping low installer workload is competing with making the process easier
(Traceability, products condition, efficiency) but is necessary for their participation ­
if the system is co­created and they get compensated fairly for some work, it might
be beneficial.
4. Having the lower possible cost solution is not compatible with having a good
control of the EoL of the products.
5. More type of products is more costs and less easy to capture value and share
costs ­ probably why a very flexible solution is needed.
6. Capturing higher value is more costly especially in disassembly.
7. The more information on product, the harder it is to recover it from
decommissioning but the easier it is later for capturing value.
8. Focusing only on high scrap price is hindering better recycling practices and less
interesting for OEMs.
9. Tracing products EoL is costly at early stage but brings value later, however, only
interesting if a better recycling practice is achieved.
10. The different stakeholders will compete to have a better share of benefits and less
costs.
86
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:97==
Figure 4.2: Full requirements and criteria with captions, see ?? for the version without
captions.
4.4
Required detail level of solution for analysis
Selecting the right abstraction level to evaluate the solutions is key to have a
manageable, useful and efficient analysis. For this project a few factors ultimately dictate
the viability for Viessmann to implement a take back system, specifically: 1. Logistics
costs per product. 2. Labour costs per product. 3. Treatment costs per product.
4. Earnings/cost reductions per product. 5. Sustainability improvements per product.
Economical viability is essential, as the current end of life is compliant. The lowest level
of economical viability would be both as an accepted loss per product, for
branding/good­will ­ if the environmental improvement is significant enough. However
this will be infeasible for a brand agnostic system without orchestrating deals with
most/all brands. Key data is currently not possible to get, making full scale analysis
impossible, e.g., lack of choice of logistics providers and OEMs willingness to pay for
components at this stage (#Tariq 2023).
To investigate the viability, instead a focus will be put on determining the limits and
boundaries of the main factors impacting the viability of taking back heating systems, to
support comparison of different configurations. Key performance metrics of the
simulations will be:
1. Costs per heating systems and required pricing of COI.
2. Installer time usage & value.
3. Environmental impact reductions in GWP and energy usage.
Key will be to also investigate the difference between boilers and heat pumps. It may
prove infeasible to take back the gas boilers ­ but possible for heat pumps. Identifying
what factors drive this difference, will be valuable, to recommend what Viessmann can
do to improve the feasibility of taking back the next generations of heating systems.
This level of assessment will reduce the need to assess smaller aspects of the proposed
solution. It will enable us as modellers, to focus on the overarching effect of these
solutions. However in practice, smaller solution elements will influence the performance
and scalability of the take back system.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
87
==PAGE:98==

5
Search for solutions ­ How can take
