5.1
The process of ideating solution elements
There are clear types of solutions, which will determine the majority of the take back
systems impact and viability. But there is a lot of smaller partial solution elements which
could be interesting, both for research and understanding of the possibilities of take
back system design. Whilst ideas are a dime a dozen ­ and the difficulty is not innovative
ideas, but implementing and executing innovative ideas. It is however always valuable
and interesting to expand the solution space, map and document the process.
The internal process:
The ideation process was done predominately internally ­
mostly to allow for a longer ideation process. Exploration and evaluation of less
realistic/long term ideas. Then filtering and grouping the ideas, to be able to hand off a
more exhaustive mapping of ideas relevant to take back system design.
The overarching process was:
1. Individual ideation for solutions to each challenge.
2. Inclusion of existing ideas.
3. Plenum discussion and ideation of idea for each challenge.
4. Clustering of ideas and naming of clusters.
5. Elimination of duplicate ideas in each cluster.
6. Creation of fitting requirements and criteria for each cluster.
7. Qualitative evaluation of ideas.
1­3.
To systemically cover the entire identified problem space, we took basis in the
combined fish bone diagram. For each challenge, a 10­15 minutes individual brainstorm
was conducted. After the initial generation of ideas for each challenge, plenum
discussion of the ideas, and further ideation was done. This was less structured. The
output of this is illustrated in Figure 5.1, zoomed in and higher resolution versions are
available in the appendix ­ but the goal was to have an internal understanding of the
solutions to support the next steps.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
89
==PAGE:100==
Figure 5.1: Raw ideas around the fishbone
4.
The ideas were then clustered to find similarities, and inspire new ideas in relation to
each other. The clustering is illustrated in Figure 5.2. After the ideas and clusters were
explored and new ideas were added.
90
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:101==
Figure 5.2: Clustered ideas
5­7.
As is evident in Figure 5.1 and Figure 5.2, there is a very large amount of ideas,
and of very varying quality/applicability/realism. The first step was to give the clusters a
name and 1­2 sentence description to help keep focus during rating. To ensure a
common understanding, each unique idea was also described. As each cluster of ideas
addresses different challenges, they are best evaluated using different requirements and
criteria. Due to the amount of ideas, most clusters were only evaluated on 2­3
criteria/requirements. Ideas were ranked on a scale from 1­5 within each criteria. If a
solution scored lower than 3 on average it was eliminated.
In the rating solutions which were applicable to this projects case and company were
strongly preferred. This bias has limited the solution space significantly ­ but this was the
goal. The remaining ideas are now brought into the next step, combining the partial
solutions into various full solutions.
The full solution catalogue like the above work is rather shabby and uses a lot internal
wordings and assumes intimate understanding with the project/area ­ however it is
available upon request.
5.2
Types of solutions
The first family of solutions are the ones that avoid or don’t need a take back system to
exist. Building on the fact that there is already a waste management network specialised
in recovering value from waste, it was considered whether it was possible to have good
results in circulating materials by having targeted improvements in collaboration with
these actors.
1. Incentivised recovery: Knowing that just a few components could potentially
already have a large impact on the material circularity, the idea of asking the
installers to recover the parts themselves seems interesting. It would also be
possible to ask the scrap dealers to do the task before selling the scrap. The
recovered parts would then be sent to OEMs and the remains would follow the
current disposal route. This would have a significant cost reduction compared to a
take back system as there would be very limited logistic needs. The drawbacks
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
91
==PAGE:102==
would be the limited amount of components recoverable as the installers would
rather prioritise their main business operation ­ installing and servicing heating
systems or other products.
2. Utilising the existing Producer Responsibility Organisations (PROs): Using
collaborators to treat the heating systems for Viessmann would also probably be
an efficient solution. They already have most of the waste handling capabilities
and would certainly be able to address a large waste flow. A lot of uncertainties lie
around the possible pricing of such a solution. There would also need to find ways
to oblige or incentive’s installers to use these streams.
Solutions focused on take back systems build on the previous project done on that
subject. Here the aim is to detail them further and find other options building on the
accumulated knowledge of the existing take back systems.
The logistic and infrastructure ideas describe the different possible ways to pick­up the
products and the potential collaborators that can help in that task.
1. Third party partners: This solution suggests to contract third party logistic
providers to handle the transport and buffering of the collected products. It is
thought that their expertise and their existing capabilities would be competitive and
keep the prices low. There would also be the potential of having a good
geographical coverage.
2. Scrap dealers partners: It was considered the possibility to contract the scrap
dealers to handle the waste from the customer or installer place to bulk them up at
their scrap yards. Then Viessmann would be in charge of collecting the bulks and
moving forward with the recovery themselves. Here a key issue is to pay them
enough so that they would not scrap the products directly instead of keeping them.
3. Industry­wide solution: The opportunity of recovering more than just heating
system could lead to an industry­wide take back system. Here there would be a
separate treatment facility that would accept a large amount of product types. It
would range from white goods to smaller electronic wastes and mechanical
equipment. The logistic costs could be highly improved as the coverage of such
waste would be more dense and one trip might be able to collect more effectively.
This would actually be a sort of PRO but would owned by Viessmann.
4. Changing own logistics network: Here Viessmann would take advantage of
their just in time delivery of new heating systems to pick up old ones. This requires
installers to change practices, and reworking a lot of very optimised logistics. But
since the trucks are driving anyways, this is likely the cheapest and most
environmentally performant option.
5. Drop­off points: It was considered that having scattered drop­off points would
enable installers to drive the heating systems at a nearby place. This would enable
Viessmann to come and collect them regularly. Options would be containers with
access through codes, leasing space in storage centres, or franchising so that
anyone can have a drop­off point, thus increasing the potential coverage.
However, a limitation is the access and the probability of theft to sell the scrap.
The treatment of recovered products could happen in multiple ways. This is a crucial
step to make a take back system viable as it will determine the value of the material and
components recovered.
92
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:103==
1. Scanning the product: There could be multiple ways and reasons to scan the
product. Scanning the product serial number would enable, if made available, to
know the composition of the product and thus help to categories and plan the
treatment. Another possibility is to use image recognition or even x­ray­like
scanners to identify the product type and its materials. This might enable to detect
unfitted and damaged products on the treatment line.
2. Treating one­by­one: The treatment could happen product by product and having
one worker doing all the job before moving on. This would rather fit small streams
as a more streamlined approach could save costs and time.
3. Streamlined treatment: Larger streams would benefit from treating products in
steps. Breaking the process in smaller steps would achieve more repetitive actions
but would make them more efficient too. Less tool changing and movements
would be required at the same time. Destructive, careful and special treatments
could then be done separately.
4. Bulk treatment: It is also possible to process products in bulk. This would require
to sort and store the products that arrive at the treatment centre. Then the
products would be processed by type. This would enable to have a similar
approach to the same bulk and thus a more streamlined process.
5. Treatment at buffer: It would be possible that the products have large parts that
are uninteresting. If the logistic buffers allow it, the products could already be
separated in two or more pieces with simple cutting activities. Then only the
interesting parts would be sent to the next treatment facility and the rest would be
sold as scrap ­ reducing logistics and treatment costs.
6. Planning: The treatment procedure could take different forms. There could be a
heuristic way of treating the pieces with general practices, like cutting the cable
and the tubes. This would allow for creativity and building on the experience of the
workers. Another way could be to meticulously plan each treatment step. This
would allow to optimise processes and easily sharing information. It would also be
possible to only develop that and share it to scrap dealers to guide them in the
dismantling. They would then send the interesting parts back.
7. Disassembly tools: There is a large range of options for the tools, from angle
grinders, bolt cutters, electric screw drivers to plasma cutters, and more. The main
differences are the destructive options and the non­destructive ones. Only
knowing about the interesting components and their fixtures in combination with
the allowed treatment costs would tell which is the best option.
8. Automation: Depending on the goals, there may be possibilities for automating
some of the tasks. From sorting heating systems into its cateogires, to cutting up
the chassis.
9. Smart downcycling: By utilising knowledge on product, component composition
and scrap sorting, some components/modules can possibly be shredded with little
environmental downsides.
The more targeted heating systems that are captured, the higher the environmental
delta. The more OEMs participating, the larger potential for upcycling like reuse and
remanufacturing. Finding ways of driving participation is key
Driving installer participation
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
93
==PAGE:104==
1. Rebates: Offering rebates based on participation, can be a cheaper or more
synergistic than offering cash. This can be rebates on new heating systems, spare
parts, training or other product/service offerings.
2. Flat rewards: Offering flat cash rewards, either per heating system or per type of
heating system can boost transparency and make it tangible the benefits installers
are receiving for participating.
3. Dynamic rates: Differentiating cash rewards for different heating systems and the
context can be a way of managing the influx and cost of heating systems.
4. Convenience: Offering a more convenient way of working can drive participation.
This also enables installers to focus on the core of their business ­ servicing and
installing HVAC systems.
5. Co­branding: Providing marketing materials or other ways of installers to
differentiate them in the market, could drive interest. This will also positively brand
Viessmann.
6. Rate behavior: There may be a need to evaluate and punish installers who
misuse the system. This can also help make the system more exclusive,
synergising with co­branding.
Driving OEM and manufacturer participation
1. Providing samples: By offering free samples of their components at EoL and can
help make the upcycling potential clear.
2. Providing consultancy: By offering the built up expertise in handling old
products, and what other OEMs are doing, can put Viessmann in the position of
guiding other OEMs in how to best upcycle their old components.
3. Requirements: Setting requirements or goals for key components/materials from
suppliers to be upcycled or otherwise handled responsibly.
4. Criteria: Offering rewards or rebates on take back of their components for
upcycling their components. This can be expanded to rewarding continuous
improvement in OEMs upcycling efforts.
5. End of Life services: By offering doing the treatment, remanufacturing or similar
for OEMs, this can reduce the risk and capital expenditure for OEMs to handle
their products. This can be a temporary offering and outsourced to third parties.
6. Functional recycling: Using product composition information can be used to
improve the recycling performance and scrap earnings. Gathering this information
can be an avenue for driving interest.
7. Transparency and publicity: By publicly sharing information on how OEMs
components are being handled at EoL, and their lack of interest in possible
upcycling efforts. It can also illustrate differences between OEMs in otherwise red
ocean markets.
8. Walking the talk: finding any and all components that can be upcycled and
reused internally, to show commitment and expertise. This may be difficult as most
major components of future heating systems are manufactured by 3rd parties, but
it is not impossible. This may require significant internal efforts in convincing
94
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:105==
decision makers. Possible avenues are also creating limited lines or editions of
select heating systems entirely of upcycled materials.
9. Reoccurring circularity workshops: Continuously refining methods and
reevaluating what is possible for current EoL products and components could drive
long term interest. The transition to a circular economy may be long.
There are many supportive solution types which deserve mentioning:
1. Product identification: If all/most heating systems and potentially brands are
accepted, there is an increased need to quickly identify the products.
(a) Markers: Installers write product number/code on the chassis. Specific
letters/symbols can indicate installer company, reason for decommissioning,
condition or other critical information
(b) Logging app: Scan or input the product ID into an app and log necessary
information. The same app can be easily expanded to facilitate pickups of
heating systems. The key strength of this compared to markers is to use it to
filter out products or targeting efforts further. I.e., some products may want to
be taken back, whilst others only need a single component to be removed.
2. Product design: Long term there should likely be efforts in improving product
design for take back.
(a) Dismantle for a day: Let product designers, owners and engineers,
participate in dismantling heating systems and components to get hands on
experience.
(b) Minimise critical and tramp elements in fractions created during
disassembly So all critical elements can easily be recovered in a separate
fractions. One example being boron being present only in the screws of some
products investigated in this project, which is not recoverable using semi
destructive disassembly.
(c) Long term consistent modules and interfaces: product and technology
architecture and modularization comes with many benefits. But very long
term consistent interfaces could potentially enable more upcycling and reuse.
However this is a 10­20 year gambit.
(d) Over­engineering parts: Utilising expertise in knowing what components
generally fail, it may be possible to overengineer other parts to support reuse.
3. Redesign the scrap yard: Collaborate with scrap yards and other large
manufacturers in redesigning how products can end up there in a safe and
protected manner. This can cover everything from regulation, to lot design to
design of new mixed metal containers with fractions and so much more.
4. Reverse logistics packaging: Design new ways for installers to store their waste
onsite, and at their company. This could help minimise sorting at the facility,
protect products from weather and handling. Reverse logistics packaging also
represents a branding opportunity.
5. Free samples of shredded/scrapped components: by offering free samples to
universities, startups and other ventures of components that may be fit for
upcycling, reuse or refurbishment ­ it could help drive a large market for these
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
95
==PAGE:106==
components. This needs to be marketed with images and details on the
components and volumes the system can provide, to intise the interestd.
5.3
Combining solution elements into full solutions
To systematically create full solutions morphologies were used. A morphology is a
systematic way of generating system/product configurations. First identifying the ’must
have’ solution categories, listing all ideated solutions for each category. This enables
one or more solutions from each category and have a complete solution. The full solution
may need some adaption or further design to function in practice. The key strength of
using morphology, is to combine disparate elements, that would otherwise not be
considered, which could complement each other. When generating designs using a
morphology, it is preferable to create goals for the system, to actively break ones priming
and actively select solutions one is less keen on ­ to fairly judge the solution space.
Key for ensuring a high quality morphology is identifying solution categories that offer:
1. A good overview of the solution space. 2. Distinct groups, whose solutions impact the
performance uniquely. 3. Minimise selecting many solutions in one category 4. Order
them so tracing a path through it helps select the next solutions.
The process
To create the solution groups, we took basis in a product life cycle. But
many ideas did not fit into the traditional life cycle. Especially regulation and
organisational ideas did not fit in, as both categories are relevant both before and during
the system life cycle. In the team the understanding is that the organisational structure
shapes what kind of installer, take back, disposal solutions and regulation solutions are
the most applicable. Therefore it was chosen as the first category. Regulatory solutions
were seen as long term options, which should be add­ons/nice­to­have outcomes, and
therefore left as the last category.
From the ideation process there were a lot of ideas, and numerous groupings, based on
the clusters from the ideation process. However after filtering many groupings had few
ideas left and addressed similar aspects, they were therefore renamed and remapped
into the overarching categories, resulting in the subcategories shown in the
morphologies below.
96
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:107==
Figure 5.3: The ungrouped raw morphology
Other views
The morphology above offers a very condensed view of the solution
space, but this is also its crux. The dense nature can be overwhelming, and be hard to
relate to the ideas, and loose ones overview.
Therefore the ideas were once again clustered within their groupings. Here copying
ideas was allowed, but minimised as much as possible. Each grouping was then named
to offer a better overview.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems

has been roughly mapped to according what criteria it impacts, and how it may be
evaluated. The criteria and icons are presented below. The analysis measures take

5.3.1
Generated solutions
To investigate the solution space, the solutions below were created internally in the
team. They represent a qualitative and expert opinion on what solutions are most
applicable for different goals. These full solutions were not quantitatively evaluated, as
that would require too much data and resources. But the goal is to demonstrate how
different solution elements contribute to different goals. The solutions are not to be seen
as definitive, but explorative.
The pilot project solution
The goal in the pilot project is to clarify the solution space
as much as possible at a low cost. Therefore it focuses on voluntary participation of
installers, OEMs, recycling centres and scrap yards. By sourcing products from multiple
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
99
==PAGE:110==
endpoints, it enables thorough evaluation of the products condition. By dismantling the
products at scrap yard, one can avoid potential regulatory hurdles ­ and learn from the
best!
Figure 5.6: Pilot project oriented selection of ideas
Cheapest solution
Handling unwanted heating systems is expensive, both logistically
and in riddance, punishing the installers and rating their behaviour could reduce this.
OEMs need to pay for extra treatment and benefits will only be shared through a ranking
of the components value and environmental impact at EoL. Logistics and treatment is
outsourced, and treatment is done in steps. Minimal product protection is offered, in
form of heavy duty plastic bags.
Figure 5.7: Cheapest solution
Most sustainable solution
The main driver for sustainability is by treating the most
systems as possible ­ therefore prioritising convenience and adapting the take back
system to the installers workflow ­ and accepting all heating systems. As far as possible
just in time delivery of systems is reworked to pickup the old systems at the clients
100
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:111==
house, to avoid unnecessary transport. Product expertise is gained through DPP and
sharing trade secretes between OEMs to know more about alloys, and what can be
reused. The products are protected in reusable packaging to enable more reuse. All 3rd
party components are removed. Made feasible with custom jigs & tools and careful
disassembly. OEMs are encouraged and guided in white­labelling the reused
components to avoid cannibalisation of their markets and damage to their brand.
Treatment of products is guided by material scarcity and digital tools are used to guide
the process to minimise the amount of down cycled material. Mono­fractions are
produced smartly, trying to ensure that mono­fraction sorting machines can remove
most of the impurities! The materials are only sold to ambitious recyclers with long term
sustainability and circularity plans.
Figure 5.8: Most circular/sustainable solution
Most scalable
By incentivising installers economically to participate, it will be easier to
scale to new regions. Focusing on sharing benefits between OEMs based on scrap
value, will be fast and homogeneous. OEMs need to pay for extra treatment and we can
offer better EoL for OEMs components ­ for a price. Disassembly will be based on
heuristics and smart cutting ­ minimising the need for planning and organisational costs.

==PAGE:112==
Figure 5.9: Most scalable selection solution
5.4
Decision support tools ­ the design of the poster
This section discusses the process of designing the poster. For details on the final
poster, how to use it, and discussion of its’ applicability, go to subsection 7.1.4. It was a
goal from the beginning of the project to develop a tool to support the implementation of
a take back system. There was a clear need for tools to support this type of
assessment, as it covers many different areas and includes a multitude of variables.
As discussed in the literature review; Earlier work has outlined key things to consider for
each product and take back system design. Here we are especially leaning on Mallick
et al., 2023; De Brito and Dekker, 2004. However these frameworks centres on the
context, design of reverse logistics network, governance, evaluation and scale up. They
form a great outline for the overarching process, but are missing specifics ­ and tools
and methods for how to evaluate product in a large product portfolio. This mirrors much
prior research on circular economy and take back systems; there is a lack of concrete
tools and processes to go through to evaluate when and how to take back products.
When the preliminary results from the quantitative analysis were ready it began to be
clear that the uncertainty around a lot of values ­ especially logistics costs, labour costs
and OEMs participation & cost sharing. These key values will remain uncertain and be
very product and regionally dependent, and logistics only be determined when a
logistics provider is selected. A lot of this will also not be determined within the scope of
this project, making it hard to arrive at definite results ­ and the context will change
significantly over time. However a lot of the analysis for a specific product/product
category is more static: the environmental impacts of components, how long it takes to
disassemble the product and practical logistics concerns.
From the cost analysis it was clear that there would not be time for careful disassembly,
nor that it was necessary. The video study of independent scrap dealers practices also
made it clear that a lot could be sped up with the right creative disassembly techniques.
Disassembly planning was therefore clearly important for each product/product category.
However the identified research into disassembly planning, as discussed in the literature
review, does not have this focus. Those tools require CAD models and expert
102
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:113==
knowledge in how to do the analysis. The tools were just unfit for the large product
portfolio and mixed and changing requirements for components and the goal of
disassembly. Given the way disassembly is conducted is such a key performance
aspect, both for costs, what materials are free and what COI can be upcycled ­ there
needs to be a tool to assist in this process.
Creating a simple environmental assessment and evaluating scrap prices, requires a lot
of product knowledge and preparation. In this project we necessitated to use new
products, as BOMs were readily available, for many of Viessmanns products this is hard
to find, and may even be in a paper archive. These BOMs also need to be converted
into a ”simplified LCI”, to make it manageable (i.e., 10 material fractions is already a lot,
the BOMs may have 20 to a 100+). As covered in this section, this data treatment was
not trivial, and can take days to do manually.
Similar product insights, like the condition of product components at end of life, the
OEMs interest in them and compositions, is also spread around and might be unknown
to the organisation. Especially for 10­20 year old products.
This story repeats itself for so many necessary considerations for the products, if it is
going to be viable to take the products back and upcycle components. Without these
insights upcycling will be hindered, and it will be almost impossible to profit.
There is therefore a need to map what product data and knowledge that needs to be
collected. There is also a need to map what considerations and assessments that are
needed to be made to evaluate a product. Without this it will be difficult to maintain an
overview of the products going into the take back system. Such mappings will support in
building and consolidating the knowledge internally for old products ­ and ultimately
create the possibility for breaking even with an environmentally based take back system.
Several types of decision support tools were considered in the early phases.
Spreadsheet could be used to support the calculations of evaluating the scrap values,
sustainability impacts etc.. However these are poor at facilitate the multidisciplinary
discussions, insights and qualitative considerations. Spreadsheets can quickly become
hard to manage. It is also unclear how to support the disassembly planning in a
meaningful way in excel sheets. Given the large of different expertise required to
evaluate if a product should be taken back, spreadsheets were found to be a bad type of
boundary object to facilitate these assessments. The same goes for custom scripts or
programs to help evaluate these things. As these could be even less accessible.
Given that a lot of different information needed to be unified and discussed in relation to
each other, a poster seemed to be a good fit. It was however not without some
significant downsides. Especially simplified cost and environmental analysis would be
somewhat tedious to update. However the easier overview, more approachable format,
and ability to include more explanatory graphical elements, to explain structural
elements to new actors was deemed important.
Originally the early posters were create to try to condense and create an overview of the
analysis we had made. It was quickly becoming difficult to communicate both the new
elements (take back system configurations) with the costing and environmental focus of
the products. This led to try to map how to redo the analysis and what elements of the
analysis would remain fairly static. More than 10 different poster iterations were tried out
and they are briefly described in the section A.5.
Most iterations were tried in the team, and when major revisions were undertaken they
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
103
==PAGE:114==
were discussed with the external thesis supervisor. The ’final’ iteration was presented
and tried out with the external thesis supervisor and an internal UX researcher, to get
fresh input on it. This led to the stronger focus on on­boarding participants and
asynchronous preparation for the workshop ­ which greatly improved the final poster.
Some of the key challenges in designing the poster were
1. How to plan and quantify semi destructive disassembly
2. What practical considerations to have for the product
3. Who in the organisation could provide the necessary information to answer each
element
4. How to select what components and parts to focus on (i.e., COI and structural
elements)
5. Costing, how to price COI and share costs with OEMs
1.
Given the size of the portfolio and the dissatisfaction with current techniques, we
wanted a more approachable way of planning semi destructive product disassembly.
After a few unsatisfactory attempts with disassembly trees/graphs, a low practical
approach of using product photographs was tried out. This allow made it easy to also
get a practical understanding of each product, where component it are located, how it
can be removed, what implications that has for further treatment and just an overall
deeper understanding of a product that participants may not have worked with ­ or seen
in a long time.
2.
Leaning up against frameworks like Mallick et al., 2023; Larsen et al., 2022; De Brito
and Dekker, 2004, provided a good overview of considerations for products. These were
filtered based on the discussions and experiences with Viessmann. The focus was also
altered, as the new poster assumes one or more take back systems have been
implemented. These same considerations also outlined the points in the discussion and
conclusion.
3.
A lot of data is required, and it was clear that quite a lot of preparation was required
to evaluate a product. In the testing with Viessmann, it was found that this need was
even greater than anticipated. Many participants maybe only be able to provide a single
piece of information, or relevant for only one step of the process ­ but be critical for the
assessment. Given the size of the enterprise, it was therefore important to support
asynchronous preparation.
4.
A key thing in these products is prioritising the efforts to the most important
components. But how does on select those components? In the project up until this
point the focus had been somewhat implicit ­ we knew what components OEMs had
expressed interest in, and existing LCIA showed what components/materials were the
most polluting. But for other products and categories this might vary. Therefore multiple
different ’lenses’ were used to establish the COI. Both their scrap value, OEM interest &
plans, environmental impact.
5.
The costing was found to be fairly straight forward, when all the elements are
known. However knowing what is scrap, what is COI, what the logistics costs etc..
requires a lot of different data, insights and considerations. Finally, breaking even and
104
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:115==
profiting requires, sharing the costs, selling components to OEMs, finding internal reuse
for components or all of the above. Different OEMs old components will have
significantly different values to them, environmentally and as scrap. OEMs Cost sharing
preferences will therefore vary and discussing how to share the costs to break even and
profiting, is therefore a key element.

6
Evaluation of solutions ­ Which take
back system has the most potential?
6.1
Selection of solutions
To support the assessment of solutions, there was a need to simplify the full solutions
presented in the previous chapter, to its main elements. In discussion with the contact
person and our prior work, 4 main take back system configurations were identified ­
which were significantly different and deserved more extensive analysis and
consideration. The main benefits of each solutions are described below. The selection
process was an iterative concept synthesis using the qualitative insights from phone
surveys, weekly reviews, the use of criteria, and through the authors’ design experience
and personal opinion. The process being done internally it has the limitation of being
rather opaque. However, the advantage is that the selection is less subject to long
discussions and thus, relatively fast.
1. Incentivised component recovery
Instead of taking back the whole heating
systems, the installers and possibly scrap dealers would be incentivised to remove
components from the heating systems, bulk them in large bags/plastic containers, and
then a 3PLS would pick them up. This solution is attractive as it drastically reduces the
main cost driver, logistics costs whilst achieving the bulk of the environmental benefits.
2. Bulk collection
Here installers will transport heating systems home, bulk them in a
8­10 ft container, fitting between 10­25 heating systems and request a pickup from a
3PLS free of charge. The containers will be replaced, to minimise handling time of
heating systems, as storing that amount of heating systems requires stacking them.
3. Kerbside collection
Here heating systems will be left on the kerb at the clients
house, installers will notify the take back system and a 3PLS will come by as fast as
possible to pick up the heating system. The installers would then forego any possible
earnings on the heating systems in exchange for the increased convenience.
4. Marking heating systems
Here installers will mark with a large marker, what
product model was replace. Then by changing their waste management practice to
taking heating systems to a recycling centre or having it picked up by a local PRO. This
requires the heating systems to be sorted as white goods at the recycling centre. PROs
will then have a positive list of products where select components are interesting. The
rest will undergo the PROs standard treatment. Viessmann and other brands will then
pay for the increased treatment and recovery of components. But this is akin to Mieles
take back system of white goods. This solution can possibly be expanded to scrap
dealers, whoever the product integrity is hard to ensure. Please note this would require
gas boilers also be sorted as white goods at recycling yards. This solutions was not
evaluated quantitatively.
Finally the four solutions have been illustrated below to outline the main differences:
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
107
==PAGE:118==
Figure 6.1: Figure illustration the new waste management routes. Note the PRO was not
considered in depth.
6.2
Qualitative evaluation
This section will first focus on the solutions selected for quantitative solution, diving in
more depth with installers, Viessmann and our thoughts on them.
Installers preferences
This should be explored in more depth and systematically ­
and for every market the take back system is expanded into. A limited amount of mostly
danish installers were surveyed over two rounds. Most only willing to spend a few
minutes discussing the options.
The belief in this being feasible and want to change habits varies a lot. Installers were
generally split in two groups: one groups willing to accommodate a new kind of disposal,
and the others not interested in changing their practices. The response to taking back
old heating systems ranged from ”that is entirely possible, just a change of habits” to ”I
don’t have time” but then spending 15 minutes explaining intricately why they did not see
that being feasible. Some also expressed handling some products differently to bee too
complicated. It is worth noting that many HVAC companies deal with a lot more than gas
and heat pump products. This makes the predominant waste riddance through mixed
metal containers all the more persuasive. Some installers already deal with a lot of
waste fractions, separating plastics, cardboard, refrigerants etc. and are therefore wary
of increasing the complexity (#Installers 2023/05/16, see subsection 3.2.3).
Incentivised component recovery
Incentivised recovery is preferred by the installers
phone surveyed. However all but one would require their wage to be paid, which would
make it an expensive option ­ and susceptible to misuse. However in practice it may be
possible to incentives participation with a monetary reward for some components,
pulling on installers goodwill for the environment /co­branding effect or similar. The
installers were surveyed outside of peak heating systems season, and actual
participation may differ in the busy late summer / autumn period. 3 installers reported
being part of the Grundfos circulation pump system. However the more components that
has to be removed the bigger the task becomes, which could divide the installers into
108
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:119==
the ones interested in doing it and the rest. It also makes pickup of components more
complicate, unless the systems are unified.
Bulk collection
40­60% surveyed had room for either a container or room outside for
storing heating systems separately. The main concern here were the significant amount
of installers uninterested in having to handle heating systems differently, and it would
complicate their waste handling. One installer did have room outside for heating
systems, and was sorting them there, until a scrap dealer picked them up. They had
experienced multiple times people breaking in to get copper and similar. There is
therefore a concern of theft, and access management.
Kerbside collection
Very few of the surveyed installers were keen on having heating
systems collected at clients homes. Their main concern was that end clients would not
be interested in having the heating system laying around when they left the site. The
reason being it was their reputation on the line, and installations being a critical part of
the customers experience of the quality of their services. Several installers also noted
that they doubted the profitability and realism of the economics of the logistics.
Add­on solutions
Positively inclined installers responding positively for scanning and
handling some heating systems different based on this. The same installers also sees
drop­off solutions as an option. However their actual behaviour is likely different
depending on the execution of those ideas. The density of drop off locations will
naturally play a role, and so will the quality of the scanning app. With drop off locations,
the main driver for this solution in practice is likely to be the density of drop off locations
and how much out of their way they would have to go.
Viessmann preference
As this project has covered and attempted to analyse, it is the environmental and
economical factors deciding whether it will be feasible for Viessmann to go beyond the
obligatory waste management. Taking back heating systems is rightfully understood as
a difficult and potentially expensive option, and very product and region category
dependent. Their preference was understood as the solution with the largest
environmental impact delta ­ but that was feasible to implement.
Qualitative system dynamics
To provide and transfer some qualitative understanding
of the take back systems at this point, two system diagrams were constructed, based on
our understanding of the topic. These help capture some of the interrelations in an easy
to understand graph, illustrating what solution elements and factor impact the two key
aspects of the system: economical viability and sustainability.
From the profit per heating system diagram, Figure 6.2 the main insights were: 1. That
disassembly planning and heuristics can lead to more efficient treatment time. 2. That
circulating more components (component retrieval in the diagram) reduces the required
treatment time ­ but requires planning. 3. Sharing the benefits may incur a cost, as each
component represents potential scrap value ­ but is the more sustainable option. 4.
There is an need for OEMs participation to guide disassembly planning. 5. Finding more
avenues for upcycling & reuse gives more credits.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems

==PAGE:120==
Figure 6.2: Figure illustrating the design teams qualitative understanding of the factors
impact profitability per heating system, red arrows = negative impact, green arrows =
positive impact, blue arrows = possibly both negative and positive impact (positive and
negative impacts on the business case)
For the sustainability diagram, Figure 6.3, the main insights were: 1. Capture rate plays
an important role on the total sustainability, which hinges on the convenience of
especially the logistics setup. 2. Different logistics setups can incur more new transport.
3. Information sharing from installers can improve some treatment options but possibly
reduce participation.

6.3
Quantitative evaluation of solutions
The overarching flow of the solutions that will be modelled is presented in Figure 6.1. As
discussed in section 4.4, the main goal of the quantitative evaluation has been to outline
the limits and general tendencies between the solutions ­ to see what should be
investigated in pilot projects, evaluated and then scaled up.
The key scenarios to be investigated across all simulation methods here are:
1. Heat pumps vs boilers in various mixes
2. Incentivised recovery vs. bulk collection vs. kerbside collection
6.3.1
DES modelling of TOBE
Just like the baseline modelling section, this will follow the Rosetti methodology, outlined
in the the literature review. The baseline and TOBE models are conducted in the same
anylogic model, and can be mixed. This is however not done as the goal was to do a
comparative analysis of the different options. The same transport function, agent setups
etc. are used here.
The modelling consists of two different problems, 1. the inbound logistics setups
and 2. the treatment facility. Quite a few parameters are used, and there is a lot of
shared parameters. The varied ones are mention in the graphs. The full overview of the
parameters and the ones varied, is available in appendix section A.3.
6.3.2
DES, TOBE, inbound logistics
Phase 1 ­ problem formulation
As outlined in the qualitative evaluation of ideas, the most interesting and feasible ways
of taking back heating systems appear to be: 1. Kerbside collection, 2. Incentivised
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
111
==PAGE:122==
recovery and 3. Bulk collection form installers companies. The goal of this simulation is
what the tradeoffs are for the different actors for these systems in terms of: time spent,
environmental delta and support economical assessment.
Data gathering
For the inbound logistics there were a few different missing datapoints for each setup.
The key ones are discussed here. Values and the remaining data are described in the
appendix.
Bulk collection
The transport distance from installer companies to a logistics buffer.
This very much depends on the provider selected, and their network density and route
planning. Both the more urban placement of HVAC companies and regularity of pickups
makes route optimisation easier, reducing transport distance. It was therefore assumed
to be on average, twice the scrap dealer collection distance ­ which were partly based
on survey and map distances.
Kerbside collection
The transport distance from installer companies to a logistics
buffer. This very much depends on the provider selected, and their network density and
route planning. It is however assumed to be significantly higher than bulk collection, due
to the large spread of diversity of locations, making route optimisation harder. It was
therefore assumed to be on average, twice the bulk collection distance.
Incentivised recovery
Time estimates for removing components of interests, was a
key factor here. Here reliance on the surveys, phone surveys and scrapping video study
helped choose values. Most installers also expressed only removing components and
materials from heating systems if they had the time. As installation of heating systems is
very seasonal there might be a hard limit on the amount of time they can use, and want
to use. Given that there only are 2­4 COI per HS, the effect of the max time is fairly
limited as 20­40 minutes is sufficient to remove them.
The amount of components the installers are able to storage, and how often to pick them
up. The packing density depends on: size, weight, need for product protection and if
they can be mixed.. Keeping products separate and having to pack them safely makes it
much harder to get installers to participate. Careful takeback packaging design can help
alleviate this and support component upcycling.
In modelling it was simply assumed to be 23 items per container (e.g., a big bag, as
used for small construction waste), and assuming they can be mixed. A lot of the new
components that could be taken back are large, like the expansion vessels and heat
exchangers. This is overly simplistic. Other simple ways would be adding a size
attribute to each component type/instance. For reference, the heating system products
modelled all have 2­4 COI installers can remove, this results in a container lasting 5­10
HS removals, or more depending on the time spent by installers and the heating
systems in the model.
Phase 2 ­ Simulation model building
The simulation model is fairly straightforward using the same transport functions, and
agents from the baseline model. The main challenge being handling incentivised
component removal of included and not included heating systems.
The Incentivised removal function
is modelled using drawing a maximum time
installer will use from a distribution. The looping through the highest value COI in the HS
112
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:123==
and removing components until the max time is reached. The parts are then bulked in
the same container, and the remains are put in the mixed metal container.
Parameters
The main parameters used to control and explore the inbound logistics
were: 1. flow control parameters, 2. capacity of containers for COI and heating systems,
3. mix of heating systems, 4. the amount of time installers are willing to spend removing
components.
Figure 6.4: Simplified activity flow diagram for the inbound logistics setups simulated.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
113
==PAGE:124==
Phase 3 ­ Experimental design and analysis
As the main goal of the new system is comparative, each flow was run independently by
setting its parameters to 0 or 1. Mixes were also explored, but not explored extensively.
The mix of heating systems was also varied for each flow, as in the baseline simulation.
Mixed scenarios are also limited as the student version of Anylogic limits on amount of
agents, makes it hard to achieve satisfactory standard deviations for the results ­ as few
agents (heating systems) pass through all flows. This cannot be solved properly with
replications ­ only by adding more agents or changing the modelling approach.
Phase 4 ­ Evaluation and iteration
Generally the model is very stable, and requires few replications (15­20 replications) to
produce small standard deviations for the key KPIs models, as long as it is run in
min/max configurations targeting single flows. Standard deviations generally being in
the range of 3­10% of the mean value, and most KPIs being linearly correlated with the
varied parameters.
Results
As the inbound logistics and facility treatment are intertwined some of these
results will also be discussed in relation to the facility treatment, in subsection 6.3.7. The
main points for Figure 6.5 will are: 1. Kerbside collection is by far the priciest, mostly
due to the associated labour costs. Bulk collection is 2/3 of the labour time. 2. Mixing all
inbound logistics setups results in the highest costs. 3. Installers spending more time
has little influence on the amount of recovered components, as currently most COI can
be retied in less than 50 minutes. Keeping in mind the total time to remove all COI will
be important. 4. Incentivised recovery is much cheaper in terms of logistics, but yields a
low proportion of steel (expected) but quite significant proportions of the aluminium and
copper. 5. Bulk collection appears to be the most viable solution, as it reduces costs,
maximises COI and material retrieval, whilst keeping installer time usage low.
(a) Comparison of inbound logistics routes.
(b) Comparison of waste routes, time spent
by actors.
Figure 6.5: Main graphs of inbound logistics.
Phase 5 ­ Experimentation
One of the experiments were to investigate if other brands systems would impact the
logistics significantly. The results get a little unstable due to the limit on agents in the
model. Please note the graphs count number of discarded heating systems, not
handled/taken­back systems. The main take aways from this experiment were that
accepting other brands linearly increase the amount of time and pickups required.

6.3.3
DES, TOBE, facility simulation
Phase 1 ­ problem formulation
The main goals was determining the amount of work required to handle a significant
portion of interesting systems.
Parameters
The main to investigate the facility are: 1. The mix of incentivised
component recovery vs. bulk recovery. 2. The process efficiency ratio for dismantling
and treatment. 3. The amount of time to max spend on removing components.
Data gathering
A key issue was determining the realistic dismantling and removal times for each
heating system and components. To simplify this the same values used in the
incentivised recovery were used (survey, phone survey and video study (#Survey 2023
b, #Scrappers 2023, #Installers 2023/05/16). As it is assumed the efficiency of removing
components can be sped up significantly in a facility setting compared to in the field for
installers, the process time is half of the values used in incentivised recovery. This is
deemed realistic as this put the total time to remove the COI in the same range as the
small video study on independent scrap dealer practices today. The process efficiency
ratio is then further increases on top of that.
The capacity of the facility is entirely dependent on the participation rate, the regions that
system is offered in and the decommission rate of heating systems. To gauge the
potential amount of decommissioned heating systems, it was extrapolated from the
current installation rate of heat pumps, assuming a participation of 33%, this would yield
circa 270 heating systems per week day. This would theoretical peak in 2030, with circa
1150 heating systems per day, w. 33% rate. With full participation the upper bounds
being circa 900 in 2023, and 3850 in 2030. After the peak it may flatten out in 2045 at
600­800.000 installations a year, yielding circa 2300­3000 HS daily. This assumes full
participation and all heat pump installations results in heating system being discarded.
In the DES model, the model creates individual products, 300 per work day. But as heat
pump systems consists of an outdoor and indoor unit, this results in a 150 ’full’ heating
systems per day, due to the way agents are created in the model. This corresponds to
circa 15% of Germanys heat pump installations in 2023.
(German Heatpump Association, 2023)
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
115
==PAGE:126==
Phase 2 ­ Simulation model building
As there has not been taken basis in a specific facility, and the goals of the quantitative
analysis, the main goal has been to see if it is realistic to fully treat the heating systems
in 30­60 minutes. It is therefore a rather rudimentary facility simulation, with simplistic
handling seizing of agents and estimation of times.
Figure 6.7: Simplified facility flow diagram of the treatment facility
Phase 3 ­ Experimental design and analysis
Multiple experiments were conducted for the facility, mainly the influence of dismantling
time reduction (both initial removal of COI and non COI.) AS it was quickly found to be
the largest contributor to total facility time per heating system. The full use of parameters
116
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:127==
is available in the appendix, however a limited amount of comparisons are presented,
and a limited amount of full comparisons are available.
Phase 4 ­ Evaluation and iteration
The model is simplistic but does illustrate some key points.
1. That a significant proportion of the time is spent on handling, sorting and packing
systems, scrap and COI.
2. That the limited amount of COI today, results in dismantling of non COI
components a large time sink.
3. Process speedup for removal of COI flattens out quickly.
4. The more COI, the less time needs to be spent dismantling components.
5. That it is realistic to treat heating systems in 40­50 minutes ­ including facility
handling.
6. That heat pumps take longer to dismantle due to more components in total.
7. The outdoor unit currently have few COI, bringing down the COI ratio.
8. Incentivised component retrieval makes the time spent per HS and per COI much
less. However this is partly due to the lack of information on COI treatment, and
assumed fairly destructive disassembly of them. This should be explored in more
depth if chosen.
Explanation of the measures
1. COI ratio is the amount of COI per discarded heating system.
2. Dismantling time accounts for both removal of components and splitting parts into
its raw materials .
3. Treatment time per COI only covers the treatment of COI after removal.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
117
==PAGE:128==
(a) Effect of COI removal time on facility
time, per HS
(b) Effect of COI removal time on facility
time, per COI
(c) Heat pumps vs boilers per COI
(d) Heat pumps vs boilers per HS
Figure 6.8: DES plots of rudimentary facility analysis 1/2 .
Discussion of the TOBE models
The logistics and facility models have a lot of shortcomings and results should be treated
with a lot of caution. However a few key conclusions can be drawn:
1. Focusing on smart downcycling/shredding of non COI can drastically reduce time
per heating system. As most of the time is dismantling non COI. This yields more
scrap value.
2. Minimising handling is key. Evaluating if it is necessary to sort heating systems at
arrival would yield significant reduction here. Other things, like how much time is
spent on storing any item, from scrap to components is key too.
3. It is possible to reduce the time per HS to 40­50 minutes, if not even significantly
less, if handling and dismantling is optimised.
4. Bulk recovery and current baseline waste management is very comparable for
installers. It will save time for the installers taking heating systems to the recycling
yard.
118
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:129==

per COI

Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
119
==PAGE:130==
Why not go in more depth?
The current analysis is very uncertain and uses a lot of
very low quality data. Adding upon this was not found to valuable use of time, as it was
clear that some heating systems may not be valuable enough and that driving OEM
participation would be key. A more detailed analysis with the data available would not
address this. Quite a lot of results, especially logistics, were also found to be linear,
which at significant scale is confirmed in literature. In the project focus was now put on
finding ways of more easily repeating parts of this assessment and evaluating products.
6.3.4
LCA comparison of TOBE scenarios
This part of the LCA aims to compare the three solution scenarios with the baseline that
was defined in section 3.4.6. Here again the three mix of products are considered but
the main one for the comparison is the best estimation mix 1, with 50% of gas boiler and
50% of heat pumps. The difference in the flow compared to baseline is the treatment
phase in the decommissioning that includes the energy consumption form power tool
use to dismantle the products (figure 6.10). The same assumed power of 100 Watts is
multiplied by the time of treatment given by the results of the corresponding DES (see
section 6.3.1).
There is also an added component treatment and avoided production due to the reuse of
most of it. This is defined after the findings in sections 2.1.2 and 3.4.6.
All other parameters are updated using the corresponding DES results (time, ton.km,
amount of material ending in each flows). The detailed values and LCI are to be found in
appendix A.2, table A.6.
Figure 6.10: LCA flow TOBE

the product taken in account. This result is very much in line with the literature findings
in section 2.1.2. However, all components are not remanufactured and the actual
potential benefits from the best estimate scenario is much lower.

Figure 6.17: Environmental performance of scenarios normalized to baseline mix 2

The uncertainties are combining parameter uncertainty and method uncertainty. The
process was to take the input parameters uncertainties as geometric standard deviation
[GSD2(input)] and run the model with one at a time being to its maximum value . Then
the uncertainty contribution method was applied defining their sensitivity in relation with
the resulting output with the equation: S = (δoutput/output)/(δinput/input). The
variance was then calculated using the formula: var(input) = S2 ∗ln(GSDinput)2. The
sum of all parameter variance was then taken var(ouptut) = Σ(var(input)) and their
contribution could be calculated by taking var(input)/var(output).
Figure 3.23 shows that the main uncertainty contributions are the remanufacturing rate,
collection rates and the heavier components.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
125
==PAGE:136==
Figure 6.19: The main uncertainty contributions, cut­off 2%
The method contribution was calculated by running the LCA model with ILCD 2011
midpoint+, EN15804+A2, and Impact2002+. The combined parameter and method
uncertainties were found to be 13.8% (+5% ; ­8.8%). This means that the results are just
at the edge of being significant. Using the Monte Carlo simulation method would have
given more accurate uncertainties. However, the modelling was incompatible with it’s
use as there are some known issues in the software that were preventing it to function
well.
The results are very similar across the solution scenarios (8%­11% savings compared to
baseline for mix 1 with 50% boiler and 50% heat pumps), which means that
environmentally speaking, there would not be a better solution than another. However, it
is probable that the scenarios still have higher environmental benefits than the baseline.
This also means that any solution including heat pumps in the mix, is better than the
current situation. As mentioned, the heat pumps have more potential than boilers to
drive environmental savings by remanufacturing. Indeed, they have materials with a
larger carbon footprint than the latter. The results also show that, as literature says,
there is between 60% and 80% CO2 emissions savings potential in remanufacturing and
more generally speaking, upcycling products. There are of course some limits and given
the condition of the product it might become more beneficial to just recycle it at some
point. However, a more detailed and targeted study should be done at the component
specific level to find out that limit.
6.3.5
Eco­effectiveness of the solution ­ how does it weight on the entire
balance.
Taking a quick look at the global situation can help understanding the magnitude of the
savings made by the proposed solution. It is a good thing that the potential take back
systems enables to reduce the environmental impact of heating systems production by
around 8%­11%, but is it enough? This section attempts to answer with calculation on
the back of an envelope, the importance of the gains not only for the net­zero targets,
but for ecosystems to stay in the planetary boundaries.
126
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:137==
What is the heating system safe operating space?
As said in section 2.1.2, the ecosystem can be seen with planetary boundaries that shall
not be crossed. (Bjørn and Hauschild, 2015; Ryberg et al., 2018; Ryberg et al., 2021;
Steffen et al., 2015b; Hauschild, 2015; Bjorn et al., 2020; Ryberg et al., 2020) These
papers give a method and data necessary to calculate the probability that a given
product or system crosses the boundaries. for simplicity, only the global warming
boundary will be taken in account here. The method consists of allocating a share of the
total CO2 emissions of the product of it’s life cycle emissions. The share is calculated
with the value that people give to the use and acquisition of the product or service. It can
be monetary allocation, or other types of value allocation. The main ones used here are
the price of a heating system (buying and using it over the life time), and the current
share of the emissions from heating systems for German people. (Ryberg et al., 2021)
gives the global share of global warming available (+1 Wm­2) before passing through
the planetary boundary called a safe operating space (SOS). It is then related to the
amount of the German population compared to the global population. This is the first
step attributes a share of the safe operating space to every German based on an
equitable distribution to the world population. It is the calculated how much does the
heating service weighs on the Germans wallets (Value to human, VTH) or how much it
contributes to their carbon footprint (Grand fathering, GF). This allocates a part of the
share of the safe operating space (SoSOS) available per person. When combining the
peoples’ share with the products’ share, it gives the the amount of used safe operating
space allocated to that product.
Figure 6.20 shows that the heating systems use between 20 and 160 times their
allocated share of the safe operating space for global warming (above the threshold). It
is also to be noted that the baseline, the scenarios and even the full potential hypothesis
savings have little influence on the results for the life cycle of the product. It is then not
possible to only recycle and remanufacture materials if one aims to go back to a safe
operating space. However, it can be noted that the full potential of remanufacturing
would probably bring only the share of the material impact below the threshold. The
main contributor being the use phase, it is important to reduce its consumption. This is
possible by improving the efficiency, isolating the houses, and reducing the heating
behaviours. The other way would be to have a less carbon intensive electricity grid, but
that is only applicable for heat pumps.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
127
==PAGE:138==
Figure 6.20: Use of the share of safe operating space by the heating systems materials
6.3.6
Costing of TOBE solutions
Logistics costs
This is the hardest part of this project to estimate, as there is a lot of uncertainty, both in
terms of what setup is chosen, what brands are accepted, and the 3PLS provider
selected. There are large studies focusing on minimising the logistics costs, how to
setup reverse logistic networks etc.. this is outside the scope of this project. The goal
here is to find the approximate cost or at the least the different scales of costs for each
setup.
Extrapolation of similar transportation systems
The costing of the logistics costs is
very uncertain. The costing of picking up products is very regionally dependent. A case
study of Norwegian WEEE collection prices found that rural and urban costs can vary
with almost a factor 10 (Bø and Baxter, 2017, 2020), and the main cost was contributor
being wages. This was used to extrapolate in relation to different containers and the two
extremes: urban collection and very rural collection of containers, illustrated below
Figure 6.21: Extrapolation of logistics costs as a function of container size.
128
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:139==
Mass, distance and volume based estimates
Due to the lack of direct contact with
logistics provider companies, the best approach for estimating these costs were to ask
for online calculated quotes. These estimates are assumed to be high because neither a
contextual approach nor special agreements exist between the two parties. It is also
based on a single trip calculation and not a reoccurring contract.
The platform used to estimate the prices is freightos (Freightos, 2023) that enable to
provide online quotes of transport services based on the size, volume and number of
item per shipment. The amount of products per shipment was based on assumed
number of heating systems per transport in the DES for the pick­up transport from
installers’ place (2) and the bulked­up transport after logistics buffers (10). The values
used for the products size and mass are to be found in appendix A.2, table A.3. Even
though the distance is slightly changing among the scenarios runs, they have a good
consistency which allowed to use average values that would enable to do a limited
amount of quotes as they are to be done manually. To get the distance in the calculation,
the delivery place was set to Allendorf and the pick­up was set to a town being at the
desired distance from the Allendorf.

Discussion of logistics costs
It is clear that logistics costs, no matter the evaluation
method, can wary much depending on the region and provider. However it does appear
to be possible to recoup the logistics cost mostly in scrap value for most of the setups.
This leaves the treatment costs to be reduced and partly covered by cooperation with
OEMs.
There are ways to estimate more closely the actual logistic costs. It would be
recommended to contact and discuss deal possibilities with potential logistics partners
which was limited in our case. The granularity could also be improved by taking more
steps in distance averages which would enable to decide on the radius that a treatment
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
129
==PAGE:140==
facility or a logistic buffer can cover.
Facility costs
The labour costs are taken as a simple time per heating system multiplied by the wage
17.63 euro /hour, the source for the wage being “SalaryExpert”, 2023. However to
achieve a feasible processing time, and handle circa 15% of decommissioned heating
systems in Germany, this would require a workforce of minimum 5­7. This is fairly
optimistic, and requires parallelization of tasks. If one assumes no parallelization this
works out to an average processing time of 19­25 minutes per heating system. However
many tasks can be run in parallel or in bulk, enabling a significantly greater degree of
efficiency. See appendix on extrapolations for calculations and DES facility model for the
investigation on processing time per HS. Energy usage, equipment, capital depreciation,
capital expenditure and similar has not been considered for the facility.
6.3.7
Costing of different scenarios
Data on how much various OEMs want to pay for their old components has been
unclear, and from early in the project it has been clear that this is necessary for the
system to be viable. Instead of assuming the components value, it has been estimated
what the required earnings would need to be per heating system ­ and average per
component assuming equal cost sharing.
There are a lot of ’moving parts’. All scenarios shown below take basis in 50% heat
pumps / boilers.
1. Bulk collection, with 30 minutes max removal of COI in facility.
2. Bulk collection, with 50 minutes max removal of COI in the facility.
3. Incentivised recovery, installers spending max 50 minutes removing COI.
4. Incentivised recovery, installers spending max 70 minutes removing COI.
5. Kerbside collection, 50 minutes max removal of COI in facility
6. Kerbside collection 70 minutes max removal of COI in facility
7. Bulk+inc max 50 minutes both places
8. Bulk+inc max 70 minutes both places
9. Even Mix of all logistics setup (bulk, kerbside & incentivised recovery), 50 minutes
at facility.
The main take aways from the graph below are:
1. Bulk collection is preferable
2. At scale and with significant optimisations, it is feasible to recover the currently
identified COI within the time frame.
3. Bulk collection results in significantly lower installer time usage compared to
incentivised recovery, with only 50% higher average COI price.
4. Only bulk collection and incentivised recovery yield what we would assume to be
realistic COI average prices.
5. Mixing the inbound logistics costs, appears to result in much higher costs. this
could however be the model note reaching a stability due to not enough HS being
simulated.

6. Selling the remaining scrap contributes to a large part of the potential benefits and
thus, separating the fractions is recommended.

would be ready to pay for the components at all and, in the case they would, how high
would the price be. That being said, there is a potential of breaking even and profiting
given these results.
