7
The proposed solution ­ Bulk collection
7.1
Solution description
As covered in our evaluation of the solutions, there is lot of uncertainty in taking back
heating systems, and even breaking even is hard. Therefore our solution consists of
three elements:
1. A recommended solution architecture for taking back heating system
2. An implementation plan ­ how to start taking back
3. A co­creation poster to help plan and evaluate how to take back heating systems
Together these three elements provide an overview of how to approach taking back
heating systems and plan the efforts for every single product and product category.
7.1.1
The recommended solution for a successful take back system
The solution will likely require slightly different approaches depending on the region.
However the predominant solution, at scale ( 15% of heating systems replaced by heat
pumps in Germany) would work as presented in figure 7.1).
Figure 7.1: Solution illustration.
1. Installers decommissions the heating system as usual, being that they transport it
back with them to their company’s home/base of operation.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
133
==PAGE:144==
2. Depending on the amount of space available they will either store it as they see fit
or have a ’small’ 10 foot closed container to bulk the heating systems. This is in
addition to their other waste management obligations. With room for 10­20 HS,
depending on the HS accepted and their stack­ability.
(a) The ones without the space for a small container, can either be accepted as a
lost leakage, or collect an amount around 2­3 heating systems, before they
run out of storage space. Highlighting the importance of target/intelligent
product recovery (IPR)
3. When they container is full the installer company informs Viessmann, that their
container is ready for pickup, and a 3rd party logistics’s provider (3PLS), will pick
up the heating systems / container
4. The 3PLS will transport the container/heating systems to a logistics buffer and
transfer them to a 20 foot container. Leaving room for around 30­60 heating
systems, depending on how stackable they are, and the size of heating systems
accepted.
5. When they container is full, it will be emptied and transported to the treatment
facility. Currently imagined to be at Allendorf or a new facility.
6. To operate at this scale, it will require 5­7 full time workers.
7. First step is sorting the heating systems into new containers depending on the
heating system type, as efficient dismantling requires product uniformity.
8. When a product category’s container is full or time affords it, the product treatment
begins. (Please note this process differs significantly for different product
categories)
9. First the outer chassis is removed. Then the product is broken into modules with
angle grinders / pilot arc plasma cutters. The modules are stored in large bins for
bulk processing, and easy moving around.
10. Components are then removed and stored in bins sorted by type. Note the larger
the variety in heating systems and components, the more time is spent on handling
11. When components of interest, have been accumulated in bulk, they are then are
inspected, sorted and possibly treated. Parts of interest are sent back to their
OEM. Components not of interest whose material composition makes it either
economically or environmentally interesting, are broken down into its pure scrap
materials, in bulk.
12. In average for the gas condensing boilers considered, 2 components were of
interest to send back. For heat pumps the average was 3.5. Driving interest from
OEMs and finding ways of sharing the cost of their products end of life is very
important, for this to become viable.
13. The required earnings from COI to break even per heating systems is 20€,
resulting in an average COI price of 9 € per COI. An average of 50 € is earned
from dismantling the heating systems into its raw materials. It yields a reduction of
around 8% for global warming, and only 3% of energy consumption reduction
compared to the baseline.
The solution has also been outlined as a service blueprint, presented below. Service
blueprints as a design methods are described in depth by (NNGroup, 2023).
134
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:145==
Figure 7.2: Solution as a service blue print
As is evident from the cost analysis, taking back heating systems is a costly affair. Even
with significantly cheaper logistics costs, labour costs in replacing heating systems is
significant. It is not economically viable to do this without multiple interested OEMs and
potentially internal interest in reusing components.
One of the key competences to develop in the treatment facility is continuous
improvement and finding innovative ways of quickly dismantling the heating systems.
This can be in the form of custom jigs, tools and embedding a culture of continuous
improvement in the facility.
A constant trade­off is the amount of time to spend vs. the increased value of the
discarded heating system. However in this analysis it was found a significant amount of
revenue and reduction of environmental impact comes from also dismantling
components not of interest into its’ raw materials. It should also be within possibility, if
effort is put in speeding up this process.
7.1.2
Wicked uncertainty and how to adapt this solution proposal
This study is limited in many ways as discussed in more depth in sectionsection 8.2, and
the problem being wicked section 1.5, the solution proposal will need to be adapted and
understanding the intricacies and dynamics of it, is necessary for it to be valuable.
The most attractive ways to adapt the system:
1. Intelligent product recovery
2. Drop­off areas
3. Just in time reverse logistics
4. Incentivised component recovery
Intelligent product recovery
Sorting at the source is generally preferable in any waste management system. By
installers scanning/looking up the heating system before/when decommissioning offers a
lot flexibility. Synergising with incentivised component recovery and just in time reverse
logistics.
Accepting all brands
This analysis is very limited in what components OEMs are interested in and in scope of
considered heating systems. It was found that the system can only break even and
become profitable with the OEMs sharing the costs. Which requires negotiating with
OEMs, and here it is significantly easier for Viessmanns own products. The existing
relationships with the OEMs and large orders makes it easier to find innovative ways of
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
135
==PAGE:146==
splitting the cost, i.e., rebates on future products. It will simply require more effort to
establish these relationships and find the OEMs for competitors products.
Whiteout the OEMs participation the only way to increase their value is dismantling into
its’ scrap materials, which is unviable. However when there is success with Viessmanns
own products, they can use this as an example and blueprint for taking back heating
systems, and offer this as a service to competitors. Having multiple competing take back
systems, complicates the riddance process for the installers and will likely result in
leakage of heating systems and ultimately sub­optimal end of lives. The long term goal
should therefore be accepting all brands.
Incentivised component recovery
Making installers and or scrap dealers remove select components, results in significantly
cheaper parts of interest and yields the main environmental benefits, assuming that they
will be remanufactured (6.3). It is however limited what components are suitable for this,
and if the gains are realised. Circulation pumps and some heat exchangers are well
suited for this.
Inspecting, sorting and treating just components is also easier to outsource and requires
significantly less storage space. It does requires installers/scrap dealers to scan heating
systems, and user their time to remove components. From surveys, the amount of
estimated time to remove components varies a lot.
Removing components from what technically/legally is waste, also currently requires
certifications. A problem that Miele has run in to consistently with their take­back of
electronic components (#Miele 2023). It is possible this can be circumvented or
exceptions can be made, as heating systems are replaced before failure. I.e., making
the argument for not viewing it as waste before the components are removed, because
the system is still functional. However legal experts should be consulted in this area.
Incentivised component recovery is not attractive for all installers, but for some installer
companies it may be the best option, if they are unable to bulk up heating systems.
There are possibly also ways of framing this task as valuable, besides compensating the
installers for their efforts. E.g., this task can offer apprentices a lot of experience in how
to quickly, safely and carefully remove components ­ making them faster in servicing
heating systems.
Minimising logistics costs
Due to the large size and current disposal route, the logistics of getting a hold of
products is likely going to be the main expenditure. In this study, only 3PLS were
considered quantitatively, picking up from many different installer companies or kerbside
collection from end users residences. These are expensive options, as handling heating
systems is time consuming, and picking up in small bulks is expensive.
There are several other logistics setups than can be run in tandem, to reduce the
expensive options. Some of these can also ensure safe storage and give installers more
flexibility.
Just in time reverse logistics
As Viessmann is a premium brand, delivering heating
systems just in time to the end customer house, then it should be possible to coordinate
picking up the old heating system at delivery. This does of course change installer work
practices and adds complexity to an already complex ’just­in­time’ system. However this
would make the reverse logistics for heating systems practically free.
This does however limit the scope of the take back system to heating systems replaced
136
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:147==
with Viessmanns products. It is also limited to regions where Just in time is offered, and
products replaced by competitors will not be retrieved. Given that this is also a change
in how installers operate, it will decrease the value of just in time delivery, and brands
not participating might be seen as slightly easier to work with. Industrial cooperating
here, would however create an incentive to standardise this practice and alleviate these
concerns.
Drop off locations
Another way of reducing logistics costs, is to make installers drop
off heating systems at new locations. This is an uncommon approach and more
impractical than the dominant waste disposal route, but could be attractive in some
regions. Storage would ideally be in access managed container, 10 or 20 foot, to avoid
concerns of polluting the site, theft and misuse. Ideally the drop off locations would be
locations where installers already frequent in their day­to­today. The most attractive
identified locations here are:
1. HVAC supply stores and similar hardware stores installer frequent
2. At existing infrastructure
(a) Partnering with OEMs and competitors could increase the locations.
3. Drop off at scrap yards
HVAC stores and similar are seen as ideal, as Viessmann, their OEMs and competitors
may already have regular deliveries, and potentially reverse logistic setups to handle
warranty claims. The installers already frequent these stores, and there is the potential
of scaling the system up to handle HVAC products in general, all at a logical convenient
place. It does however require the stores to have room for a container, comply with
logical legislation for storing what might be waste ­ which poses a liability.
One key issue with drop off location is the scrap value of the stored items, especially
with intelligent product recovery (IPR). As covered in the situation analysis, current
waste management streams deals with a some amount of theft of valuable components.
Access management is therefore an important consideration (#Elertur 2023, #Installers
2023/05/16).
Summary of adaptations
These solutions are however not without their trade offs (summarised in table 7.1, nor
are they mutually exclusive. As installers options and interests differs, a hybrid approach
may be the most viable ­ or some solutions may be preferable in some regions/contexts.

Table 7.1: Summary of the solution nuances and how they might affect the systems re­
quirements (+ means a potential improvement, ­ means a potential degradation, blank
means not­assessed/negligeable)
Why bulk collection and treatment
Due to the potential regulatory hurdles around incentivised component retrieval and the
high cost of kerbside collection, bulk side collection and bulk treatment is the most
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
137
==PAGE:148==
economical viable solution (#Miele 2023). It is also the solution with largest
environmental reduction potential, significantly reducing the amount of dissipated critical
raw materials, especially copper. The solution is flexible and can be adapted as the
types of heating systems change. However the solution is entirely dependent on OEMs
helping pay the costs, or finding multiple common components in the old heating
systems to be repurposed internally (6.3).
Kerbside collection may be possible be reworking how just­in­time delivery works today,
or at large enough scale, with accepting all brands and most heating systems, to benefit
from economies of scale. However in this limited analysis it was found to be infeasible,
especially in the explorative implementation phase.
7.1.3
The implementation plan
Due to the difficulty in taking back specifically heating systems, it is recommended
starting with a few smaller different regions and learning from this before scaling up.
Using simple extrapolation, in a German region of 500.000 it is expected that 170
heating systems are replaced with heat pumps per month, assuming full participation
(see subsection A.5.6), using 2023 heat pump installation values. Using 20 foots
containers as drop off points this roughly corresponds to 3­4 pickups per month. Of
course with more pickups if picked up directly from installer companies.
Starting small is recommended due to a few factors that will be discussed in more depth
here:
1. Testing what installers actually will participate in.
2. Learning to dismantle different types of heating systems efficiently & effectively.
3. Field testing and fine­tuning a digital service to support intelligent product recovery.
4. Evaluate if and when weathering, storage and handling is problematic for the
heating systems integrity.
5. Driving interest from OEMs, competitors and internally.
1. Testing what installers actually will participate in:
What people say they are
interested in and what they will participate in can often differ (#Installers 2023/05/16).
Getting installers to changer their habits and work practices will require continuous
effort, especially in the beginning. As logistics costs are such a large factor determining
profitability, it is worthwhile testing different inbound logistic solutions as described in
(section 6.1), in the discussion of ways to minimise them. Ideally these are tested at
small scale where the risks are smaller.
2. Learning to dismantle different types of heating systems efficiently &
effectively:
There are many techniques and ways of increasing the efficiency of
dismantling the heating systems. Creating custom jigs & fixtures, experimenting with
tools and finding the optimal way of splitting them apart is important to break even and
allow for further dismantling. The more the heating system is split into its’ core materials,
the more valuable it becomes. As an organisation it is therefore needed to spend some
time in optimising this process before scaling up.
3. Field testing and fine­tuning a digital service to support intelligent product
recovery:
To do intelligent product recovery, it needs to be easy for installers to scan,
log and know what systems to treat differently, and if any precautions needs to be taken.
138
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:149==
Besides the evaluation of heating systems, it needs to be easy to find potential drop off
locations, order pickups etc.. For the system to be widely adopted, this needs to be ’pain
free’ ­ especially as the installers are changing habits and likely doing more work. This
needs to be tested at a smaller scale, so the supportive services ’just work’ when scaling
up the system. It may also be necessary to be implement functionality to be able to
exclude or punish installers for misusing the take back systems. As for some take back
systems (#Wade 2023) installers may see the take back system, as a way of getting rid
of waste somewhat unregulated ­ which is not the intent.
4. Evaluate if and when weathering, storage and handling is problematic for the
heating systems integrity
It is currently presumed that most components that can be
used for re­manufacturing, reuse etc.. are rather unaffected by the weathering and
handling. However this of course depends on the amount of the time they are stored
outside. It needs to be tested and evaluated if this impacts the up­cycling potential of the
components.
5. Driving interest from OEMs and competitors
Several OEMs have expressed
interested in getting their old products back. However many suppliers have not been
contacted and may not have evaluated if it even is possible. However, the more who
share the cost the better. In the upscaling phase it is important to provide OEMs with
samples of their old products, and show how they can also be broken down, to ensure a
lower impact end of life. Negotiating the cost sharing principles with each OEM is also
important. These will likely have to differ, as the value, weight, size, environmental
impact, etc.. of each suppliers components differs drastically.
When to scale up
There are multiple way to scale up the take back system. The system can be expanded
into a new region, into an new product category, to accept a new product or to a new
brand. Each poses its own challenges and perspectives. Generally when expanding the
take back systems scope in a region, it will lead to reduced logistical costs per heating
system. Studies have found that separate take back systems logistical costs scale non
linearly, whilst municipal take back systems (run by PROs) scale linearly ­ and that scale
lowers cost per product (Atasu et al., 2009) . Whilst expanding into a new region mean
accepting more, and dealing with more complexity, requiring a higher capacity in the
treatment facility.
Expanding to a new region
When expanding into a new region, it results in up front
costs, mainly providing containers, storage solutions and drop off points for installers. It
also requires efforts in marketing the new service to installers and potentially partnering
with new logistics providers and drop off locations.
The following discussion assumes that some kind intelligent product recovery is
implemented.
Expanding to a new product
When beginning to accept a new product category, it is
necessary to evaluate if it is environmentally and economically sensible and viable to
take it back. This hinges on multiple OEMs or Viessmann internally being interested in
upcycling some of the components. After this has been established, it needs to be
planned how to disassemble the product and evaluated if current tools, jigs/fixtures are
sufficient.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
139
==PAGE:150==
If it turns out to be viable to take the product back, it now needs to be integrated in the
digital service, ensuring that all variants can get registered easily.
Expanding to new products within a category is fairly straightforward, however some
preparation and exploration of the possibilities is necessary.
Expanding to a new product category
It is not trivial to expand to a new product
category, however the same formula can to a large extent be followed, however multiple
products within the category should be examined in depth. Logistics also needs to be
considered in more depth, as handling and weathering concerns differ more across
product categories.
Product categories will generally require more adaptation of the treatment facility. It
requires the possibility of bulking the product category separately, and its’ modules,
components etc.. when dismantling it. Some product categories may also be hazardous
or require more time consuming steps when dismantling. E.g.., propane base heat
pumps that have not been evacuated (removal of the propane) can take up to 2 days to
completely vent, which is necessary to avoid a fire hazard (#Tariq 2023/05/12). New
components may also require certifications of personnel or new tools to process. All of
this impact the amount of time that can be spent on the product, impacting what can be
retrieved and what is earned.
Expanding to a new brand
Expanding to a new brand requires much more planning
and preparation. Product architectures might be significantly different for some product
categories and many components will be manufactured by other OEMs, which there
hasn’t been established upcycling and cost sharing deals with. It can inherently be
approached from different angles: 1. as a business opportunity, 2. as a service offering
for a competitor or 3. to enhance the ease of use and versatility of the take back system
for installers.
The difference between 1 & 2 being whether you can profit from the products without the
competitors direct collaboration. 1. and 3. can also coexists. It is generally the authors
perception that 2. is the best option, as the competitor can utilise their product expertise
and relations with OEMs to drive participation.
The new brands products needs to be evaluated and compared against similar products,
that are currently accepted. It needs to be evaluated if disassembly plans/guides needs
to be changed, the same goes for fixtures, jigs, tools etc.. All of this determines if the
brands products can be bulked with in the same categories. The brands portfolio of
systems within accepted categories also needs to be evaluated.
Whilst this can be achieved by gathering some example products, dismantling and
investigating their composition ­ or simply assuming it. It will be significantly easier to get
the material composition, list of suppliers etc. directly from the competitors.
7.1.4
The Poster ­ evaluating what to take back
Taking back heating systems is and arduous task, with a lot of uncertainty. Covering up
to 20 years of product innovation, multiple brands and numerous architectures,
components and OEMs ­ even breaking even is difficult. The dismantling of heating
systems needs to be very efficient, without damaging select components and extracting
all the components that can be sent back. All of this needs to be done whilst keeping the
environmental impact reductions in mind. Coordinating and planning this for all the
different heating systems is difficult. It is not a one time task, as the heating systems
140
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:151==
received changes and lag 10­20 years behind the original sales date. OEMs interest in
taking back their components or paying for better end of life treatment, may also change.
The poster aims at acting as a boundary object, unifying the essential parts of this
analysis in one place for every heating system in a single easy to approach format. The
poster is meant to be a collaborative tool, helping balance the technical QSA, practical
considerations, disassembly planning and costing, for every product/product category.
The goal is the poster can with little modification be used for other product categories,
especially larger products.
This is the result of finding that most parts of this thesis quantitative analysis are quite
static for the product category, but that practical considerations will impact the viability
drastically ­ especially things that are hard to quantify meaningfully. These are
considerations like: how does the product weather, can it be stacked, does it require
special handling, where can we cut it to disassemble it etc.. For these questions impacts
how to recover the product, if we can remove all the components, how much pure scrap
we can extract from components etc.. All of which determine the profitability and the
environmental impact delta. It is also all iterative but finally determined by how the
product is dismantled on the line. There also needs to be clear, easy to understand
instructions for the people on the factory line, for where efforts needs to be targeted.
These questions can of course technically be answered quantitatively, but it appears
little is gained and it is expensive & time consuming. The answers and estimates needs
to be easy to update ­ for an OEM may suddenly want to start taking something back, or
the scrap prices might change significantly. Both requiring the disassembly to change
and changing the viability of taking back many heating systems.
For when an organisation has an established take back system(s), there needs to be a
consistent method for evaluating what products should be taken back and what should
be done with them. For large, obsolete and old products this is difficult.
The poster is meant to be used in collaborative setting, ideally bringing in the product
owner/experts, dismantlers, sustainability expertise, logistics and the like. Together this
will allow understanding what parts of the product are worth salvaging, which are
cumbersome to dismantle completely and what might be unfit for
reuse/re­manufacturing. Given the iterative process of planning what can and should be
removed from the product, it is best done in collaboration.
The poster can also be shared with the OEMs to see if they have any concerns,
preferences or requirements for how their components are removed and treated.
The poster elements
The poster consists of 5 elements and 10 steps, you can find the poster at the end of the
subchapter and in the appendix. The 5 elements are: 1. The context for take back, 2.
the circular focus, 3. disassembly planning, 4. the costing and 5. the evaluation. The
five elements and their reasoning will now be described:
1. The context for take back
The product in Viessmann’s portfolio wary widely, and
they are decommissioned for different reasons. The first step is therefore to consider the
where, how and why’s for taking the product back. How is it disposed of today? How
large and heavy is it? How it be stored etc..
It is important to consider the current disposal, to have a frame of reference, for some
products can be disposed this way. It also may be the most feasible options unless there
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
141
==PAGE:152==
is significant potential for upcycling. Some products may also be subject to certain
regulation, which may taking them back more important or more difficult.
2. The circular focus
After understanding why the product is replaced and concerns
for taking it back, it is time to evaluate the environmental priorities of the product. Here
the focus is EoL, so the use phase is ignored and the focus is on the materials,
components and structural elements. Any environmental end­ or midpoint indicator can
be selected but we elected to use GWP in the poster. Each components environmental
impact is evaluated and they are ranked. All the ’non’ components, i.e., piping, chassis
etc.. is then ranked, to illustrate where the focus should be in the product. This focus is
important, for there can be a mismatch between the scrap value, upcycling potential and
the environmental impacts.
Of course multiple impact indicators can be used, and other rankings will emerge. Here
we stress the importance of relying on the sustainability personnel’s expertise, to help
capture some of the materials/components which may have disproportionate large
effects on other mid/endpoint indicators ­ to avoid GWP tunnel vision.
This prioritisation feeds into the definition of components of interest ­ the components
that should/could be upcycled. As most of the large environmental impacts except
structural elements are supplied by OEMs, the focus here is to map if the OEMs have
take back systems, what they do with the components and what value the components
represent both as scrap and at purchase. This mapping will help to identify if there is
potential for collaborating with OEMs in sharing the cost, who should handle the
component treatment, and if the OEM should be contacted.
A lot of this information may not be known, OEMs might have changed over the years,
and may change in the future. OEMs may also have narrow take back systems, only
accepting a limited range of components ­ or require samples/studies of the components
that can be supplied.
3. Disassembly planning
Now that it has been established what components and
parts of the product that are the most important from and environmental point of view,
and what components can be upcycled, it is time to investigate how to disassemble the
product. For many take back systems, the product treatment and labour costs is one of
the main costs drivers, so optimising this process and finding the optimal ways of
proceeding is very important. The large heating systems are well suited for semi
destructive disassembly, as only a few components of upcycling potential.
Disassembly planning is difficult, and multiple techniques exists. Many remain rather
abstract, like graph based and disassembly trees. These are hard to interpret and
difficult to construct without a CAD model. To make this assessment easier to the
developed method is to use product photos and highlighting. The assessment of
disassembly time is therefore quite theoretical, but it can be refined with practice and
experience from dismantlers as the organisation learns to scrap. Multiple product
variants can likely be assessed at the same time. The suggested methodology is shown
in the figure below.
142
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:153==
Figure 7.3: Poster disassembly process
The process is best done digitally, as a lot of duplicate images are required. Miro is
already embedded in the organisations way of working, making this a good fit. However
in theory, it can also be done on paper.
The process is broadly follows: 1. highlight the COI, 2. highlight the remaining in its’
main material fractions. 3. outline what needs to be handled with care (e.g., COI for
reuse.) 4. Highlight where you can be destructive (e.g., piping). 5. Evaluate if breaking it
into modules makes handling easier, if so how to do it. 6. Describe and highlight how
you will remove each COI, use the table to document tools and time estimates. 7.
Evaluate if it is necessary to break the components/remains apart, if so do 4­6 for those
components. 8. sum up and discuss the findings. 9. Iterate. This process is quite
approachable, and lends itself to co­creation and use of different product insights. It does
however require good product photos, and esp. for OEMs components, it may be more
difficult to procure exploded views of components and the like.. Over time, a repository
of component disassembly estimates, may be built up, and many components do not
have to be evaluated. I.e., Most circulation pumps are likely pretty similar to dismantle.
4. The costing
Now that it has been established, how the product is taken back, what
components were removed, how long it takes and what scrap is created it is possible to
do the costing. At this point the costing is fairly trivial. In the poster this is reduced to a
per heating system assessment, without equipment deprecation and capital
expenditure. However this can of course be added. The main consideration here was to
establish how large the loss is before sharing the cost with OEMs and to price the COI.
Multiple different take back systems can be evaluated here, e.g., kerbside, incentivised
recovery and bulk collection.
The costing of scrap is suggested to be viewed from three different lenses: if nothing is
done (mixed metal value), complete separation (the maximum attainable scrap value),
complete separation minus COI. These 3 views can help asses if more or less time
should be spent on disassembly, and if so, what components and assemblies should be
prioritised. This part is largely iterative together with the disassembly planning, as the
labour costs are generally significant.
5. The discussion and conclusion
At this point the results should be discussed. Key
is consider: 1. is this product typical for the product category, or competitors products
similar? 2. What drives the cost for this product? Can we share the costs with the OEMs
and can we interest more? 3. Does taking it back improve the EoL sustainability
performance significantly? Does it make sense, sustainability wise, to send the
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
143
==PAGE:154==
components back to their manufactures? 4. Does taking the product back require
adaptations for the facility? Is it safe to dismantle the product and what products can we
group it with? Finally it needs to be concluded: are OEMs interested at this price ­ are
we? Should the product be taken back? If not, then what would it take for this
assessment to change ­ and is it likely to change?
The use of the poster is recommended to be an iterative process. It is meant to guide the
design decisions based on the current knowledge of the system and products. As shown
in the analysis there are high uncertainties on multiple parameters of the process.
Following are described some of the inputs that might evolve through usage. The
context for ”1. The context for take back” can have evolving regulation considerations
and current end of life insights. For ”2. The circular focus”, better understanding of
environmental impact and other method can slightly modify the hotspots considered.
However, the actual willingness to get components back of most of the OEMs is unclear.
Thus, consultations should be conducted with them in order to define their needs and
readiness to accept components back. This means that after evaluating the components
that are a part of the product hotspots, the identified interest of OEMs can be evolving
with the discussions and change the outcome of the poster evaluation. The ”3.
Disassembly plan” is meant to prepare for collecting actual disassembly data
specifically to the take back context. Ground data will improve and change the planning.
”4. The costing” will evolve with the scrap market prices. So will OEMs interest in old
products and components as the CO2 taxes and emission permits increase in price.
The poster steps and use in practice
Each poster element, except the disassembly section is divided into two sections. This
is done to make it more manageable and approachable. The hope is this makes it easier
for participants to find where their knowledge & skills are relevant. In a workshop setting,
it also affords logical points for breaks, energizers and other pauses.
A very important aspect of the poster is that it is well suited for asynchronous
preparation. Much of the required data can be difficult or time consuming to procure,
esp. product composition, environmental impacts, OEMs interest and product photos. To
avoid being blocked during the workshop, this should be acquired beforehand, by many
different employees in different departments. As the poster is a lot, both for the facilitator
and the employees doing the preparations, we have attempted to make this easier. This
has been done by outlining what roles/departments to invite, giving each an icon and
clearly marking each step with the icons. Each employee can then easily connect to the
Miro board, find the relevant sections, acquire the information they can ­ and contact the
facilitator if there is any information they do not have access to. Each section also has a
very short explanation and goal in the top, to give an overarching overview.
Figure 7.4: Poster onboarding and preparation section
And finally, the poster in it’s entirety. For a higher res version or access to a Miro board
with the poster, contact the authors. Piror versions and iterations are available in the
appendix, section A.5.
