Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:155==
Figure 7.5: The final poster
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
145
==PAGE:156==
7.2
Discussion of solutions
7.2.1
Is the poster valuable/useful?
As far as the authors are aware, the poster presents the single most comprehensive
boundary object for making a semi quantitative assessment of taking back a large
product. The poster has been fitted to the organisations product categories, but can be
expanded to other products.
Evaluating when it makes sense to take back, especially large products, is difficult, as it
requires: 1. disparate information form across departments and disciplines. 2.
Balancing accuracy and time required to conduct the assessments ­ if it is too hard, it
won’t happen. 3. Different products need different considerations in assessment.
The poster arguably addresses these three difficulties. It is a very structured and
comprehensive ­ offering key identified considerations for each step. But it is very easy
to change and adapt what is considered in each step. It combines all the elements
needed to make the assessment in one place, makes it easier to divide the preparation
asynchronously. This makes it more feasible for different departments/roles to gather
the necessary information, and know in what context it will be used and when their
expertise is relevant.
The main critiques of the poster is the lack of validation and testing on new products. It
has only been tested internally on very analysed products. The more novel elements,
such as the semi­destructive disassembly technique, needs validation with real world
disassembly. The disassembly assessment also does not end with a specific format for
a disassembly plan that dismantlers can use in the factory ­ i.e., a graphic/disassembly
tree illustrating the steps.
The poster does also not have direct elements nudging the organisation to
reuse/re­manufacture parts of the products itself ­ it focuses on finding ways to send
components back to OEMs. Whilst this was identified as the key way to make it
economically viable for the gas boilers and heat pumps studied ­ this may not apply for
other product categories.
Given the lack of validation of the poster, the organisation needs to take ownership of
the poster. This will require planning a workshop (which is in the talks at the time of
writing), further fit and develop it to the organisations needs. The poster requires a lot of
preparation and planning from multiple departments, whose interest/inclination/view to
both workshops and the feasibility of take back systems for their products will vary a lot.
Some may also view parts of the poster/framework as being too qualitative, or otherwise
conflict with their work/academic/organisational/departments identity.
With most participants having to invest multiple hours to prepare for the workshop, it is
important that it is clearly communicated: why the poster/assessment is needed, why
this is a good approach and that it is subject to change. There is a large risk for the
poster/framework falling short in practice, leading to some stakeholder refraining from
participating in future assessments. However the poster only works through iteration
and evaluation of multiple products. It is the authors learning from the project that these
types of take back systems, can only meet their sustainability potential and be
economical viable through planning and assessment. There needs to be utilisation of
product expertise & knowledge, like: what can be upcycled, what can be destroyed and
which alloys are used. For this both serves to increase the value of the product as
scrap, improves the environmental performance and can reduce costs.
146
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:157==
Involving product design in these workshops will help give them learning’s in how their
products will be disassembly in the future. Current design for disassembly guidelines
tends to recommend non destructive techniques and the goal being fast careful
disassembly. These a good principles to adhere to in general, but they are general
recommendations that need to be adapted to the context. Giving the product designers
agency and experience with semi destructive disassembly planning could transfer
significant learning’s, ultimately leading to easier to dissemble products in the future. It is
especially important for the end of life phase, not to over constrain the product design for
heating systems, as the focus should be on the production and especially use phase
impacts. A more energy efficient heating system design, or an efficient heating system
design more consumers can afford/use could potentially outweigh the gains in the end of
life phase.
A need for design for take back?
In the project it has been discussed if design for take back is significantly different than
other design for end of life strategies, such as design for disassembly and design for
recycling. In this project, the need to have a take back system has been questioned too
as there is existing waste management, which is developing its’ capabilities.
Technologies such as digital product passports, will enable a wide array of waste actors
to do more targeted efforts and for manufactures to transfer their product expertise to
them. For there is a need in this linear economy/system, to be able to utilise product
expertise to improve the products end of life ­ otherwise value recovery remains the best
option. However product expertise and product knowledge is not enough on itself, it
needs to be actionable for the waste actors. Generally this requires a certain
homogeneity and scale of waste streams, to make it profitable for many waste actors.
Most of them handle very large amounts, of very different products, makes targeted and
special treatment inherently very difficult. Alone sotrint and handling products with
enough care to enable upcycling can be dififcult. EPR, PROs and DPP do represent
large opportunities for addressing som of this, but the current waste routes can be
infeasible for many products. Lack of weather protection, theft in the stream and ’waste’
being treated as waste, poses large challenges. Creating new routes for products to be
disposed also complicates an increasingly complex waste management landscape for all
actors. Multiple HVAC companies do not participate in any today ­ and were not inclined
to participate in new ones either, mostly for it being too difficult, or yet another thing to
account for. Regional waste management practices can vary widely in both regions and
countries ­ and it is not always a transparent nor easy to understand ecosytem.
Design for take back would for these authors encompass:
1. Methods for evaluating the potential for taking back the product
2. Methods for evaluating how to take back the product
3. Methods for planning the product treatment
4. Methods for driving OEM interest
7.2.2
Is it feasible to take back heating systems?
With the results in the cost analysis, it is risky to take back the heating systems. But with
involvement with enough OEMs, increasing scrap prices and finding was to dismantle
the products very efficiently there is potential for profiting.
Not all HVAC companies are interested in participating in the system, so some product
leakage will occur. this can be mitigated with proper incentives, however given that the
current end of life is very convenient, not all will be interested. take back systems which
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
147
==PAGE:158==
accept all brands should be preffered and be the goal, as it will be infeasible for most
HVAC companies to manage and navigate multiple take back systems. Product
treatment can vary within the same heating system, it is mostly the inbound collection
route that needs to be simple for the installers.
Product treatment and logistics can be outsourced with enough planning. Some
companies are already collaborating with PROs to facilitate proper recycling of select
heating systems. However this would require some scale and bulking of the heating
systems to make it viable for them to upcycle components. The more homogeneous the
waste stream, and product knowledge, the more potential there is for ambitious product
treatment.
Are heat pumps different
Gas condensing boilers are especially difficult as the
potential for component upcycling is difficult, and the product designs cannot be altered.
Heat pumps are significantly different and there is a much larger potential in taking them
back. This is driven by multiple factors:
1. Good opportunities for reuse in the same use case
2. Higher material value per HS
3. Increasing material value w. increasing scrap prices
4. Low wear components
5. Long term option of changing the product designs
Some things complicating the take back of heat pumps are: 1. venting of gases and
safety during dismantling. 2. Larger size making logistics more costly. 3. Potential
technological obsolescence of components.
148
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:159==
8
Discussion, recommendations and
conclusions ­ Why is it the best solution
so far and how to implement it?
8.1
Summary
The thesis started by outlining the context and the reason for the study. The large
amount of decommissioned gas boilers and the future decommissioning of heat pumps
gives a good foundation for considering the development of a take back system.
Moreover, the legislation, the growing interest for circular economy, and the sustainable
commitments of Viessmann are factors that push towards this idea. The defined goals
were to map the boilers and heat pumps disposal routes and search for take back
solutions. The scenarios were then to be economically and environmentally quantified to
allow a comparison and choose the best option. It was decided to investigate which
steps were needed to design a take back system, the characteristics that influence its
operations, and what decision making tool could be used to plan the implementation of
such solution. A large literature review was conducted to identify the important concepts
of circularity, steel recycling, methods for designing take back systems and how to
quantify such systems. The relevant regulations were also explored to determine the
regulatory frame in which waste management is. The current situation was then
explored. It was found out that most of the heating systems end up in mixed metal
recycling routes, with some of the heat pumps being treated with white goods. The main
handling practice was for installers to bring the waste at their company’s place and get a
scrap dealer to pick them up when a few of them were stored. It was found out that there
was potential for creating economical and environmental benefits by improving the
sorting of scrap for recycling and recovering some components for remanufacturing. In
fact, a few suppliers were found to be interested in potentially taking their components
back for that purpose.
The next step was to identify the challenges which were: 1. Competing with scrap
dealers, 2. Boilers being geographically sparse, 3. Product having a low material value,
4. Recycling not being efficient for those products, 5. Driving participation being hard, 6.
Having a variety of workflows for installers, 6. Installers not being able to make additional
effort, 7. Having an heterogeneous waste stream even with only heating systems.
However, opportunities were also defined as: 1. Creating a flexible inbound logistic, 2.
Allowing for a brand agnostic waste stream, 3. Having a targeted disassembly, 4.
Creating value by reducing downcycling, 5. Driving collaboration with a clear roadmap.
Afterwards, the aforementioned challenges and opportunities were used to develop
ideas. The main criteria for good ideas were the potential costs, time spent and
environmental savings. Two main ideation rounds were conducted followed by a
qualitative assessment of the ideas. It first ended up with a large morphology diagram,
which was then used to define 4 concepts. These 4 concepts were the refined and
improved, and three of them were then elected to go through a quantitative selection.
Namely: 1. Incentivised recovery, 2. Bulk collection, 3. Kerbside collection.
To evaluate the solutions, a DES model is developed to quantitatively assess the time,
transport and derived costs. The results are then fed into a LCA model that quantifies
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
149
==PAGE:160==
the environmental saving of the solutions compared to the baseline. It was found that
bulk collection and incentivised recovery options have the potential to start being
profitable. The condition being that OEMs are willing to share the costs and by the
components the get back to a fair price. The environmental saving are found to be
around 10% compared to the baseline and similar across the three solutions. Moreover,
it was also found that heat pumps have more potential for environmental savings from
material end of life as their material footprint is larger in comparison to boilers.
Concluding the quantitative evaluation, the main insight is that the logistics costs,
treatment labour and environmental savings are fairly linear for the system. And logistics
costs being a large uncertainty driver in this case due to the lack of on ground data.
The linearity of the results synchronise well with the proposed decision making tool.
Indeed the developed solution for this part is a poster that help guide Viessmann’s
various experts through the design and evaluation steps of a take back system. As
details were missing on the logistics costing and the ability of OEMs to share the costs,
the goal of the poster is to enable Viessmann to define those details in a collaborative
manner with their suppliers. The linearity of the results synchronise well with the
proposed decision making tool. Indeed the developed solution for this part is a poster
that help guide Viessmann’s various experts through the design and evaluation steps of
a take back system. As details were missing on the logistics costing and the ability of
OEMs to share the costs, the goal of the poster is to enable Viessmann to define those
details in a collaborative manner with their suppliers. The linearity of the results
synchronise well with the proposed decision making tool. Indeed the developed solution
for this part is a poster that help guide Viessmann’s various experts through the design
and evaluation steps of a take back system. As details were missing on the logistics
costing and the ability of OEMs to share the costs, the goal of the poster is to enable
Viessmann to define those details in a collaborative manner with their suppliers for each
product and product category.
8.2
Limitations
This project has covered a lot of ground and attempted to combine multiple modelling
approaches, multiple product categories, posters and policy. The goal has been to have
acceptable accuracy with low precision ­ to investigate what is possible for this case.
However, this approach comes with limitations on depth and precision.
Viessmanns Climate Solutions department has been in the process of being bought by
the Carrier group, which has limited some departments ability to dedicate time and
provide data for the project, which has decreased the precision of some key elements
significantly.
Some of the main limitations are:
1. This project looked only at gas condensing boiler(s) and air­to­air heat pumps ­ no
other heating systems like solar thermal, ground source heat pumps. It limits the
analysis to a portion of the market and does not represent the diversity of the real
waste stream that could be captured.
2. Modern products were used instead of old products. Which in this case too,
constrains the representativeness of the current market as these products are not
massively decommissioned yet.
3. Few boilers and heat pumps were considered, which lowers the confidence that
the results hold for the large product portfolio in the market.
150
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:161==
4. The heat pump product is considered as the average between two widely different
product sizes, and there is not a unified functional unit based on the actual function
they provide (heat output). Averaging products makes the modelling easier but
comes with representativeness issues. Moreover, the fact that the comparison was
done at a product level and not at their functional level lowers the confidence in the
comparison.
5. The use phases was ignored, whilst fine for the energy use, it omits replacement
parts. It firstly limits the environmental evaluation to a portion of the life cycle.
Secondly, it neglects some of the opportunities that could arise in the use phase,
such as the capture and circulation of spare parts.
6. Limited details on what components, conditions and treatments OEM are
interested in. The context was not favourable for contacting OEMs which ends up
in the fact that their willingness to pay for the recovered parts could not be
assessed. Ultimately, it leaves the conclusion with an open statement, saying that
making profit from the take back system relies on their interest. It was possible to
approximate the components potential price but without validation.
7. Limited details on the extent to which the OEMs are ready to buy components that
are sent back to them, and whether they would pay for a better EoL of components
they don’t want back. Even if they are interested in paying for taking back
components, it was not possible to assess whether it would be enough to cover the
costs and have a viable business model in the end.
8. Very uncertain kerbside collection prices ­ likely overestimate. The contacts with
potential 3rd party logistics provider was also impossible in the time frame. This
ended up with large logistics costs uncertainty.
9. Very uncertain bulk collection prices ­ very regional dependent. The same contact
issues as the point above holds true here.
10. Lack of poster validation ­ the poster was not used in a workshop setting with a
new product. Even though the poster was evaluated by a user experience
researcher, the ability to have on ground validation would have greatly improved
the result and confidence in its usability.
11. Simplistic costing of the facility, only looking at wage costs. The missing cost
evaluation of management, administration, facility maintenance and resources use
was assumed of low importance, which could end up in an underestimation of the
overall treatment costs.
12. General lack of validation of the discrete event simulations.
13. The refrigerant production and disposal were not taken in account in the analysis
because of a lack of confidence in the current and potential disposal. This could
have a large impact on the environmental analysis as well as on the treatment
procedure.
14. Lack of in depth evaluation of installers interest in different take back solutions.
The language barrier with German installers prevented us to have interviews with
them and we had to rely on Danish and Swiss installers for the evaluation. Even
though surveys were sent to around 200 German HVAC companies, the response
rate was low, and the usability of surveys limited. The time constraint of installers
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
151
==PAGE:162==
also prevented us to have in depth discussions even with Danish and Swiss
enterprises.
15. The low quality of information on remanufacturing, due to the lack of direct OEMs
contact, lowers the confidence in the results.
16. The environmental analysis based mostly on global average flows and processes
also shows representativeness issues as the processes have specific locations in
or outside Germany.
8.3
Discussion
The project has been guided by three main research questions:
1. What are the necessary steps when designing take back systems for different
heating systems?
It was identified that a few frameworks exists, and they provide a
good overview of the necessary considerations for taking back products in general.
Whilst the frameworks, are generally focused on B2C, they are adaptable to the B2B2C
value chain. However the current frameworks and methods falls short for addressing
improper disposal, which was found to be an issue for heating systems. It is hard for
literature to offer more general frameworks as take back of equipment is very product
and region dependent. Current studies and frameworks have mostly dealt with smaller
and shorter lifespan products.
Some of the key differences when designing take back systems for heating systems
compared to other product categories are: 1. a larger emphasis on reducing logistics,
and getting a hold of product. The large dimensions of heating systems can be difficult
for installers, users and the like to store. 2. Taking external companies operations into
account ­ in this case HVAC companies. Some solutions, such as kerb side collection
and incentivised component recovery require changes in HVAC companies ways of
working. However generally introducing a brand or product specific take back system
will be very difficult as HVAC companies are dealing with many different services,
products and already have a very convenient waste route today. 3. Accounting for
seasonality of product decommissioning/replacement, may put significant demands on
the inbound logistics system, especially due to the product size. 4. Accounting for
’competing’ take back systems. The more complicated the waste management system,
the less successful each will be, unless they are somewhat unified. How can OEMs
avoid reinventing the waste management and recycling industry.
Amongst different heating systems, there is also needs to consider
1. What components can be upcycled and what OEMs can help share the costs.
Upcycling was identified in sections 2.1.2 and 3.2.7 as a great potential for
creating value and environmental savings. For that to happen, it is necessary to
drive OEMs interest as they were identified as the main upcycling potential drivers.
This step is essential in the early phase of the take back system design to ensure
to break even and have the chance to make profit of the system.
2. What is the value of the product at different levels of scrap. It was determined in
section 3.5.1 the economical benefits from the take back system not only come
from the upcycled components, but also in large part from the remaining scrap. It
was suggested that the fractions should be separated as much as possible during
the treatment to capture the best value possible (see section 6.3.6.
152
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:163==
3. How can the product be disassembled very efficiently. For that destructive
disassembly was found to be a good candidate in section 2.1.3. It was identified in
section 4.1 that a targeted disassembly was a great opportunity to both recover the
interesting components for upcycling and dividing the scrap fractions efficiently.
4. If regulation influences your obligations or solution space for the product. Indeed,
section 2.1.6 showed that depending on the product, specific obligations apply to
the waste handling. For example the disposal rules of F­gases in heat pumps has
to be investigated depending on the used refrigerant, which might affects the
operations.
5. If the current baseline waste management can be improved enough if there is little
potential. In section 3.4 the current situation environmental and economical
impacts were identified with their potential improvement. It was shown that without
largely improving the end of life treatment of materials and components, it was
hard to see any benefits in only adjusting the baseline.
2. How does heating systems characteristics influence the design, planning and
operation of take back systems?
Section 4.3 sets the criteria to assess the guide the
assessment of the characteristics of the solutions. The main types are the costs, time
spent, as well as the carbon and energy footprints. Moreover in section 6.3 the extent of
the influence of the characteristics were explored for the selected solutions.
The most important findings are listed below
1. The logistics costs per HS is the largest contributor to the overall expenditure (see
section 6.3.6). Heavy and wide products results in relatively expensive logistics,
which in addition, are significantly harder to store for most. The analysis shows
that heat pumps are different compared to condensing boiler, both due to variety in
product dimensions, but also composition, and the need of handling an indoor and
outdoor unit. In section 6.3, it was found that these consideration greatly influence
the options available when designing a take back. The best identified option was
to handle them in containers which also allows to bulk them up and thus have
more efficient transport. Alternatively, it would be possible to dismantle the
components early and transport only those, however, it comes with more feasibility
issues (see point 5 below).
2. The long products lifespan has large implications as well. Heating systems unlike
many other white goods, is predominately replaced due to use phase efficiency
and fuel economy. I.e., obsolescence ­ unlike other white goods, where features,
product failure and fashion also plays a significant role. Upcycling and reuse of
components in the same use case (residential heating) can be very difficult. The
lifespan reaching up to 20 years is a challenge when there is a shift in technology
as mentioned in section 6.3.4 which shows that only a few components from the
boilers could be potentially reused compared to heat pumps. It is especially true
when trying to upcycle components to a large extent. When not feasible, recycling
is still a good option and there are ways to improve it’s value, e.g, with functional
recycling.
3. For value recovery, separating the product into its raw materials/scrap categories
is very important to consider both economically and environmentally as seen in
section 6.3.6. Here there are significant differences between heat pumps and
boilers. The former holds more value in their material in general, which is a
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
153
==PAGE:164==
positive point when selling scrap for recycling. Nonetheless, boilers having less
interesting components to upcycle, their scrap fractions make up for a large
amount of the benefits that can be realised at EoL. Accounting for capabilities of
mono fraction recycling and the material composition of the dismantled ’modules’
can speed up the process. It was also determined in section 6.3.6 that there is the
potential of breaking even if the components are bought by OEMs, however this
assumption remains unverified.
4. The inherent lack of reusability and refurbishment as an EoL or EoU strategy limits
the options for profiting off of the entire product. This puts higher demands on
product expertise to know what components may be valuable for OEMs or
worthwhile extracting to reduce the environmental impact. Therefore, section 6.3.6
identifies the need of involving the OEMs in the take back design and planning as
they are the experts in their own products and can guide the efforts towards the
best take back system design. Moreover, collaborating with the scrappers
community that already strive to recover the highest value possible from old
products would increase the expertise when planning the products treatment.
5. The products being characterised as waste requires the treating body to have
waste management certifications. This hinders the ability to have anybody
recovering the components and locks the feasible solutions to treatment centres
and existing waste management actors as seen in section 7.1.2.
3. What decision making tools can be used to plan efforts of taking back large
product portfolio?
Currently there exists tools and methods to asses and optimise
take back for products. However current tools focus on few aspects, or require
specialised assessment for each area.
Frameworks like De Brito and Dekker, 2004 and Mallick et al., 2023 gather most of the
relevant topics to consider the context and overarching take back considerations for a
product. However if products, like heating systems, have large variance in their take
back potential, there is a lack of tools which gather the disparate information and
considerations to asses the viability for taking back that specific product in a take back
system.
Current disassembly planning tools are too detailed and require extensive efforts to plan
mostly careful disassembly. This limits its applicability in assessing a large group of
products to support intelligent product recovery. Different heating systems also require
different practical logistical considerations ­ which may impact the viability for taking
them back. However a multitude of logistical optimisation tools exists for designing an
optimal logistics network ­ however once this is done the logistics costs per product are
fairly linear at scale.
Simply put, there is a lack of tools to simplify the assessment and evaluation of what
makes the take back of a single product viable or unviable. These tools need to be
flexible and help collect the information, as for long lived product, key product
information may be spread around in the organisation ­ or require close dialogue with
OEMs who may be the deciding factors.
To overcome that challenge, we propose the use of a co­creative poster that includes all
the steps to effectively evaluate the feasibility of taking back products in section 7.1.4. It
is designed to allow a simple assessment of the most important factors for taking back
products. Moreover, it is adapted for multiple product types and technologies. Finally it
154
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:165==
creates an overview of all the take back elements, allowing it to be used din workshop
settings and in collaboration with a large variety of experts. It’s iterative nature allows to
explore solutions and raise questions that can be answered later and allow to come
back to the design process. It’s asynchronous aspect also allows multiple experts to
collect information separately and thus have a efficient plenum discussion with all the
needed background.
8.3.1
This thesis contribution to research on take back system design
This thesis main contribution to research is as an explorative study on the viability and
considerations specific for heating systems. It found that whilst there is environmental
potential, it is hard to achieve and may be very product and region dependant.
Section 2 contributes in defining the state of the art of the scientific literature about the
relation of circular economy and sustainability of take back system elements.
Additionally, it covers the existing take back systems design modelling methods.
Section 3 describes the currant situation and captures a large amount of the data
needed to asses the viability for taking back old products which is spread amongst the
organisation.
Section 4 defines the relevant criteria to include in a detailed economical and
environmental analysis of take back options.
Section 5 covered a large amount of problem and solution space and presented its
findings. Here especially the morphology could possibly serve as inspiration for
generating novel take back system configurations.
Section 6 defines the important characteristics and finds that the viability of a take back
system depends on OEMs interest in taking back their components or participating in
sharing the cost. With increasing interest and possibilities in circular economy ­ such as
environmental reduction, risk mitigating in the supply chain, branding and more, the
viability is subject to change over time.
Section 7 proposes a poster which represents a comprehensive, but approachable
assessment method for all key considerations for taking back large products. Uniquely, it
is focused on heating systems in a B2B2C context. It represents one of the first semi
quantitative tools, taking into account regulation, environmental, economic disassembly
and practical considerations for taking back products. It helps establish an overview of
what old products are ­ which can be partially lost through the products long lifespan. It
supports large companies ways of working, especially the need to gather old product
information across the organisation and OEMs.
The posters novel disassembly approach, is one of the first semi destructive
disassembly planning tools. Which was found to be needed to asses whether it is viable
to take back many of the potentially low value products. Whilst the poster was
developed to asses old product for take back, it can be used to evaluate how a new
product design may perform when taken back. Many parts of the poster can easily be
omitted or extended to support the needs of any organisation.
8.3.2
Reflection of the project management and process
The design process was mostly based on systems design engineering method with the
addition of a large part of model based system engineering. The approach was well
suited for the project and yielded a large amount of results and recommendations.
The lack of access to data and the quality of it had to be handled in multiple way. Data
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
155
==PAGE:166==
wrangling of BOMs and and large assumptions in estimating prices and environmental
benefits of remanufacturing had to be done. Some improvement could have been
probably done by reducing the detail level of the analysis in the first iterations and thus,
targeting more efficiently the important data points. Not doing this lead to a large amount
of time spent on modelling and managing data with very low return on investment. It
also lowered our ability to iterate on the concepts design and their modelling.
The choice of modelling method showed great results but also had various issues.
Material Flow Analysis was a well suited method to assess quickly the effect of supply
and demand in the steel flow. Even though it was solely scoped to heat pumps it still
gave the expected results.
Choosing Discrete Event Simulation to model the product flows and time spent at each
steps was successful. However, there have been issues with handling data outputs as
they were large and hard to navigate. Here, the lower detail first assessment could have
shown where to put efforts and what data and level of scenarios detail were needed to
ease the final analysis. It was also limited by the available Anylogic student version
which constrained the process to a unique flow. In connection with that, the Life Cycle
Assessment has been slightly tedious because of the large data output from the DES
simulation. There are options to automate it with python and with more time and a more
iterative process, it could have been done. However, it still gave the expected results.
One issue was the ecoinvent database resources had limited detail on the type of alloy,
which resulted in having to average most of the materials to one type. This could have
been avoided by using the EcoAudit option of CESEdupack (Ansys, 2021), which has a
larger database of materials and alloys. The issue with that would have been the
modelling of remanufacturing process as the options are limited in EcoAudit. Moreover,
it would have limited to a CO2 and energy footprint. In the end, the LCA was the right
tool for fulfilling the needs.
It was hard to get interviews with some of the stakeholders. Especially OEMs and 3rd
party logistics provider. However, it was late in the project that their importance was
defined as data was hard to get otherwise. As mentioned before, having a quick
modelling in the beginning would have made that clear earlier and enabled us to have
more time to convince them to participate.
It was also hard to interview relevant installers as they were busy and the language
barrier was there. Even sending surveys did not yield a high response rate. The
potential reason is the length of the surveys and the way the questions were formulated.
Translating them to German was also a potential source of misunderstanding, and thus,
an obstacle for installers to answer. In this sense the process could have been improved
by designing more carefully the surveys and selecting the right questions.
8.3.3
Sustainability and end of life management
A set of challenges were identified which take back systems do not appear to be the
ideal solutions for. Mainly there appears to be a conflict between the amount of take
back systems and consumers/actors ability to participate and navigate them.
Companies, such as HVAC businesses, (and other B2B2C enterprises, garages and
more) will increasingly have large amounts of products where different actors will
establish take back systems. This increase in complexity could cause a rebound effect,
making it even more preferable to use the mixed metal waste route. Here the practices
of scrap dealers and other waste handlers would be the logical avenue. Here there just
needs to be the right set of incentives and/or regulation. If the waste handler can earn
more on the scrap value this is what will happen ­ with else regard for the embodied
156
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:167==
energy or other environmental savings. Here polluters pay principles (PPP), deposit
systems, advance recycling fees (ARF) may fall short. As under PPP it may not be
entering a monitored waste stream ­ so no OEM pays. Deposit systems value may be
smaller than the scrap value. ARF may be paid by the end consumer and not the
company disposing of the product.
The value of the disposed products also decreases if the disposed products are treated
as waste and not products. Exposure to weathering, partial scrapping and theft of the
best condition products/components ­ can significantly hinder PROs/take back systems
upcycling efforts. These issues requires systemic changes, from behaviour change in
how EoU/EoL products are treated from consumers houses all the way through the
waste stream.
All of this varies widely across regions, the EU and the world. From the capabilities of
waste management systems, to the disposal practices, regulation and amount of
component/material salvaging. The world of waste management is complex and is a
speciality of itself. Large scale change across of product categories should arguably be
driven through a reframing of how waste is handled by all actors, and processed and
handled by specialists to let all actors focus on their specialities.
The value of products as scrap can also be somewhat difficult to asses. Current publicly
available scrap prices are generally offered in quite rough categories. Assuming copper
is copper, stainless steel is stainless steel, is quite simplistic. Increased transparency in
what different alloys, could possibly be a driver for decreasing critical raw material
dissipation. However this is of course a trade­off between invested time vs. gains and
feasibility of realising the gains of increased information and purity of scrap.
8.4
Recommendations
This section mirrors many of the recommendations for the implementation plan, but aims
to generalise some of the insights for taking back products and especially large products
in general.
1. Start small and learn how to target the efforts toward the valuable resources that a
take back system can provide. We suggest semi­destructive disassembly to keep
the interesting components condition with a fast processing, and the rest being
sold as pure as possible scrap.
2. Establish an overview of the mostly static costs and what influence them early on
in collaboration with providers. Logistics costs and scrap values are key factors for
large products and depend largely on the provider and region.
3. Develop semi quantitative tools that gather the information on a product/product
category, so the information and insights are gathered in one place for each
product/product category. Product take back is inherently very multidisciplinary,
and in large organisations it can be time consuming to establish this overview.
Much of it can also change over time due to changes in the market or new found
interest from OEMs.
4. Find creative ways of reducing static costs, for example through collaborating with
competitors or the existing waste management systems.
5. Accept that some products are best handled through the established waste
management system. Investing time and resources in products with little
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
157
==PAGE:168==
environmental focus can limit your organisations ability to do more good in other
areas.
6. Consider stakeholders ability to manage an increasingly complex waste
management system. Too many competing systems create a rebound effect,
reducing overall participation in any of time.
7. Do not only focus on GWP/CO2. Investigate what materials may be dissipated and
lost through existing recycling routes and where those are located.
8. Simplify your product, look at it through a scrap dealers eyes.
9. Find ways to consider destructive disassembly in your design for disassembly
practices.
8.5
Conclusions
This research attempts to model the economical and environmental impact of taking
back heating systems. The baseline was defined using literature, interviews with
Viessmann’s employees, phone calls with installers and waste management companies,
as well as surveys. It then produced a large amount of solution ideas for take back
system design and selected three main concepts that were then modelled and
quantitatively evaluated. It was found that collecting heating system from installers
company’s site in bulk and treating them in bulk in a treatment centre (known as bulk
collection) was the most suitable option today. Alternatively, paying installers to recover
a few key components was found to be the less costly option but had regulatory and
feasibility issues. All assessed scenarios showed small environmental savings in all
impact categories. However none of the scenarios were found to have a guaranteed
economical profit at the end. The main issues being the lack of OEMs contact to confirm
and quantify their interest, in addition to the uncertainty connected to the logistics costs
assessment method. The assessment also showed that the economical and
environmental benefits were product dependent, thus creating the need of a tool for an
easy and holistic assessment of each product before taking them back. In order to guide
Viessmann in developing their take back system, a co­creative poster was proposed,
which is a unique comprehensive semi­quantitative method for assessing the feasibility
of taking back products. It enables the company to continue the journey and collect the
data that were missing in this analysis.

Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems

Table A.1 shows the market sales and growth rate derived from
German Heatpump Association, 2023 research. As data tables were not available, one
was created that would follow the main points of what was said in section 3.4.5. The
data is not 100% accurate but should represent the tendencies of the market prediction
relatively well.

Table A.1: MFA market predictions
Table A.2 shows the potential recycled content using the old products scrap to supply
secondary steel. It also indicates the transfer coefficients (TC) used in the MFA 2030
and 2050 scenarios. Finally it gives the numbers for the 2030 assumed amount of heat
pumps installed derived from the European database and the share of the German
population (IEA, 2022c and PopulationPyramid, 2023).

A.3
DES appendix
For the raw data used in figures and subsequent data analysis. See the separate
appendix of excel sheets and python scripts.
A.3.1
Agent structure
Anylogic uses Java, and agents moving through the system are Java classes, and
therefore use Java data structures. For the uninitiated, broadly speaking, a
linkedhashmap is functionally analogous to a JSON object or key value pairs in general.
Heating component inherit classes and functions from heating system, as far as
possible.
Agent: Heating System:
In the table below, the full array of functions and their
function is described.
Heating system
Collection
Description
Datatype
Materials
Stores the cumulative materials of the parts in the heating system
LinkedHashMap<String,
Double>
Parts of parts
A map of the parts
LinkedHashMap<String,
Linked­
HashMap<String,
Double>>
Parts
Tracks which parts are present
LinkedHashMap<String,
Double>
Parts of Interest
Tracks which parts are of interset. String being the key, double being if it is of interest, if so how many.
LinkedHashMap<String,
Double>
Parts of interest, installer
Same as parts of interest but for installers as less parts are feasible to remove.
LinkedHashMap<String,
Double>
Transport distance
Tracks amount of transport and by what actor
LinkedHashMap<String,
Double>
Time spent
Tracks amount of time spent and by what actor
LinkedHashMap<String,
Double>
Parts_partName, e..g, parts_blower or parts_heatExchanger
For each component in the heating system (varies slightly)
LinkedHashMap
Functions
heatingSystem_function(HsType)
Initializes all collections above. Sets database values for parts, materials, who they are of interest for
Constructor
Add_part(name, amount, COI, COI_installer, part_partname)
Take a part_partName and adds it to parts lists
Puts part in collections if relevant: Parts, Parts_of_parts, COI, COI_installer
Helper function.
Remove_component
Removes a given component
Setter function.
calc_mass_total
Verifies that mass balance is preserved. Is used when components are removed
Helper function.
debulk_container
Updates transport and time when the agent is removed from a container.
Setter function.
Agent: Heating Component
The agent heating component shares all functions and
collections with the heating system with the exception of: parts_of_interest,
parts_of_interest_installer.
For more clearly and flexibility in the analysis it implements a few other functions. To
allows analysis of specifically components it moves all time spent by actors into a
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
189
==PAGE:200==
pre_time collection. This allows us to more easily compare the time spent specifically on
components afterwards.
Agent: Container
Container inherits the transport functions and timing functions and
collections. When emptied the values here are transferred to the contents, using the
agents setter functions.
A.3.2
Parameters
A lot of parameters are used to control the simulation. As a lot are shared they are
presented first.
Most have been varied, as illustrated, and many more have been explored in the model
building. Only select data will be presented, as the full parameter variation data­sets are
10­40 mb per model.

A.5
Poster design process
This section shows the many different iterations of designing the poster. A short
comment is attached to each iteration, briefly covering some of the insights. Most
iterations were tested internally, on a product, to evaluate the usability of the poster.
The poster iterations are present chronologically when possible, some sections and
iterations were however made in parallel.
A.5.1
Early exploration
Life cycle inspired
There were two different approaches to explain the relations
between the take back system, new product development and reuse of components.
One was based on the traditional product life cycle methods.
Figure A.12: Poster iteration 0
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
201
==PAGE:212==
Figure A.13: Poster iteration 1
Figure A.14: Poster iteration 2
After the initial exploration the product life cycle approach was developed further. Here
inspiration was drawn from existing take back system / reverse logistics frameworks to
add more of the qualitative elements ­ i.e., going beyond the component and material
focus.
Circular life cycle ­ closing the loop
The other main approach was inspired by
illustrating the ideally circular nature of the product life cycles. The figures below show
different scenarios. The closer towards the circle a line (scenario) is, the more material
is conserve. This illustrates the different levels of circularity which can be lost in the
more linear diagrams.
202
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:213==
Figure A.15: Poster iteration a
Figure A.16: Poster iteration b
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
203
==PAGE:214==
Figure A.17: Poster iteration c
A.5.2
Selecting the product life cycle approach
One key element we identified that was missing was how to actually define a component
of interest. This had remained implicit in the process so far, as we from the project
beginning had an idea of what OEMs would be interested in what. But of course this
was not the case when starting a greenfield evaluation of a new product category, or in a
different company/brand.
Figure A.18: Poster iteration 3
It was found that the table had a lot of cells that would rarely be used, and that a lot of it
became quite abstract. It was therefore chosen to develop this into question sections
where participants could simply post post it notes. This offers more guidance and less
’exhaustion by table’.
204
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
==PAGE:215==
Figure A.19: Poster iteration 4
In practice we found it very hard to do meaningful things with the disassembly graph. It
simply required too much information about the product, that couldn’t be inferred without
having the product in front of you, having a CAD model or similar. Also estimating the
times and tool usage was very difficult. It was also speculated that the disassembly tree,
quickly would become unwieldy and hard for laymen/the uninitiated to understand. The
disassembly tree was replaced with best estimates and a few static times, that were
unavoidable.

This poster was chosen as being a possible endpoint, capturing the most essential part.
But internally in the team it was unsatisfactory. The lack of disassembly times, and how
to optimise the disassembly was found to be a significant missing step.

Figure A.22: Poster iteration 7
A new method was developed for estimating semi­destructive disassembly times. This
was based on highlighting the scrap fractions, key components, outlining what could be
destroyed etc.. This could then allow workshop participants to investigate how to
dismantle the product on paper. The images and highlighting of important components
etc., would support them in knowing where to use an anglegrinder ­ and when to use a
screwdriver.
The process was tested, but it was missing the iterative and exhaustive nature of
removing all components and splitting the components apart. The process was also
quite internal, and a little hard to grasp for participants without someone explaining it.
This led to the iteration below, shown in two parts of legibility purposes
Figure A.23: Poster iteration 8 A, 1/2

A.5.4
The final poster design
Based on the feedback of evaluating the 8th iteration of the poster, it was finalised with
the feedback. This poster still needs to be properly tested through a workshop, and at
the point of creation is missing some of its’ supportive elements, like a proper schedule
(playbook) for how to handle it. Most of this is addressed in the presentation of the
poster, in the chapter on solutions.
For a higher res version or access to the Miro board, contact the authors.

A.5.5
Video study documentation of independent scrappers practices
This is not exhaustive by any means. The main goal of this appendix is to ducment and
reference some videos which can serve as inspiration for how to scrap heating systems
and water tanks.
Designing take­back systems to improve the circularity of existing and future heating systems
211
==PAGE:222==
Small video study of publicly available scrapping
guides for water heaters and gas boilers.
The findings are presented first and below are the notes from watching the videos
and images of the interesting processes.
Findings
A lot of time is lost in these videos as they are fully dismantling a single system,
and not multiple. This results in a lot of changeover of tools, working positions
etc..
Most components needs to be broken apart as the goal is selling pure scrap
metals
Every component we need to get back reduces operating time but may
increase the need to be careful
Using an angle grinder to cut piping and bolts is time saving
Power drills save a lot of time
Long screw drivers (30-60cm) allows the operator to reach all bolts and screws
with ease
A proper workbench is important
Watertanks and combi heaters are VERY different and needs entirely different
process flows. We need to separate and bulk different types of heating
systems
Testing what components are made of takes up some time, and as scrappers
are sorting in material fractions they need to do this immediately. Grouping by
component type, could allow us to do this faster later.
One scrapper posted time and earnings, which was about an hourly wage of
20usd (in the US). Some earns around 35 pounds per combi boiler.
Please note that we can speed this up and if we can sell intact components to
suppliers then we can earn more.
conclusion
We can copy a lot of the processes and with bulking up the processes and
benefiting from proper workstations, powertools and targeted cutting we can
==PAGE:223==
reduce the disassembly/breaking down of a heating system A LOT.
With training in it, from this video study I'd guesstimate to around 20-30
minutes per heating system including breaking down components
Especially considering that there are components we do not need to break
down ****
Checking material composition can also be moved to the end in a sorting
state.
Videos and images
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z4Mf-b5wMnA 
cutting away solder makes it able to be sold as clean copper

leverage appears to be faster.
